# Interface UX & layout — FULL research corpus (recovered from transcripts)

> **What this is.** The complete per-agent deep-research behind the `interface-ux-and-layout` skill,
> extracted **verbatim** from the `StructuredOutput` of every research agent across the three UX research
> workflows (UX-evidence, aesthetic-preference, practical-interface). This is the RAW corpus the
> distilled findings docs (`ux-evidence-findings.md`, `aesthetic-preference-findings.md`,
> `ux-practical-findings.md`) were synthesised from. **De-noised by construction** — only StructuredOutput
> research content; no prompts, tool logs, rate-limit/retry/token text. Extracted deterministically.

> **Scale:** 83 source extractions · 225 adversarial verifications · 23 synthesis records, from ~331 agents over 3 workflows.

---

## Part A — Source extractions (83)

### UX evidence — designing interfaces (fluency, first impressions, scanning, a11y)

#### Source `a013c537` — quality: **primary** · published: ?
- **[central]** Material Design 3 instructs designers to use spacing and proximity to group related elements and to separate unrelated ones — a direct application of the Gestalt proximity principle to UI layout.
  > "Use spacing to visually tie elements together and establish boundaries between unrelated items."
- **[central]** M3 treats generous negative space / whitespace as a mechanism for establishing visual hierarchy and emphasis, not merely as empty filler — most important content gets prominence via generous spacing.
  > "Framing important actions or content with generous spacing creates emphasis."
- **[supporting]** M3 distinguishes explicit grouping (outlines, dividers, shadows / common region) from implicit grouping (proximity and open space), recommending proximity/whitespace as an alternative to drawn boundaries — restraint over decoration.
  > "Implicit grouping uses close proximity and open space (rather than lines and shadows) to group related items."
- **[supporting]** M3 specifies that spacing is responsive: the spacing scale adapts to breakpoints, and desktop layouts should use more generous spacing than mobile, rather than a single fixed spacing value across viewports.
  > "Desktop layouts can use more generous spacing than mobile layouts"
- **[tangential]** M3 frames layout density as expressive/tonal (a 'feel') rather than as an empirical comprehension or performance gain — explicitly presenting spacing choices as taste/personality, which supports separating fashion from function.
  > "A denser layout can feel more serious and focused, while a more spacious layout can feel calm and open"

#### Source `a15b8598` — quality: **primary** · published: ?
- **[central]** Apple specifies 44x44 pt as the DEFAULT control size for iOS/iPadOS and watchOS — not the minimum; the documented minimum control size is 28x28 pt (iOS/iPadOS, watchOS, visionOS), 20x20 pt (macOS), and 56x56 pt (tvOS). This nuances the widely-cited '44pt minimum touch target' claim.
  > "Strive to meet the recommended minimum control size for each platform... Platform Default control size Minimum control size iOS, iPadOS 44x44 pt 28x28 pt macOS 28x28 pt 20x20 pt tvOS 66x66 pt 56x56 pt visionOS 60x60 pt 28x28 pt watchOS 44x44 pt 28x28 pt"
- **[central]** Apple's contrast guidance is drawn directly from WCAG Level AA: minimum contrast ratio of 4.5:1 for text up to 17 pt, and 3:1 for text at 18 pt or for bold text. This agrees with WCAG and with the data-viz skill's contrast findings.
  > "uses the following values from WCAG Level AA as guidance in determining whether your app's colors have an acceptable contrast. Text size Text weight Minimum contrast ratio Up to 17 pts All 4.5:1 18 pts All 3:1 All Bold 3:1"
- **[central]** Color must never be the sole carrier of information; redundant non-color encoding (shapes/icons) is required because color-blind users have difficulty with red-green and blue-orange pairings. This reinforces the data-viz direct-labeling / redundant-encoding principle.
  > "Convey information with more than color alone. Some people have trouble differentiating between certain colors and shades. For example, people who are color blind may have particular difficulty with pairings such as red-green and blue-orange. Offer visual indicators, like distinct shapes or icons, in addition to color"
- **[supporting]** Spacing between controls matters as much as control size: Apple recommends roughly 12 points of padding around elements with a bezel and about 24 points around the visible edges of elements without a bezel, to reduce mis-taps.
  > "Consider spacing between controls as important as size... In general, it works well to add about 12 points of padding around elements that include a bezel. For elements without a bezel, about 24 points of padding works well around the element's visible edges."
- **[supporting]** Interfaces should let users enlarge text by at least 200 percent (140 percent on watchOS), and Apple publishes per-platform default and minimum type sizes (e.g., iOS/iPadOS default 17 pt, minimum 11 pt) to promote legibility.
  > "Ideally, give people the option to enlarge text by at least 200 percent (or 140 percent in watchOS apps)... Platform Default size Minimum size iOS, iPadOS 17 pt 11 pt macOS 13 pt 10 pt tvOS 29 pt 23 pt visionOS 17 pt 12 pt watchOS 16 pt 12 pt"

#### Source `a2e59007` — quality: **primary** · published: 2026-06-01 (Understanding document last updated)
- **[central]** WCAG 2.1 SC 1.4.3 (Level AA) requires a contrast ratio of at least 4.5:1 for normal text and at least 3:1 for large-scale text (defined as 18pt, or 14pt bold).
  > "The visual presentation of text and images of text has a contrast ratio of at least 4.5:1 ... Large-scale text and images of large-scale text have a contrast ratio of at least 3:1 ... with at least 18 point or 14 point bold"
- **[central]** WCAG contrast is computed from luminance, not hue: color/hue is explicitly NOT a key factor in the contrast calculation, so meeting the requirement is independent of color perception.
  > "contrast is calculated in such a way that color (hue) is not a key factor ... Requiring good luminance contrast accommodates this by requiring contrast that is independent of color perception"
- **[supporting]** The 4.5:1 threshold was chosen to compensate for the contrast-sensitivity loss of users with roughly 20/40 vision (a 1.5x factor over a baseline 3:1), and the AAA 7:1 threshold (SC 1.4.6) compensates for roughly 20/80 vision.
  > "The contrast ratio of 4.5:1 was chosen for level AA because it compensated for the loss in contrast sensitivity usually experienced by users with vision loss equivalent to approximately 20/40 vision ... The contrast ratio of 7:1 was chosen for level AAA because it compensated for the loss in contrast sensitivity usually experienced by users with vision loss equivalent to approximately 20/80 vision"
- **[tangential]** The contrast requirement has defined exemptions: text in logos or brand names, and text in inactive/disabled UI components, pure decoration, or text not visible to anyone, have no contrast requirement.
  > "Text that is part of a logo or brand name has no contrast requirement ... Text or images of text that are part of an inactive user interface component, that are pure decoration, that are not visible to anyone…have no contrast requirement"

#### Source `a2ee568c` — quality: **primary** · published: 2019-08-25
- **[central]** The F-pattern is one of four scanning patterns and reflects that, in left-to-right languages, users read text on the left and near the top of a page more than text on the right or near the bottom.
  > "text on the left and towards the top of the page is read more than text on the right or towards the bottom"
- **[central]** The layer-cake pattern, in which users fixate mostly on headings and subheadings while skipping body text, is the most effective way for users to scan pages (short of reading every word).
  > "by far the most effective way in which users can scan pages"
- **[supporting]** Formatting choices shape scanning behavior: the spotted pattern becomes more effective than the F-pattern when designers name links well, make important words visually distinct from body text, and use bulleted lists.
  > "slightly more operative than the F-pattern if web designers did a good job naming links, making important words look different from the rest of the body text, and creating bulleted lists"
- **[supporting]** Designers should support scanning by chunking content into sections and bulleted lists, using meaningful subheadings, and applying special visual styling to keywords.
  > "Support [users] by chunking your content into sections and bulleted lists, by using meaningful subheadings, and by special visual styling for keywords."

#### Source `a45c0446` — quality: **primary** · published: 2019
- **[central]** In a controlled experiment that manipulated aesthetics and usability as separate (independent) variables, aesthetics had NO significant effect on participants' judgments of usability; the significant effects came from manipulated usability and from occasion (repeated use), not aesthetics. This directly qualifies/contradicts the strong form of the aesthetic-usability effect (Kurosu & Kashimura 1995; Tractinsky 1997).
  > "Results failed to show the hypothesized effect of aesthetics on participants' judgments of usability, and analyses showed a significant effect of occasion and manipulated usability, rather than aesthetics, on participants' judgments of usability."
- **[central]** When usability and aesthetics are independently manipulated, users render independent judgments of the two, and aesthetic differences produced no significant difference on the System Usability Scale (SUS) — meaning beauty did not inflate measured perceived usability in this study.
  > "Findings support the idea that users make independent judgments of usability and aesthetics, as differences in aesthetics had no significant effect on usability as measured by the System Usability Scale (SUS)."
- **[supporting]** The aesthetic-usability literature is methodologically weak on causality: the causal relationship and its direction are not firmly established because few prior experiments manipulated aesthetics and usability as separate variables. This is grounds to treat the aesthetic-usability effect as a CONVENTION rather than a settled law.
  > "The causal relationship between aesthetics and usability and the direction of that relation have not been firmly established because of a shortage of experiments that have manipulated aesthetics and usability as separate variables."
- **[supporting]** The hypothesis that aesthetics' influence on usability judgments diminishes with continued use and experience received only very limited support, so claims that the aesthetic halo fades predictably over time are weakly evidenced.
  > "Analysis of whether users' judgments of usability and aesthetics, as well as any association between the two, might change with their continued experience with a system, provided very limited support for the hypotheses."

#### Source `a4c9021d` — quality: **blog** · published: 2026-03-07
- **[central]** Miller's 7±2 rule does not apply to navigation menus, because on-screen menus keep their options visible — making menu use a recognition task, not a recall (working-memory) task.
  > "On a screen, menus content and options stay visible, so users do not need to keep them in working memory. This is a recognition task, not a recall task."
- **[central]** Miller himself did not treat the number seven as a hard cognitive limit; he called it a coincidence and used it as an allegorical storytelling device.
  > "He used that number as an allegory, a storytelling device to nicely connect parts of his work."
- **[supporting]** Later research (Cowan 2001) revises short-term memory capacity downward to roughly four items, not seven, undermining the '7±2' figure as a design constant.
  > "later research suggests that short-term memory capacity is closer to four items. Not seven."
- **[supporting]** Miller's quoted self-skepticism casts the 'magical number seven' as an unreliable basis for rules.
  > "But I suspect that it is only a pernicious, Pythagorean coincidence."
- **[central]** The evidence-based takeaway for navigation is chunking — grouping related content into small units to aid scanning — rather than capping menu items at any magic number.
  > "We can group related content, and break complex information or data into small units, to make sites and apps easier to scan."

#### Source `a59d8b04` — quality: **unreliable** · published: 1995

#### Source `a5abb798` — quality: **primary** · published: 2024-09 (arXiv 2409.10841; IEEE VIS '24 short paper)
- **[central]** There is no clear dark-vs-light winner for visualization performance: each polarity benefited comparable proportions of participants, and the polarity that produced better performance frequently differed from the one the participant preferred. This directly corroborates the data-viz finding that dark mode has no clear performance winner.
  > "each polarity benefiting comparable proportions of participants. However, the contrast polarity that led to better performance did not always match their preferred polarity."
- **[central]** Stated preference does not predict performance: participants often performed better in their non-preferred polarity, so 'what users like' is not 'what users are best at' (preference != performance).
  > "what participants prefer may not actually be what they are best-suited to use, perhaps due to general familiarity or other outside factors."
- **[supporting]** Contrast polarity has a large effect on task speed (response time) even though it does not produce a clear accuracy winner, with an average percent difference of about 36 percent, comparable in magnitude to choosing a different visualization type.
  > "the choice of contrast polarity can have an impact on time similar to that of the choice of visualization type, resulting in an average percent difference of around 36%."
- **[supporting]** The strength of contrast polarity's effect on visual-analysis performance did not noticeably change with age (younger adults vs. participants aged 60+), across 134 participants and three chart types (bar, line, scatter).
  > "the effects of contrast polarity on visual analysis performance do not noticeably change with age."
- **[supporting]** Because neither polarity wins universally and preference diverges from performance, the authors recommend offering both light and dark (positive and negative contrast) palettes as a user-personalization feature rather than mandating one default.
  > "we recommend that practitioners offer both positive and negative contrast color palettes of visualizations when possible, perhaps as a universally-helpful personalization feature."

#### Source `a6a0f2af` — quality: **primary** · published: 2015-09-27
- **[central]** Flat design's removal of clickability signifiers reduces user efficiency by making it harder to tell what is clickable versus not — i.e., stripping depth/shadow cues imposes a measurable interaction cost.
  > "long-term exposure to these flat yet clickable elements has been slowly reducing user efficiency by complicating their understanding of what's clickable and what isn't."
- **[central]** Flat 2.0 ("semi-flat"/"almost flat") deliberately reintroduces subtle shadows, highlights, and layers to create some depth while keeping a simple aesthetic; Google Material Design is the exemplar of this balanced approach.
  > "Flat 2.0, also called "semi flat" or "almost flat," uses "subtle shadows, highlights, and layers to create some depth" while maintaining visual simplicity. Google's Material Design exemplifies this balanced approach."
- **[supporting]** Users identify clickable elements via three cue types, with traditional external signifiers (blue underlined text, raised buttons) being the strongest; even experienced users still need some clickability clues rather than none.
  > "But just because users are better at detecting linked elements doesn't mean they don't need any clues at all."
- **[supporting]** Pure flat design trades usability for fashion — it prioritizes trendy aesthetics over users' actual needs (a fashion-versus-function distinction).
  > "flat design "tends to sacrifice users' needs for the sake of trendy aesthetics.""

#### Source `a6a1c128` — quality: **primary** · published: 2006-04-16
- **[central]** Eye-tracking of 232 users across thousands of web pages found a dominant F-shaped reading pattern composed of three movements: a horizontal top sweep, a shorter second horizontal sweep further down, and a vertical scan down the left edge.
  > "Users first read in a horizontal movement, usually across the upper part of the content area. ... move down the page a bit and then read across in a second horizontal movement that typically covers a shorter area. ... Finally, users scan the content's left side in a vertical movement."
- **[central]** Users do not read web text word-by-word; they scan, so the most important information should appear in the first two paragraphs.
  > "Users won't read your text thoroughly in a word-by-word manner."
- **[supporting]** Because of the left-side vertical scan, headings, paragraphs, and bullets should begin with information-carrying words to be picked up during scanning (front-loading).
  > "Start subheads, paragraphs, and bullet points with information-carrying words"
- **[supporting]** The F-pattern is a rough general shape, not a precise law; it varies (E-shape, inverted-L) with layout and task, though the main behavior was consistent across sites and tasks.
  > "users' main reading behavior was fairly consistent across many different sites and tasks"

#### Source `a6ac507e` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2024-02-03 (article by Kate Moran; cited research is Kurosu & Kashimura 1995. The found-via reference dates it 2017, indicating an original 2017 piece later updated)
- **[central]** The aesthetic-usability effect is the documented tendency for users to perceive visually attractive products/interfaces as more usable, independent of their actual usability.
  > "The aesthetic-usability effect refers to users' tendency to perceive attractive products as more usable."
- **[central]** In Kurosu & Kashimura's 1995 ATM-interface study, users' aesthetic ratings correlated more strongly with PERCEIVED ease of use than with ACTUAL ease of use — i.e., appearance drives usability judgments more than real usability does.
  > "The correlation between the participants' ratings of aesthetic appeal and perceived ease of use was stronger than the correlation between their ratings of aesthetic appeal and actual ease of use."
- **[supporting]** Aesthetic appeal makes users more forgiving of minor usability problems, effectively masking small defects.
  > "Users are more tolerant of minor usability issues when they find an interface visually appealing."
- **[supporting]** The forgiveness effect is bounded: attractiveness offsets only minor usability problems, not large ones, so aesthetics cannot substitute for functional usability.
  > "A pretty design can make users forgiving of minor usability problems, but not of large ones."

#### Source `a6c15071` — quality: **primary** · published: 2018 (Quarterly Journal of Experimental Psychology, Vol. 71(6), 1281–1299; received 21 January 2017, accepted 3 April 2017)
- **[central]** Hick's law states choice reaction time is a logarithmic function of the number of equally likely alternatives: RT = a + b·log2(n), where a is the intercept and b the slope; it can also be written RT = a + bH where H is the average information/uncertainty in bits.
  > "The more widely accepted version of Hick's law for n equally likely alternatives is RT = a + b log 2n, in which a is the intercept and b the slope of the function relating RT to information. Log 2 has come to be used because of its link to binary digits (bits) of information... the equation for Hick's law is sometimes expressed in terms of the average information or uncertainty (H) in a series of trials, in the form RT = a + bH."
- **[central]** Hick's law is not invariant: the slope depends on stimulus-response compatibility and can be driven to zero. In highly compatible tasks (e.g., responding to the finger that vibrates, saccading toward a stimulus) set size has no effect, so the slope of the RT-vs-information function ranges from zero up to several hundred milliseconds. This contradicts the popular UI framing that adding choices reliably and uniformly slows users.
  > "the slope of the function can vary between zero and several hundreds of milliseconds."
- **[supporting]** Practice reduces the slope of the Hick's law function, and with sufficient practice reaction time can become independent of set size — so the 'more options = slower' penalty shrinks or vanishes for experienced/expert users, a key caveat for designing interfaces used repeatedly.
  > "Teichner and Krebs (1974) analyzed results from many experiments and concluded that with sufficient practice, RT might become independent of set size."
- **[central]** The earliest empirical HCI validation (Landauer & Nachbar 1985, touch-menu selection) found selection time fit a logarithmic (Hick/Fitts) function, and because of that logarithmic relation the number of alternatives per screen does not affect total selection time — only the number of screens does — so broad, shallow menu trees beat narrow, deep ones.
  > "Landauer and Nachbar concluded, "Broader, shallower menu trees yield faster search than narrower, deeper ones" (p. 76)."
- **[supporting]** Hick's law (logarithmic) applies to selection from a known/ordered set, not to visual search of an unordered list: serial visual search yields a LINEAR (not logarithmic) function of item count. This is a critical boundary for applying Hick's law to UI — scanning an unfamiliar menu of N items is closer to linear-time search than to log-time choice.
  > "a linear function, which would be more in agreement with visual search—for which "there is strong evidence that the visual search mechanism basically proceeds in a serial mode" (Schwarz & Miller, 2016, p. 1656), yielding a linear function."

#### Source `a7c142ef` — quality: **blog** · published: ?
- **[central]** The 7±2 figure from Miller's research describes the limit of human short-term/working memory, not the number of choices or visible options a user interface should present; applying it to menu/option counts is a misapplication.
  > "the law "only applies to humans' working memory, not to information that is readily available to be read""
- **[central]** Limiting on-screen menu choices to 7±2 is a false constraint because information visually present on a page does not have to be memorized, so users can manage many more options than seven.
  > "Since information is visually present on webpages, users don't need to memorize it and can manage many more options."
- **[supporting]** George Miller, the author of the original 1956 paper, stated his finding about the limit on discrimination of unidimensional stimuli has nothing to do with a person's capacity to comprehend displayed/printed text.
  > "The point was that 7 was a limit for the discrimination of unidimensional stimuli...neither of which has anything to do with a person's capacity to comprehend printed text"
- **[supporting]** Edward Tufte argued Miller's paper does not state or imply any rule for how much information to show in a presentation or interface.
  > "Miller's paper "neither states nor implies rules for the amount of information to be shown in a presentation""
- **[supporting]** Broad, shallow navigation menus (including link-rich homepages such as Amazon's 90+ category links) are more usable and less error-prone than deeper hierarchies with fewer top-level items.
  > "Broad top-level menus are "most efficient and least error prone""

#### Source `a85bcbf0` — quality: **primary** · published: 1997-03-27
- **[central]** Tractinsky (1997) replicated Kurosu & Kashimura's finding of a strong relationship between an interface's perceived aesthetics and its perceived usability, running three controlled experiments in a different culture (Israel) and finding very high correlations between perceived aesthetics and a priori perceived ease of use. This is a foundational primary source for the aesthetic-usability effect.
  > "Three experiments were conducted to validate and replicate, in a different cultural setting, the results of a study by Kurosu and Kashimura concerning the relationships between users' perceptions of interface aesthetics and usability."
- **[supporting]** The usability measured was 'apparent' / perceived usability judged a priori from the interface's appearance, not measured task performance — so the documented correlation is between perceived beauty and perceived ease of use, which directly supports the research question's 'preference does not equal performance' caution (the effect is on perception/judgment, not demonstrated task efficiency).
  > "very high correlations"
- **[supporting]** The aesthetics-perceived-usability correlation held cross-culturally (Israel as well as Japan), and although the magnitude differed between cultures, the differences ran contrary to the researchers' prior hypothesis — indicating the effect is robust across cultures but its cultural moderation is not well understood.
  > "were not in the hypothesized direction"
- **[tangential]** This is the canonical, heavily-cited CHI 1997 primary study (DOI 10.1145/258549.258626, 602 citations as recorded) establishing the aesthetic-usability effect named in the research question, confirming Tractinsky 1997 as a legitimate primary citation rather than secondary reporting.
  > "Aesthetics and apparent usability: empirically assessing cultural and methodological issues"

#### Source `a97b23ee` — quality: **primary** · published: 2017-10-15
- **[central]** In NN/g's eyetracking study, weak or absent visual signifiers (typical of flat UI) attracted measurably less attention than strong visual signifiers, causing users to struggle more to locate clickable elements.
  > "weak or absent visual signifiers...attract less attention than strong visual signifiers"
- **[central]** The flat-design clickability effect was statistically significant at the conventional academic threshold (p<0.05), i.e. not attributable to chance — making it an empirically supported [STRONG] finding rather than mere convention.
  > "p<0.05 level, which is the level used for published academic research"
- **[supporting]** The study was an adequately powered controlled comparison: 70 users, 9 paired website designs, across 6 domains (ecommerce, hotel, travel, technology, finance, nonprofit), using fine-grain fixation count/duration metrics rather than only task success.
  > "fine-grain metrics (number and duration of fixations on one"
- **[central]** The real culprit is weak signifiers, not flatness per se: flat design done with adequate clickability cues is fine, so the rule is about preserving signifiers/affordances, not banning flat aesthetics.
  > "weak or absent signifiers are"
- **[tangential]** NN/g openly acknowledges a scope limitation: the study measured small findability tasks rather than full realistic tasks, so the magnitude of real-world impact is bounded — an honest separation of measured effect from extrapolation.
  > "small findability tasks, rather than full, realistic, natural tasks"

#### Source `a9e9a5bf` — quality: **primary** · published: 1993-01-01
- **[central]** Roughly 0.1 second is the upper limit for a user to feel that a UI is reacting instantaneously, meaning no special feedback (beyond showing the result) is needed below this threshold.
  > "0.1 second is about the limit for having the user feel that the system is reacting instantaneously"
- **[central]** About 1.0 second is the limit for keeping a user's flow of thought uninterrupted; the user notices the delay but does not lose their sense of operating directly on the data.
  > "1.0 second is about the limit for the user's flow of thought to stay uninterrupted, even though the user will notice the delay"
- **[central]** About 10 seconds is the limit for keeping a user's attention focused on the interaction; beyond it users want to switch to other tasks while waiting.
  > "10 seconds is about the limit for keeping the user's attention focused on the dialogue"
- **[supporting]** Percent-done progress indicators should be used for operations that take longer than about 10 seconds, to reassure users the system is working and indicate how long they must wait.
  > "percent-done progress indicators should be used for operations taking more than about 10 seconds"
- **[supporting]** These response-time thresholds are not new web-era opinion: they trace to primary human-factors research (Miller 1968; Card et al. 1991) and have been stable for about thirty years.
  > "The basic advice regarding response times has been about the same for thirty years [Miller 1968; Card et al. 1991]"

#### Source `ab34a268` — quality: **blog** · published: 2022-06-21 (updated 2026-05-29)
- **[central]** Miller's Law (7±2) is about working-memory capacity and does not justify capping navigation menus at 5-7 items, because navigation items are scanned visually rather than held in working memory.
  > "Visual scanning and working memory retention are entirely different cognitive processes."
- **[central]** On smartphone screens, Fitts's Law distance term is negligible and target size is the dominant factor in tap accuracy, contrary to applying distance equally on mobile and desktop.
  > "On a typical smartphone screen, the distances involved simply aren't large enough for distance to meaningfully affect tap accuracy — the limiting factor is almost always target size."
- **[supporting]** Jakob's Law (users prefer interfaces that work like others) does not prohibit novelty; research indicates novelty improves mood and memory retention.
  > "The research is fairly clear that novelty improves both mood and memory retention."
- **[supporting]** The aesthetic-usability effect means attractive design earns goodwill but does not substitute for actual usability; functional failures in beautiful interfaces intensify disappointment.
  > "Aesthetic quality buys you goodwill; it doesn't replace usability."
- **[tangential]** Hiding shipping costs until checkout (misapplied goal-gradient effect) is a leading, frequently-cited driver of cart abandonment, which sits around 74%.
  > "Cart abandonment sits at around 74%, and last-minute revelations about shipping costs are one of the most consistently cited reasons."

#### Source `ac1c51c6` — quality: **secondary** · published: 1982-11 (original IBM Systems Journal paper, reproduced on the blog; blog post date not stated)
- **[central]** User productivity (transactions/hour) rises sharply and non-linearly as system response time drops below ~1 second: roughly 180 transactions/hour at 3s, 252 at 1.0s, and 371 at 0.3s — identifying sub-second response as a key inflection point.
  > "At 3 seconds: ~180 transactions/hour; At 1.0 second: ~252 transactions/hour; At 0.3 seconds: ~371 transactions/hour"
- **[central]** Faster system response saves MORE user time than the raw latency reduction itself, because faster systems change user behavior: a 2.7-second cut in system response time saved 10.3 seconds of the user's own time.
  > "a reduction of 2.7 seconds in system response saves 10.3 seconds of the user's time"
- **[central]** The common assumption that users passively 'think' during system waits is wrong; each additional second of system response delay adds a comparable delay to the user's time on the next command, because users hold a sequence of planned actions in short-term memory that slow responses disrupt.
  > "each second of system response degradation leads to a similar degradation added to the user's time for the following [command]... people seem to have a sequence of actions in mind, contained in a short-term mental memory buffer"
- **[supporting]** Faster response time produced measurable output gains in real programming work: Portsmouth programmers produced 58% more output (14.4 vs. 9.1 function points/month) at 0.84s response time versus 2.3s.
  > "Portsmouth programmers: 58% more output (14.4 vs. 9.1 function points/month) at 0.84s vs. 2.3s response"
- **[supporting]** On a card-wiring task, reducing response time from the 0.6–6 second range to 0.25 seconds cut task time by 20–35%.
  > "Card wiring task: 20–35% time reduction moving from 0.6–6 seconds to 0.25 seconds"

#### Source `ac421448` — quality: **primary** · published: 2019-08-04
- **[central]** When pages contain clear, visually distinct headings and subheadings, users scan in a 'layer-cake' pattern: eye fixations land mostly on headings/subheadings with occasional fixations on body text in between, producing horizontal stripes in the gaze heatmap.
  > "The layer-cake pattern... consists of fixations made mostly on the page's headings and subheadings, with deliberate occasional fixations on the (body) text in between... a set of horizontal stripes and blank spaces between them, resembling a layer cake"
- **[central]** The layer-cake scanning pattern is more efficient than the F-pattern: it reliably leads users to the information they seek (if present), whereas the F-pattern causes users to inadvertently miss meaningful information without realizing it.
  > "aside from reading every (or almost every) word, the layer-cake pattern is by far the most effective way to scan pages: most of the time, it ensures that users will find the information they are looking for (if it is present on the page)"
- **[supporting]** Headings/subheadings only enable effective scanning when they are visually distinct (via color, size, typeface, bolding, or shadowing) and descriptively cover all and only the topics in their sections.
  > "The pattern depends on subheadings being: Visually distinct through color, size, typeface, bolding, or shadowing; Descriptive of all and only the topics in their associated sections; Clear and understandable for quick scanning"
- **[supporting]** The F-pattern represents low scanning efficiency while the layer-cake pattern represents high efficiency, because layer-cake lets people direct attention to relevant sections and skip irrelevant ones.
  > "F-Pattern exhibits 'low' efficiency where 'people inadvertently miss meaningful information and have no knowledge they did so.' Layer-Cake Pattern demonstrates 'high' efficiency because 'people save time by directing their attention to the relevant page sections and skipping the irrelevant ones.'"

#### Source `ac49fc0e` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2022-07-31
- **[central]** Fitts's law states that the time to acquire a target is a function of the distance to and the size of the target: movement time increases with greater distance and decreases with larger target size (T = a + b·log2(D/w)).
  > "The movement time to a target depends on the size of the target and the distance to the target."
- **[central]** Larger interactive targets are faster to click/tap/hover, so UI controls should be made big — this is the direct, testable design implication of Fitts's law.
  > "Make targets big. Fitts's law clearly says that people will be faster to click, tap, or hover on bigger targets."
- **[supporting]** A target combining an icon plus a text label has a larger effective area than an icon alone and is therefore faster to acquire under Fitts's law.
  > "Any target made up of both an icon and a label will be greater than just an icon and, therefore, according to Fitts's law, will be easier to acquire."
- **[supporting]** Screen edges and corners act as 'infinite' targets because the cursor stops at the boundary, so controls placed at edges are faster to acquire (the cursor need not decelerate).
  > "The screen edges act as natural walls for the cursor"
- **[supporting]** Placing interactive targets too close together raises error risk because users overshoot and mis-tap, so adequate spacing between targets is required.
  > "If you place targets too close to each other, there is a risk that people will accidentally overshoot."

#### Source `ac7de8c1` — quality: **secondary** · published: Original paper: 1956 (Psychological Review); Wikipedia article: continuously updated, no fixed date
- **[central]** Miller's 1956 paper is commonly interpreted as setting human short-term memory capacity at 7±2 objects, but this is an interpretation laid on the paper rather than its actual demonstrated finding.
  > "It is often interpreted to argue that the number of objects an average human can hold in short-term memory is 7 ± 2."
- **[central]** Miller himself regarded the '7' as rhetorical, not a real cognitive constant; the apparent match between absolute-judgment limits and memory span was a coincidence, so there is nothing magical about seven.
  > "Therefore, there is nothing 'magical' about the number seven, and Miller used the expression only rhetorically."
- **[supporting]** The robust, information-theoretic result in the paper is a channel capacity of about 2-3 bits for one-dimensional absolute judgment, not a memory limit of seven items.
  > "people's maximum performance on a one-dimensional absolute judgment can be characterized as an information channel capacity with approximately 2 to 3 bits of information"
- **[central]** Modern working-memory research puts the real capacity at roughly four chunks in young adults, lower than the popularized seven and lower still in children and older adults.
  > "Cowan has proposed that working memory has a capacity of about four chunks in young adults (and less in children and older adults)."
- **[supporting]** Memory span is not a fixed number even when counted in chunks, undermining any design rule that treats a single integer (seven, or any other) as a hard cognitive ceiling.
  > "Later research on short-term memory and working memory revealed that memory span is not a constant even when measured in a number of chunks."

#### Source `ad46cba3` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2020-02-02
- **[central]** In a controlled study of normal-vision adults (Piepenbrock et al., 2013), light mode outperformed dark mode on both visual-acuity and proofreading tasks across all measured dimensions.
  > "light mode won across all dimensions"
- **[central]** Objective performance and subjective preference diverge: even though light mode produced better measured performance, participants perceived no readability difference between modes — i.e., preference does not track performance.
  > "participants did not report any difference in their perception of text readability"
- **[central]** The light-mode performance advantage is explained by a luminance/pupil mechanism (more light contracts the pupil, reducing optical aberrations), not by screen hue.
  > "there is more overall light and so the pupil contracts more. As a result, there are fewer spherical aberrations, greater depth of field"
- **[supporting]** Dark mode is not universally inferior: people with cloudy ocular media (e.g., cataracts) read faster in dark mode (Legge et al., 1985), which is why offering a user-switchable dark mode is recommended.
  > "participants with cloudy ocular media had better reading rates with dark modes"
- **[supporting]** Contrast polarity (light vs dark mode) showed no significant difference in self-reported eyestrain, headache, or muscle strain — undercutting the popular claim that dark mode reduces eye strain.
  > "No significant difference between modes on eyestrain, headache, or muscle strain metrics"

#### Source `ade54d4e` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2022-05-10
- **[central]** The optimal line length ('measure') for body text is 50-60 characters per line including spaces, a convention originating from typographer Emil Ruder ('Typographie'), not from a controlled study.
  > "The optimal line length for body text is 50–60 characters per line, including spaces"
- **[central]** WCAG (Web Content Accessibility Guidelines) success criterion 1.4.8 sets a normative upper bound of 80 or fewer characters per line for body text.
  > "Lines of text should be 80 or fewer characters"
- **[supporting]** Up to 75 characters per line is considered an acceptable upper bound for readable line length.
  > "Up to 75 characters is acceptable"
- **[supporting]** Line length affects reading rhythm: lines that are too long make it hard to locate where a line starts and ends, while lines that are too short force the eye to travel back too often, breaking the reader's rhythm.
  > "Lines that are too short cause the eye to "travel back too often, breaking the reader's rhythm""
- **[supporting]** Overly long lines of text are perceived by users as intimidating, increasing the likelihood they avoid or abandon reading (observed in Baymard usability testing, not a controlled comprehension metric).
  > "Long lines of text are typically perceived by users as intimidating and overwhelming"

#### Source `aee377a6` — quality: **blog** · published: 2019-10-26
- **[central]** Miller's 'magical number seven' applies only to one-dimensional absolute judgment and immediate-memory-span experiments, not to interface design; treating '7±2' as a limit on menu items, dropdowns, or nav elements is a misapplication of the 1956 paper.
  > "The seven applies only to "uni-dimensional stimulus in absolute judgment" and immediate memory span, which "behave differently" ... Designers treat "7±2" as a magical limit for any UI element (dropdowns, menu items, options), when Miller never claimed this applicability."
- **[central]** Miller himself regarded the recurrence of seven as a coincidence rather than a fundamental cognitive law, undercutting its use as a normative design rule.
  > "The author quotes Miller directly: "I suspect that it is only a pernicious, Pythagorean coincidence.""
- **[supporting]** The working-memory capacity limit can be bypassed by chunking/recoding information into multidimensional stimuli and sequences, so the 'seven' is not a hard ceiling on how many items an interface can present.
  > "Channel capacity limits can be overcome by increasing stimulus dimensions and organizing information into sequences with proper recoding ... When multiple variables combine, the seven disappears entirely"
- **[tangential]** Chunking-based compression only reduces cognitive load when the recoding is automatic; otherwise it adds noise rather than capacity.
  > "Information compression only works when the recoding process is automatic; otherwise noise increases"

#### Source `aee44d20` — quality: **blog** · published: 2026-05-14
- **[supporting]** Stripe, Linear, and Vercel rely on high contrast (black on white / white on black, avoiding muddy mid-tones) to direct the user's eye and establish visual hierarchy.
  > "Black on white, white on black, nothing muddy in between. When you land on any of these sites, your eye knows exactly where to go."
- **[supporting]** These premium sites use more whitespace than feels necessary; the author's heuristic is to take spacing that feels like enough and double it.
  > "take spacing that feels like enough, then double it. That's probably where it should have been from the start"
- **[supporting]** A monochrome (black/white/gray) base with a single sparingly-used accent color is more effective than using many colors; one accent 'hits harder than five colors used everywhere.'
  > "Their palette is almost entirely black, white, and gray. Then one color does all the work."
- **[tangential]** Sharp, geometric, tight typography (rather than rounded, friendly fonts) is characteristic of the modern look of these design-system exemplars.
  > "No rounded, friendly, approachable fonts. Everything is tight, geometric, slightly cold."

#### Source `af564407` — quality: **blog** · published: ?
- **[central]** EyeQuant's own large-scale eye-tracking analysis found no evidence of an F-shaped scanning bias on any of the 99 websites tested — directly contradicting the popular F-pattern claim.
  > "we've yet to find any evidence that this bias towards an F-Shape exists at all... out of those 99 websites, we didn't find any individual heat maps that closely resembled an F."
- **[central]** During initial scanning, attention concentrates as a central 'blob' biased toward the middle and slightly left of the screen, rather than along the F's stems.
  > "Instead of an F-shape, the research revealed "a blob" pattern—"mostly towards the middle of the screen, and slightly to the left.""
- **[supporting]** The empirically best place for priority content is the center-slightly-left region where initial fixations cluster.
  > "Empirically, this appears to be the best place for you to put priority content."
- **[supporting]** The original F-pattern theory traces to a single 2006 Nielsen Norman Group eye-tracking study, which this source treats as overstated.
  > "In 2006, Nielsen Norman published some fascinating research on how people scan and read websites"
- **[tangential]** There is no universal layout rule because scanning is shaped by multiple competing factors; this study only measured the initial ~15-second scan phase.
  > "there is no 'golden rule' for layouts"

#### Source `af9f74f0` — quality: **primary** · published: 2017-11-12
- **[central]** Eye-tracking studies (NN/G, early 2000s and a 2017 replication) show users scan text-heavy pages in an F-shaped pattern: two horizontal passes near the top plus a vertical pass down the left edge. The 2017 heatmap aggregated 47 users.
  > "Users first read in a horizontal movement, usually across the upper part of the content area. This initial element forms the F's top bar. Next, users move down the page a bit and then read across in a second horizontal movement...This additional element forms the F's lower bar. Finally, users scan the content's left side in a vertical movement."
- **[central]** The F-pattern is a fallback scanning behavior that emerges when content lacks formatting cues, not a desirable reading mode; it causes users to miss important content.
  > "The F-pattern is the default pattern when there are no strong cues to attract the eyes towards meaningful information."
- **[central]** F-pattern scanning is harmful to comprehension because users skip large portions of text purely as a function of column flow, including phrases that may be the most important.
  > "When people scan in an F-shape, they miss big chunks of content based merely on how text flows in a column...The skipped phrases and words are often as important — or even more important."
- **[supporting]** Designers should counter the F-pattern with concrete formatting techniques — front-loading key points, prominent headings, front-loading information-bearing words, grouping, bolding, and cutting content — rather than treating the pattern as inevitable.
  > "Start headings and subheadings with the words carrying most information: if users see only the first 2 words, they should still get the gist"
- **[supporting]** The F-shaped scanning pattern persists on both desktop and mobile, and mirrors (flipped) in right-to-left languages such as Arabic.
  > "Our recent eyetracking research shows that the F-shaped scanning pattern is alive and well in today's world — both on desktop and on mobile."

### Aesthetic preference — why people intuitively like a UI

#### Source `a0aede19` — quality: **primary** · published: 2004
- **[central]** The paper's central thesis is that aesthetic pleasure is driven by processing fluency: the more easily a perceiver can process a stimulus, the more positive their aesthetic/liking response — i.e., beauty is located in the perceiver's processing experience, not purely in objective stimulus features.
  > "We propose that aesthetic pleasure is a function of the perceiver's processing dynamics: The more fluently perceivers can process an object, the more positive their aesthetic response... we propose that beauty is grounded in the processing experiences of the perceiver, which are in part a function of stimulus properties."
- **[central]** Processing fluency is itself hedonically marked — high fluency is subjectively experienced as a positive affective state, a claim the authors say is backed by psychophysiological evidence (not merely inferred from liking ratings).
  > "Second, processing fluency is itself hedonically marked and high fluency is subjectively experienced as positive, as indicated by psychophysiological findings. Third, processing fluency feeds into judgments of aesthetic appreciation because people draw on their subjective experience in making evaluative judgments, unless the informational value of the experience is called into question."
- **[central]** The fluency-to-liking effect is a misattribution: it is mediated by the affective reaction the ease produces, and it is eliminated when people are led to attribute that feeling to an external source (e.g., background music) — direct evidence that fluency is experienced as 'I like this object' rather than 'this was easy to process.'
  > "The impact of fluency on evaluative judgments is apparently mediated by the elicited affective reactions and hence eliminated when the informational value of the affective reaction is called into question through misattribution manipulations (Winkielman & Fazendeiro, 2003)."
- **[supporting]** The stimulus features classically associated with beauty — goodness of form, symmetry, and figure-ground contrast — are reframed as fluency-enhancers, sharing a common mechanism with non-traditional manipulations like perceptual and conceptual priming, all of which ease processing of the target.
  > "Features that facilitate fluent processing include all the core features identified in the objectivist tradition, like goodness of form, symmetry, figure ground contrast, as well as variables that have not received attention in traditional theories of aesthetic pleasure, like perceptual and conceptual priming procedures."
- **[supporting]** Familiarity-based effects — the mere exposure effect (Zajonc 1968) and prototypicality/averageness — increase liking because previously seen and prototypical stimuli are processed more fluently; the mere exposure effect is described as robust across stimulus types and measures.
  > "Repeated exposure to a stimulus results in more favorable evaluations, a phenomenon known as the mere exposure effect (Zajonc, 1968, 1998). Mere exposure effects have been obtained with a variety of stimuli (faces, ideographs, words, melodies) and a variety of measures (judgments of preference, behavioral choices, physiological responses), indicating the robustness of the phenomenon."

#### Source `a11bc02a` — quality: **primary** · published: 2012-08-17 (preprint; published Int. J. Human-Computer Studies 70(11), Nov 2012)
- **[central]** Across all exposure times, websites combining LOW visual complexity (VC) with HIGH prototypicality (PT) were rated the most beautiful/appealing, while high-VC + low-PT pages produced the worst first impression — giving designers a concrete two-lever target (keep layouts simple and conventional).
  > "Overall, websites of high PT and low VC are perceived as being the most beautiful, whereas websites of low PT and high VC trigger the worst first impression."
- **[central]** Both visual complexity and prototypicality measurably influence aesthetic/beauty judgments within just 17 ms of exposure — confirming that intuitive 'I like this' first impressions form pre-consciously and almost instantly.
  > "Both factors already influence the aesthetic judgments after a very short presentation time of 17 ms."
- **[central]** For websites, the relationship between visual complexity and aesthetic preference is NEGATIVE-LINEAR, not Berlyne's classic inverted-U — websites are already complex stimuli sitting on the descending side of the curve, so simpler = more liked (qualifies the inverted-U as not universally applicable to web UI).
  > "Although the theory of Berlyne (1974) ... would suggest an inverted U-shaped relation between aesthetics and visual complexity, our data show a linear relation among these variables."
- **[supporting]** At the shortest 17 ms exposure the complexity effect (eta-squared = .38) was larger than the prototypicality effect (.19); PT's effect grew with longer exposure to match VC — supporting Leder et al.'s model that perceptual complexity is processed at an earlier stage than typicality.
  > "at 17 ms the magnitude of the effect is more pronounced for VC than for PT ... This supports the reasoning of Leder et al. (2004) that VC is processed at an earlier stage than PT."
- **[supporting]** VC and PT interact rather than add: prototypicality's benefit is blunted when a page is highly complex, and complexity's penalty is muted when prototypicality is low — so the two levers must be optimized together, and both effects were very strong (Study 1 partial eta-squared: VC=.58, PT=.81).
  > "the effect of PT on beauty is less pronounced within complex web pages than within less complex pages. It seems that as soon as VC is too high or PT too low, web pages receive lower beauty ratings, regardless of the characteristics of the other factor."

#### Source `a1c733b6` — quality: **primary** · published: 1994-12
- **[central]** Brightness and saturation — not hue — are the primary, consistent drivers of emotional response to color, measured on the Pleasure-Arousal-Dominance model.
  > "Emotional reactions to color hue, saturation, and brightness (Munsell color system and color chips) were investigated using the Pleasure-Arousal-Dominance emotion model. Saturation (S) and brightness (B) evidenced strong and consistent effects on emotions."
- **[central]** Quantified standardized regression equations show brightness dominates Pleasure and (negatively) Dominance, while saturation dominates Arousal: Pleasure = .69B + .22S, Arousal = -.31B + .60S, Dominance = -.76B + .32S.
  > "Regression equations for standardized variables were; Pleasure = .69B + .22S, Arousal = -.31B + .60S, Dominance = -.76B + .32S."
- **[supporting]** Brightness drives emotional response independent of chromatic content — the effect is about lightness/luminance, not color per se, supporting the companion data-viz finding that luminance (not hue) is the governing dimension.
  > "Brightness effects were nearly the same for chromatic and achromatic colors."
- **[supporting]** Hue does have measurable but secondary emotional effects in a specific ordering — bluish/greenish/purplish hues rated most pleasant, yellow and green-yellow least pleasant — so 'hue is irrelevant' is an overstatement; hue matters less than brightness/saturation, not zero.
  > "Blue, blue-green, green, red-purple, purple, and purple-blue were the most pleasant hues, whereas yellow and green-yellow were the least pleasant. Green-yellow, blue-green, and green were the most arousing, whereas purple-blue and yellow-red were the least arousing."

#### Source `a276e2cb` — quality: **primary** · published: 2019-02 (Journal of Usability Studies/JUX, Vol. 14, Issue 2); underlying NCSU dissertation 2018
- **[central]** In a controlled experiment manipulating aesthetics and usability as separate variables (four versions: High/Low Aesthetics x High/Low Usability patient-portal site), repeated-measures ANOVAs found NO effect of interface aesthetics on perceived usability (SUS) — a boundary condition that fails to reproduce the aesthetic-usability effect under proper experimental control.
  > "repeated measures ANOVAs failed to show an effect of aesthetics on users' judgments of usability. Indeed, results suggested that SUS ratings were unaffected by aesthetics."
- **[central]** What actually drove perceived usability was repeated use (occasion) and the system's real usability — not its aesthetics; i.e., perceived usability tracked actual usability, not beauty.
  > "the analyses showed a significant effect of occasion and usability, rather than aesthetics, on users' judgments of usability."
- **[central]** The authors argue the well-cited aesthetics-usability correlations may be a measurement artifact of how participants use rating scales rather than evidence of a real underlying relationship — directly undercutting weak correlational support for the aesthetic-usability effect.
  > "the significant correlations between users' ratings of aesthetics and usability in this and previous studies were produced by an effect of scale use by participants, rather than by a relationship between aesthetics and usability."
- **[supporting]** The causal direction commonly assumed (beauty makes interfaces seem more usable) may be reversed: apparent usability may make users perceive greater beauty, or the link may be spurious. Consistent with the journal abstract's finding that after use, low usability lowered users' aesthetics ratings.
  > "In fact, the reverse might be true—that apparent usability might cause users to perceive greater beauty in the interface. Or, perhaps the relation between apparent usability and aesthetics is spurious."
- **[supporting]** Despite a clean factorial manipulation, the two hypotheses (that aesthetics contribute disproportionately to usability judgments, and that this influence diminishes with repeated use) received only very limited support — meaning the aesthetic-usability effect did not hold in this design.
  > "Results provided very limited support for the hypotheses."

#### Source `a287714c` — quality: **primary** · published: 2002-10 (PSYCHE, Vol. 8, Issue 10, October 2002)
- **[central]** High processing fluency carries a positive hedonic mark: fluent processing produces a positively-valenced subjective experience that participants interpret as a reaction to the stimulus, yielding more positive evaluations (the fluency -> affect -> liking mechanism). [STRONG]
  > "we assume that high fluency leads to a positively marked subjective experience. Participants interpret this positive response as their reaction to the stimulus, resulting in more positive evaluations. This assumption is consistent with a more general model of how experiences influence judgments, the so-called 'feelings-as-information model.'"
- **[central]** Fluency-induced positive affect is measurable physiologically: facilitating processing via visual priming and longer presentation duration selectively increased activity over the zygomaticus ('smiling muscle') region (Winkielman & Cacioppo 2001), replicated with repetition by Harmon-Jones & Allen 2001 — direct evidence that fluency triggers spontaneous positive affect, not merely self-reported liking. [STRONG]
  > "these researchers used facial electromyography (EMG) to monitor electrical activity over participants' 'smiling muscle' (zygomaticus major) and 'frowning muscle' (corrugator supercilii). Facilitation of stimulus processing with visual priming (Study 1) and presentation duration (Study 2) selectively increased activity over the zygomaticus region, indicating that the resulting affect was more positive"
- **[central]** The fluency-driven affect is misattributed to the stimulus 'by default': in Winkielman & Fazendeiro (2001), priming raised liking for new pictures, but telling participants their reactions might come from background music eliminated the effect — showing the positive feeling is normally (mis)attributed to the judged target without awareness of its true source. [STRONG]
  > "attributing the affective reaction to the music eliminated this effect... This pattern of results is consistent with our theoretical assumption that processing facilitation elicits a positive affective reaction that is 'by default' attributed to the judged target."
- **[central]** A single fluency mechanism parsimoniously unifies multiple aesthetic-preference phenomena — preference for familiarity, symmetry, prototypicality, and high figure-ground contrast all arise because each variable eases (facilitates) processing and thereby triggers positive affect. [STRONG for the mechanism; the listed perceptual cues themselves are CONVENTION-to-STRONG depending on cue]
  > "the notion of fluency as an indicator of cognitive progress may provide a parsimonious account for a variety of preference phenomena, such as preference for familiarity, symmetry, prototypicality, high figure-ground contrast, and many others. In this account, all these phenomena occur because the respective variable facilitates processing and thereby triggers positive affect."
- **[supporting]** The mere-exposure effect (Zajonc 1968) — liking for a neutral stimulus rising with repetition — is interpreted as a fluency effect: repetition increases processing ease, which is experienced as liking; familiarity drives unconscious preference through this route. [CONVENTION/STRONG — the mere-exposure effect is well-replicated; the fluency *explanation* of it is a supported but not exclusive account]
  > "Zajonc (1968) to posit the existence of a 'mere-exposure effect' or the phenomenon that liking for an initially neutral stimulus increases as a result of repetition... Several authors have proposed that the mere-exposure effect reflects changes in processing fluency"

#### Source `a2d9104a` — quality: **primary** · published: 1988
- **[central]** Across three experiments on preference for semantic category exemplars, typicality (prototypicality) was a far stronger predictor of preference than novelty or mere exposure, accounting for roughly eight to nine times more explained variance than long-term novelty, short-term novelty, or mere exposure combined.
  > "Typicality accounted for eight or nine times more of explained variance in preference than did long-term novelty, short-term novelty, or mere exposure."
- **[central]** The paper frames a direct theoretical contest: Berlyne's psychobiological theory holds that collative variables (complexity, novelty) drive aesthetic preference, whereas Martindale's cognitive theory holds that prototypicality/meaningfulness is the main determinant — and the data favored the prototypicality account.
  > "Berlyne's psychobiological theory, which emphasizes collative variables such as complexity and novelty, versus Martindale's cognitive theory, which emphasizes prototypicality or meaningfulness as the main determinant of aesthetic preference."
- **[supporting]** Contrary to Berlyne's inverted-U prediction, in these experiments preference rose with typicality and related to collative variables via monotonic or U-shaped functions rather than an inverted-U — relevant to qualifying when the complexity/arousal inverted-U actually holds.
  > "Preference was related to both typicality and collative variables by monotonic or U-shaped functions."

#### Source `a3146466` — quality: **primary** · published: 1998-01 (Psychological Science, Vol. 9, No. 1, January 1998; received 2/10/96, revision accepted 8/4/97)
- **[central]** Processing/perceptual fluency causally increases liking even when fluency is manipulated by means OTHER than repetition (visual priming, figure-ground contrast, exposure duration) — across three experiments fluency raised preference and the experience of fluency was affectively positive. This is foundational support for the fluency-->positive-affect-->liking chain underpinning intuitive aesthetic preference.
  > "We conclude (a) that perceptual fluency increases liking and (b) that the experience of fluency is affectively positive, and hence attributed to positive but not to negative features, as reflected in a differential impact on positive and negative judgments."
- **[central]** Fluency operates below awareness and is misattributed to the stimulus itself: in Experiment 1, a 25-ms matching prime (which participants did not consciously notice) made target drawings be judged prettier (M=4.66 vs 4.39, t(52)=2.43, p<.05) and recognized faster (1,470 ms vs 2,415 ms, t(52)=4.44, p<.001) than a mismatching prime — directly evidencing 'I like this' as a misread of ease of processing.
  > "In postexperimental interviews, participants expressed surprise that priming pictures had been presented... the drawings preceded by matching primes were judged as prettier (M = 4.66, SD = 1.10) than the drawings preceded by mismatching primes (M = 4.39, SD = 1.14), t(52) = 2.43, p < .05. Moreover, the drawings preceded by matching primes were recognized faster... This enhanced perceptual fluency is then misattributed to liking."
- **[central]** Higher figure-ground contrast (clarity) directly raises aesthetic ratings: in Experiment 2 stimuli with greater contrast were judged more pretty and less ugly (prettiness increased with contrast for both dark-on-white and light-on-black, p<.001; ugliness decreased with contrast, p<.05), and the effect was a preference for contrast itself, not for darker graytones. This is the most directly actionable UI move (clarity/high contrast --> liked more).
  > "Stimuli were judged as more pretty, and less ugly, the higher the contrast... This finding shows that the effects are due to a preference for contrast rather than darker graytones per se."
- **[supporting]** Perceptual fluency is affectively POSITIVE, not affectively neutral: it pushes judgments in the positive direction regardless of question framing (raising 'pretty/liking' AND lowering 'ugly/disliking'), distinguishing it from 'nonspecific activation' which would amplify whichever feature the question focuses on. The group x linear-trend interaction was significant, F(1,34)=16.15, p<.001.
  > "The current findings show that perceptual fluency is affectively positive, rather than neutral, as reflected in increased judgments of prettiness and liking and decreased judgments of ugliness and disliking of stimuli. Thus, the present findings are incompatible with the notion of nonspecific activation."
- **[supporting]** Longer exposure/presentation duration increases liking while holding stimulus features constant: in Experiment 3, patterns shown for longer durations were liked more and disliked less (interaction of exposure time x group F(1,18)=5.35, p<.05), attributed to increased perceptual fluency from longer processing time.
  > "Stimuli shown for a longer duration were liked more, and disliked less... The results confirmed this prediction, F(1, 18) = 5.35, p < .05, for the interaction of exposure time and group."

#### Source `a3767045` — quality: **primary** · published: 2021-01-15
- **[central]** Across several product-design studies with over 1,800 participants total, there was little/no support for Berlyne's inverted-U relationship between visual complexity and liking — directly weakening it as a design law in the UI/product context.
  > "several studies with over 1800 participants in total showed that aesthetic complexity does influence consumer appraisals, but there was scant evidence for an inverted U‐shape relationship."
- **[central]** After controlling for mediators and covariates, higher aesthetic complexity was NEGATIVELY related to product liking (and positively related to perceived originality) — i.e., simpler designs were liked more, consistent with low-complexity/high-prototypicality appeal findings.
  > "When accounting for the influence of mediators and covariates, aesthetic complexity appeared negatively related to product liking and positively related to perceived originality."
- **[supporting]** The psychological mechanism is not the classic effort-vs-arousal tradeoff: arousal/interest and perceived producer effort/skill mediated liking more strongly than cognitive (processing) effort.
  > "Regarding the underlying mechanisms, arousal or interest and perceived producer effort or skill were found to be more important mediators than cognitive effort."
- **[supporting]** The complexity-liking relationship is moderated by brand status, so any complexity effect on liking is context-dependent rather than universal.
  > "Brand status was also found to moderate the relationship between complexity and liking."
- **[supporting]** The authors explicitly frame the inverted-U as an unverified common assumption needing a more nuanced view, supporting treating Berlyne's inverted-U as convention rather than a robust empirical law for design.
  > "These findings call for further research and a more nuanced perspective on the role of complexity."

#### Source `a5e1d31a` — quality: **primary** · published: 2003-02
- **[central]** Typicality and novelty jointly and equally predict aesthetic preference for consumer products, but each suppresses the other's effect — empirically grounding the MAYA principle that the most-preferred designs optimally combine being advanced/novel with being typical/acceptable.
  > "typicality and novelty are jointly and equally effective in explaining the aesthetic preference of consumer products, but that they suppress each other's effect"
- **[central]** Neither typicality alone nor novelty alone correlates significantly with aesthetic preference; each becomes highly significant only after statistically controlling for the other (a classic statistical suppression effect), so MAYA must be modeled as a two-variable balance rather than a single 'familiarity = liking' rule.
  > "Direct correlations between both variables and aesthetic preference were not significant, but each relationship became highly significant when the influence of the other variable was partialed out"
- **[supporting]** The MAYA pattern held regardless of observer expertise, indicating the typicality–novelty preference balance is not an artifact of design training and generalizes across viewers.
  > "observer expertise did not significantly alter this preference pattern"

#### Source `a60a933f` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2008 (International Journal of Human-Computer Studies)
- **[central]** For basic/abstract geometric images, symmetry and balance measurably raise aesthetic appeal ratings (Bauerly & Liu's computational models of balance and symmetry correlated with subjective appeal).
  > ""Symmetry and balance affect aesthetic appeal ratings" in basic images"
- **[central]** The strong symmetry-to-appeal relationship is context-dependent: it diminishes when the same principle is tested on realistic web pages rather than abstract image blocks, so symmetry preference is not a universal design law.
  > "the strong relationship found between symmetry and aesthetic appeal diminished when tested with more realistic (i.e., context-dependent) web pages."
- **[supporting]** The number of compositional elements influences appeal — higher visual complexity (more elements) lowers aesthetic appeal ratings, consistent with low-complexity preference for interfaces.
  > "the number of compositional elements influences aesthetic appeal ratings, with symmetry also playing a role such that participants find the more symmetric images appealing"
- **[supporting]** Bauerly & Liu built computational models to predict aesthetic preference from formal, objective image properties (balance, symmetry), aiming to identify optimal design spaces.
  > "The researchers proposed computational models aimed at identifying optimal design spaces based on formal principles of aesthetic properties."

#### Source `a6695ed5` — quality: **unreliable** · published: ?

#### Source `a756b444` — quality: **primary** · published: 2006-06
- **[central]** Colour harmony for two-colour combinations is empirically predictable by a quantitative model derived from a psychophysical experiment (1431 colour pairs rated on a 10-category harmony scale), rather than only from classical art-theory rules. This gives color-harmony modeling a measured, data-driven basis [STRONG for the model's empirical existence; the model itself is one validated formula, not a universal law].
  > "A total of 1431 colour pairs were used as stimuli in a psychophysical experiment for the visual assessment of harmony ... colour harmony assessed for each colour pair using a 10-category scale ranging from 'extremely harmonious' to 'extremely disharmonious.'"
- **[central]** The harmony model's predictive factors are chromatic difference, lightness sum, lightness difference, and a hue effect — i.e., harmony is driven by measurable CIELAB color-space differences, not by complementary-color or golden-ratio folklore. This supports treating 'color harmony' as quantifiable while undercutting the named art-school rules.
  > "A modified version of the model uses chromatic difference, lightness sum, lightness difference and hue effect."
- **[supporting]** Lightness is a major harmony driver: higher lightness correlates with greater harmony, and slight brightness variations reduce harmony. This synergizes with the companion data-visualization finding that luminance (not hue) is the dominant perceptual variable.
  > "slight brightness variations can reduce harmony, and higher lightness values correlate with greater harmony."
- **[supporting]** Same-hue or same-chroma colour pairs tend to be judged harmonious, giving empirical backing to monochromatic/analogous palettes (a likely fluency/clarity benefit) over arbitrary high-contrast hue clashes.
  > "Combinations with the same hue or chroma tend to create harmony."

#### Source `a7a4b1a7` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2024-02-03
- **[central]** The aesthetic-usability effect is the tendency for users to perceive attractive products as more usable; people believe things that look better will work better even when they are not actually more effective or efficient.
  > "The aesthetic-usability effect refers to users' tendency to perceive attractive products as more usable. People tend to believe that things that look better will work better — even if they aren't actually more effective or efficient."
- **[central]** In Kurosu & Kashimura's 1995 ATM study (Hitachi Design Center, 26 UI variations rated by 252 participants), aesthetic appeal correlated more strongly with PERCEIVED ease of use than with ACTUAL ease of use — the primary empirical basis for the effect.
  > "The correlation between the participants' ratings of aesthetic appeal and perceived ease of use was stronger than the correlation between their ratings of aesthetic appeal and actual ease of use."
- **[central]** A positive emotional response to visual design makes users more tolerant of minor usability problems (an aesthetic halo over usability judgments), but this forgiveness does not extend to large usability problems.
  > "When users have a positive emotional response to visual design, it makes them more tolerant of minor usability issues."
- **[supporting]** Attractive visual design produces a credibility/professionalism halo, making a site appear orderly, well-designed, and professional.
  > "Visual designs that appeal to your users have the side effect of making your site appear orderly, well-designed, and professional."

#### Source `a856ebdb` — quality: **primary** · published: 2006-03 (Behaviour & Information Technology, Vol. 25, No. 2, March-April 2006, pp. 115-126)
- **[central]** Visual appeal of web homepages can be assessed within 50 ms: mean appeal ratings at 50 ms exposure correlated with ratings at 500 ms exposure at r = .947 (r2 = .897) on the first phase and r = .953 (r2 = .908) on the second, both p < .001 — so a first impression of appeal forms essentially as fast at 50 ms as at 500 ms.
  > "It revealed that r = .947, p < .001 (r2 = .897). This result was slightly lower, but comparable to that obtained in Study 2 for the 500 ms condition alone... the correlation for the second phase of both conditions yielded a value of r = .953, p < .001, (r2 = .908)... Findings would thus appear to be as robust with an exposure time of 50 ms as with an exposure time of 500 ms."
- **[central]** Visual-appeal judgments are highly reliable/stable: when participants rated the same homepages twice, mean appeal ratings across the two phases correlated at r = .97 (p < .001) in Study 1, indicating appeal is consistent rather than idiosyncratic across repeated exposure.
  > "correlation coefficient (r) was .97 (p < .001), indicating that... participants' ratings were highly reliable."
- **[supporting]** Perceived visual appeal was very highly correlated with 'good design', 'good colour', 'good layout', 'interesting' and 'imaginative' (r2 = .86 to .92), but was essentially UNRELATED to the simple-complex dimension (r2 = .01, p > .80) and only moderately related to clear-confusing (r2 = .39) — i.e. in this study perceived complexity did not predict appeal.
  > "There were very high squared correlations between visual appeal and five of the seven visual characteristics: interesting – boring (r2 = .91, p < .001), good design – bad design (r2 = .92, p < .001), good colour – bad colour (r2 = .90, p < .001), good layout – bad layout (r2 = .88, p < .001), and imaginative – unimaginative (r2 = .86, p < .001). There was a low correlation between visual appeal and simple – complex (r2 = .01, p > .80) and a moderate correlation between attractiveness and clear – confusing judgements (r2 = .39, p < .001)."
- **[supporting]** Aesthetic-usability / halo effect: a website previously rated as highly visually appealing remained equally highly valued before AND after a usability test in which participants completed fewer than half the tasks successfully — visual appeal drew attention away from usability problems.
  > "one study exploring users' experience with a website previously found to be visually extremely appealing, was equally highly valued before and after a usability test in which, on average, participants completed less than one-half of the tasks successfully (Lindgaard and Dudek 2002)... the strong impact of the visual appeal of the site seemed to draw attention away from usability problems."
- **[supporting]** Although appeal forms at 50 ms, judgments at 50 ms are noisier and less consistent than at 500 ms: interrater agreement averaged r = .337/.403 at 50 ms versus r = .557/.599 at 500 ms, and 40% of within-subject phase-1/phase-2 correlations fell below r = .60 at 50 ms while none did at 500 ms.
  > "the average correlation for each phase was computed yielding r = .557 at 500 ms on the first phase, and r = .599 on the second phase. In the 50-ms condition, the average correlation was r = .337 on the first phase and r = .403 on the second... in the 50-ms condition 40% of the correlations were below r = .60 whereas none was below r = .60 in the 500-ms condition."

#### Source `a9b2b762` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2015-04-13
- **[central]** Controlled/empirical rectangle-preference studies show people do NOT prefer the golden rectangle: Devlin's repeated Stanford exercise found students pick favorite rectangles seemingly at random (and inconsistently on repeat), and a UC Berkeley Haas study found consumers prefer rectangles in the 1.414-1.732 aspect-ratio range, with the golden rectangle not the clear favorite.
  > "Devlin's experiments aren't the only ones to show people don't prefer the golden ratio. A study from the Haas School of Business at Berkeley found that, on average, consumers prefer rectangles that are in the range of 1.414 and 1.732. The range contains the golden rectangle, but its exact dimensions are not the clear favorite."
- **[central]** There is no scientific evidence that the golden ratio governs aesthetic preference; its 'beauty math' reputation is a historical myth originating from a 1799 misattribution (to Luca Pacioli) and the unfounded universal-law claims of 19th-century psychologist Adolf Zeising, who 'saw patterns where none exist.'
