  > "Those who believe the golden ratio is the hidden math behind beauty are falling for a 150-year-old scam... There's also no science to really back it up... the idea that the golden ratio has any relationship to aesthetics at all comes primarily from two people, one of whom was misquoted, and the other of whom was just making shit up."
- **[supporting]** Practicing designers and architects do not treat the golden ratio as a design rule or a driver of aesthetic quality; multiple named professionals (Meier, Andrasek, Lupi, Behar, Harriss) describe it as, at most, a minor optional tool, not a universal formula for beauty.
  > ""It is a simple number in many ways, and as a result it does turn up in a wide variety of places..." Harriss tells me by email. "[But] it is certainly not the universal formula behind aesthetic beauty.""
- **[supporting]** The golden ratio myth persists because of human pattern-seeking psychology (apophenia): people are predisposed to find meaning/patterns and retroactively project the ratio onto designs they already like, without being able to substantiate it.
  > ""We're creatures who are genetically programmed to see patterns and to seek meaning," he says... "They are victims to their natural desire to find meaning in the pattern of the universe, without the math skills to tell them that the patterns they think they see are illusory.""
- **[tangential]** Because the golden ratio is an irrational number (1.6180339887...), no real-world object can strictly embody it; claimed instances only approximate it, which undermines precise golden-ratio design dogma in logos/layouts.
  > ""Strictly speaking, it's impossible for anything in the real-world to fall into the golden ratio, because it's an irrational number," says Keith Devlin, a professor of mathematics at Stanford University."

#### Source `aaff107e` — quality: **primary** · published: 2019 (Empirical Studies of the Arts, Vol. 37, Issue 1, pp. 104-114; listed publication date January 1, 2019)
- **[central]** Symmetry is not a universal determinant of beauty; preference for it is moderated by expertise — in this study art experts (artists and art historians) rated asymmetrical stimuli as most beautiful, the opposite of typical non-expert responses.
  > "In complete contrast to responses typically found in non-art experts, art experts found asymmetrical and simple stimuli as most beautiful. This is evidence of the effects of specific education and training on aesthetic appreciation and a direct challenge to the universality of symmetry."
- **[central]** The widespread treatment of symmetry as a powerful, near-universal driver of beauty is a cross-disciplinary convention rather than an unconditional law — the same framing has 'low standing' in the arts and is empirically contradicted by expert preferences.
  > "Scientific disciplines as diverse as biology, physics, and psychological aesthetics regard symmetry as one of the most important principles in nature and one of the most powerful determinants of beauty. However... symmetry has a low standing in the arts and humanities."
- **[supporting]** The study jointly manipulated symmetry and complexity; experts favored stimuli that were both asymmetrical and simple, indicating these aesthetic dimensions interact rather than each pushing preference in one fixed direction.
  > "Two groups of art experts—artists and art historians—and a group of non-experts provided spontaneous beauty ratings of visual stimuli that varied in symmetry and complexity."
- **[supporting]** Aesthetic preference for symmetry is shaped by training and education, so a design rule like 'symmetry is more beautiful' holds for general (non-expert) audiences but not as an absolute principle across all viewers.
  > "This is evidence of the effects of specific education and training on aesthetic appreciation and a direct challenge to the universality of symmetry."

#### Source `ab5391a8` — quality: **primary** · published: 2001-12
- **[central]** Stimuli that are easier to process (higher perceptual fluency) elicit a measurable positive affective response, demonstrated by increased zygomaticus major (smile-muscle) EMG activity to easy-to-process images across two experiments.
  > "In both studies, easy-to-process pictures elicited higher activity over the region of zygomaticus major, indicating positive affect."
- **[central]** Processing ease was manipulated unobtrusively while participants viewed neutral pictures, so the positive affect arose from processing dynamics rather than from any consciously evaluated property of the stimulus — physiological evidence for the fluency-to-affect mechanism underlying liking.
  > "participants watched a series of neutral pictures while the processing ease was unobtrusively manipulated. Affective reactions were assessed with facial electromyography (EMG)."
- **[supporting]** The authors theorize that the affect system monitors the internal dynamics of information processing, such that facilitation of stimulus processing produces a brief, mild, positive affective response — the proposed causal basis for fluency-driven preference.
  > "the authors predicted that facilitation of stimulus processing should elicit a brief, mild, positive affective response."
- **[supporting]** Fluency-generated affect can bias downstream judgments, providing a mechanism by which ease of processing is misattributed to liking or other social/aesthetic evaluations.
  > "The research illuminates how cognitive fluency may underlie preference phenomena and introduce affective biases in social judgment."

#### Source `ab745632` — quality: **primary** · published: 2003
- **[central]** In a large field study, the visual 'design look' of a website was the single most frequently mentioned factor when people evaluated credibility — cited in 46.1% of comments, ahead of every content/accuracy factor. This is direct evidence for the aesthetic-usability / credibility halo: surface appearance dominates trust judgments. [STRONG — large-N field study, though it measures spontaneous mentions, not a controlled design manipulation]
  > "We found that the design look of the site was mentioned most frequently, being present in 46.1 percent of the comments. Next most common were comments about information structure and information focus."
- **[supporting]** Design appearance was noticed far more than information-substance factors: the ranked categories were Design Look 46.1%, Information Design/Structure 28.5%, Information Focus 25.1%, Company Motive 15.5%. Visual/structural surface cues outranked content-accuracy cues for credibility.
  > "1. Design Look  46.1% Overall ... 2. Information Design/Structure  28.5% Overall ... 3. Information Focus  25.1% Overall ... 4. Company Motive  15.5% Overall"
- **[central]** The finding rests on a large sample — 2,684 participants evaluating two live websites on a shared topic (health, news, etc.) — which gives the credibility-halo result strong statistical weight as a field study (vs. small-lab work).
  > "In this study, 2,684 people evaluated the credibility of two live Web sites on a similar topic (such as health or news). We gathered the comments people wrote about each site's credibility and analyzed the comments to find out what features of a Web site get noticed when people evaluate credibility."
- **[supporting]** The authors explain the dominance of design look via Prominence-Interpretation Theory: visual design is highly prominent (most likely to be noticed) and is then interpreted, accounting for why surface aesthetics drive credibility more than content. This supplies the mechanism behind the aesthetic-credibility halo.
  > "We discuss reasons for the prominence of design look, interpret the findings in light of Prominence-Interpretation Theory, and outline the implications of this research for Consumer WebWatch."

#### Source `abc81b22` — quality: **primary** · published: 2018-10-03
- **[central]** The red-attractiveness effect is small at best: men rating women showed d = 0.26 (CI [0.12, 0.40], dropping to d = 0.19 excluding an outlier), and women rating men showed d = 0.13 (CI [0.01, 0.25]) — far weaker than the originally claimed effect.
  > "Men rating women: ... d = 0.26 [0.12, 0.40], p = .0004, N = 2,961 ... Excluding an outlier study: d = 0.19 [0.09, 0.29] ... Women rating men: Very small but statistically significant d = 0.13 [0.01, 0.25], p = .03, N = 2,739"
- **[central]** Preregistered studies of women rating men found essentially no red effect (d = -0.10, CI [-0.24, 0.04]), versus a positive effect (d = 0.21) in non-preregistered studies — evidence the published effect is inflated by researcher degrees of freedom.
  > "Preregistration (women only): Preregistered studies found dramatically smaller effects: d = −0.10 [−0.24, 0.04], k = 8 versus non-preregistered d = 0.21 [0.08, 0.34], k = 28"
- **[central]** Published studies reported substantially larger red effects than unpublished ones (men: d = 0.38 published vs d = 0.12 unpublished), and funnel-plot analysis for women showed strong small-study bias (r = .52), both indicating publication bias.
  > "Trends emerged showing published studies reported larger effects than unpublished ones, with men showing d = 0.38 versus d = 0.12 for unpublished work ... For women rating men, funnel plot analysis revealed bias: r = .52 [.23, .72], p = .001. This indicated smaller studies reported larger effects."
- **[supporting]** The red-attractiveness effect has declined over time (women: slope -0.07 per year), consistent with a decline effect / regression toward zero as methods tightened.
  > "Publication year (both sexes): Later studies showed declining effects, with women showing slope of −0.07 [−0.11, −0.02] per year."
- **[central]** The meta-analysis authors split on interpretation: Lehmann & Calin-Jageman judge the effect 'very small, potentially nonexistent' and below practical significance, while Elliot defends it as real but moderator-dependent — so the 'red boosts attraction' claim is contested even among the analysts.
  > "Lehmann & Calin-Jageman conclude the effect is "very small, potentially nonexistent" and note preregistered studies find effects "too small to be of practical significance." Elliot acknowledges methodological complexity and the need for future research examining moderators and mechanisms."

#### Source `abd9df03` — quality: **primary** · published: 2010-05 (Applied Ergonomics, 41(3):403-410; first published online 2009)
- **[central]** The aesthetic-usability effect held experimentally: users rated a highly appealing mobile phone as more usable than an unappealing one, even though the two phones were functionally identical (only visual appearance differed). This is direct controlled evidence that perceived usability is inflated by beauty independent of actual usability quality.
  > "Two functionally identical mobile phones were manipulated with regard to their visual appearance (highly appealing vs not appealing) ... The results showed that participants using the highly appealing phone rated their appliance as being more usable than participants operating the unappealing model."
- **[central]** Aesthetics improved actual task performance, not merely perceived usability: the attractive phone produced shorter task-completion times than the visually identical-function unappealing phone. This goes beyond a pure halo/perception effect and suggests appearance can affect objective behavior.
  > "the visual appearance of the phone had a positive effect on performance, leading to reduced task completion times for the attractive model."
- **[supporting]** The study is a controlled experiment (between-subjects manipulation of appearance with held-constant function), supporting a [STRONG] rating for the aesthetic-usability effect rather than convention or anecdote.
  > "This article examined the effects of product aesthetics on several outcome variables in usability tests. Employing a computer simulation of a mobile phone, 60 adolescents (14-17 yrs) were asked to complete a number of typical tasks of mobile phone users."

#### Source `ac89e958` — quality: **primary** · published: 2000-12
- **[central]** Users' pre-use perceptions of an interface's beauty correlate strongly with their pre-use perceptions of its usability (correlation present before any interaction).
  > "Pre-experimental measures indicate strong correlations between system's perceived aesthetics and perceived usability."
- **[central]** The strong aesthetics-usability correlation persists after users actually use the system, not just before use.
  > "Post-experimental measures indicated that the strong correlation remained intact."
- **[central]** An interface's degree of aesthetics shapes post-use perceptions of both aesthetics AND usability, while the system's degree of actual usability does not affect those post-use perceptions.
  > "the degree of system's aesthetics affected the post-use perceptions of both aesthetics and usability, whereas the degree of actual usability had no such effect"
- **[supporting]** The aesthetic-usability relationship was tested experimentally using a computerized ATM-surrogate application, measuring perceptions before and after interaction.
  > "An experiment was conducted to test the relationships between users' perceptions of a computerized system's beauty and usability."

#### Source `ada9bde3` — quality: **primary** · published: 2006 (Psychological Science, Vol. 17, No. 9, pp. 799-806)
- **[central]** Prototypicality of visual stimuli predicts BOTH processing fluency (faster categorization speed) and rated attractiveness — establishing that the same stimuli that are easy to process are also liked more.
  > "prototypicality was a predictor of both fluency (categorization speed) and attractiveness"
- **[central]** Processing fluency statistically MEDIATES the prototypicality-attractiveness link: prototypes are attractive because they are easier to process, not (only) because of mate-value or category-signaling per se. This is the paper's core causal claim and the mechanism underlying the [STRONG] fluency-liking effect.
  > "fluency mediated the effect of prototypicality on attractiveness"
- **[central]** Viewing abstract prototypes elicits a quick POSITIVE affective reaction measured physiologically (facial EMG over the zygomaticus/smile muscle), demonstrating that fluency produces genuine positive affect — not merely a cognitive judgment — and supporting the 'hedonic marking of fluency' account that grounds the aesthetic-usability and beauty-from-clarity effects.
  > "Experiment 3, using the psychophysiological technique of facial electromyography, confirmed that viewing abstract prototypes elicits quick positive affective reactions"
- **[supporting]** The preference for prototypes is explained by general cognitive mechanisms — fluent processing plus a general preference for fluently processed stimuli — rather than by domain-specific evolutionary mate-value signaling alone; this reframes prototypicality/averageness preference as a fluency phenomenon, directly linking the MAYA/prototypicality literature to processing-fluency theory.
  > "prototypicality preference results from more general mechanisms—fluent processing of prototypes and preference for fluently processed stimuli"
- **[supporting]** The fluency-driven liking effect operated automatically — it held regardless of whether participants consciously attended to category membership or noticed the ease of processing — supporting the research question's premise that users like an interface 'without consciously knowing why.'
  > "The findings held regardless of whether participants consciously attended to categorical membership or noticed the processing fluency, suggesting an automatic mechanism linking ease of mental processing to aesthetic preference."

#### Source `adff3136` — quality: **blog** · published: 2019-02 (original; updated through 2023-2025)
- **[central]** The actual Loyola University research papers (Dr. Ellen Hoadley, 1990 and 2000) do not contain the '80% brand recognition' statistic; the author obtained the papers and found the quote absent.
  > "neither of the two papers contained that famous quote"
- **[central]** The '80% increase in brand recognition' claim traces not to academic research but to a Xerox marketing leaflet (c. 2005, possibly 2001) titled '20 Ways to Share the Color Knowledge.'
  > "it was a Xerox marketing leaflet...that is the oldest claim I found of the 80% increase in brand recognition"
- **[supporting]** The underlying Loyola studies were about color versus monochrome information processing in graphs/documents, not brand recognition, so the popular stat is misattributed and stripped of context.
  > "The famous quote is about the choice to use color or monochrome. And when it's related to product choices, it's about personal color preferences."
- **[supporting]** Any defensible version of the claim must be framed as a color-vs-monochrome comparison, not an absolute brand-recognition boost.
  > "Color increases brand recognition by up to 80%, compared to monochrome."

#### Source `aed42ebe` — quality: **primary** · published: 2013-04-27 (CHI 2013, April 27–May 2, 2013, Paris)
- **[central]** Perceptual models of visual complexity and colorfulness, combined with demographic variables, explain about 48% of the variance in first-impression aesthetic appeal ratings made after viewing a website for only 500ms (N=242 raters, 450 websites). This empirically supports that intuitive 'liking' of an interface is substantially predictable from low-level image statistics before conscious evaluation. [STRONG]
  > "we demonstrated that—in combination with demographic variables—colorfulness and visual complexity explain 48% of the variance in users’ first impressions."
- **[central]** Visual complexity is a much stronger driver of first-impression appeal than colorfulness; appeal drops sharply as complexity rises, while colorfulness contributes only a minor effect. Actionable: restraint/low visual complexity matters more for intuitive liking than how colorful a UI is. [STRONG]
  > "Our results show that visual complexity accounts for a signiﬁcantly larger amount of the variance in the observed ratings on visual appeal than colorfulness."
- **[central]** The complexity-appeal relationship is NOT a strong Berlyne inverted-U for websites; high complexity produces the largest drop in appeal while low and medium complexity are liked about equally (closer to monotonic decline). This qualifies/contradicts a naive inverted-U and aligns with Tuch et al. 2012. [STRONG]
  > "a high visual complexity results in the largest decrease in appeal, but websites with low levels of complexity are similarly liked to those with a medium complexity."
- **[supporting]** First impressions of website appeal form within 50-500ms, before conscious processing of details, and beautiful sites are subsequently judged more usable and trustworthy (halo / aesthetic-usability effect). Supports research-question section 4. [STRONG/CONVENTION review-cited]
  > "Websites that are perceived as beautiful are also perceived as usable [23, 14, 28, 22] and trustworthy [22]. Since users make reliable judgments within the ﬁrst 50 to 500ms [23, 32]"
- **[supporting]** Aesthetic preference is not universal: appeal ratings depend on demographic background. Age interacts with complexity (participants over 45 preferred low complexity more, F(4)=6.53, p<.001) and education interacts with colorfulness (PhDs most put off by high colorfulness, F(4)=2.61, p<.05); gender showed no significant interaction. Even Webby Award sites drew widely dispersed ratings. Supports cultural/individual variation caveat. [STRONG]
  > "the importance of these two website characteristics is not universal, but dependent on users’ demographic backgrounds."

#### Source `af44bc5a` — quality: **primary** · published: 2002-10-28
- **[central]** Website credibility judgments form largely outside conscious awareness: users notice and evaluate page elements 'quickly and subconsciously' while assembling an overall credibility assessment, rather than via deliberate reasoning. This is direct primary-source support for the research question's core premise that interface evaluation is fast, automatic, and pre-conscious (the mechanism behind 'I like/trust this but don't know why').
  > "In most cases, a user will quickly and subconsciously notice Web site elements and evaluate them, all the while compiling an overall assessment about the sites credibility."
- **[central]** Visual 'design look' is the single most frequently cited feature when people evaluate websites for credibility, dominating over substantive trust signals like contact information and privacy policy, which 'almost never' surfaced unprompted. This is the empirical backbone of the aesthetic-usability / credibility-halo effect: appearance drives perceived trustworthiness. (Caveat: this is from the lab's observational MostCredible.org 2002 survey, not a controlled experiment, so it is descriptive/[CONVENTION]-grade rather than [STRONG].)
  > "The 2002 MostCredible study showed design look to be the most widely mentioned feature of Web sites when they were evaluated for credibility, while things like contact information and privacy policy almost never showed up."
- **[supporting]** A single salient negative visual impression can produce a firm credibility verdict from one element alone, before any further evaluation — i.e., a bad-looking site is judged not credible on sight. Supports the persistence/asymmetry of negative first impressions in the appearance-credibility link.
  > "perhaps this is most common if people find the first element they notice to be quite bad  like a site that looks horrible"
- **[supporting]** The theory models credibility impact as the joint product of Prominence (whether an element is noticed) and Interpretation (how it is judged), and explicitly maps 'surface credibility' (the look/feel impression) to Prominence — so visual salience is a multiplicative, not additive, gate on credibility. Frames the actionable synthesis: a positive signal has zero impact unless it is noticed.
  > "Surface credibility  relates to Prominence"
- **[tangential]** Interpretation of identical interface features is not universal — it varies by culture, user context, task, and situation, so the same element can raise or lower perceived credibility across audiences. This supports the cross-cutting caveat that aesthetic/color/credibility effects are context- and culture-dependent rather than fixed properties of the stimulus.
  > "People do not interpret identical Web site features in the same way. Culture plays a role in Interpretation."

#### Source `af841493` — quality: **primary** · published: 2010-05-11
- **[central]** The Ecological Valence Theory holds that color preferences are not innate hue responses but arise from the average affective valence of objects a person associates with each color — liking colors tied to positively-valued objects (clear skies, clean water) and disliking colors tied to negatively-valued ones (feces, rot).
  > "Color preferences arise from people's average affective responses to color-associated objects."
- **[central]** The Weighted Affective Valence Estimate (WAVE), computed purely from object-association affect with no fitted parameters, predicts average color preference very strongly (r = +0.893, R-squared = 0.80, p < 0.001) — meaning ~80% of the variance in color liking is accounted for by ecological object associations, not hue per se.
  > "no free parameters were estimated in calculating the WAVE"
- **[supporting]** Aggregate color preference peaks at blue/cyan and bottoms out at dark yellow (olive) and chartreuse, giving a reproducible preference curve rather than arbitrary or uniform liking across hues.
  > "a broad peak at blue and a narrow trough at chartreuse"
- **[supporting]** Saturated colors are preferred over light and muted versions, with hue-specific exceptions (dark orange/yellow reduce preference; dark red/green increase it) — supporting that saturation and lightness modulate preference, consistent with the broader claim that non-hue dimensions strongly shape color affect.
  > "Saturated colors were "preferred to the light and muted colors.""
- **[supporting]** Color preference is culture-dependent: American object-association WAVEs predict American preferences (r=0.89) better than Japanese preferences (r=0.74), so 'color X = emotion Y' claims do not transfer cleanly across cultures.
  > "American WAVEs predicted American preferences (r=0.89) better than Japanese preferences (r=0.74)"

#### Source `afdefe53` — quality: **primary** · published: 2011 (Attention, Perception, & Psychophysics, 73(2):551-571; PMID 21264737)
- **[central]** Color-pair preference and color-pair harmony are distinct constructs: both rise with hue similarity, but preference depends more on the preference for the individual component colors and on lightness contrast, whereas harmony does not. This undercuts the assumption that 'harmony rules' alone govern which color combinations people like.
  > "pair preference and harmony both increase as hue similarity increases, but preference relies more strongly on component color preference and lightness contrast"
- **[central]** High hue contrast within a pair is generally judged neither preferable nor harmonious, BUT when colors are in a figure-ground relationship, preference for the figural color increases as its hue contrast with the background increases — figure-ground contrast and pure pair-harmony pull in opposite directions.
  > "Although pairs with highly contrastive hues are generally judged to be neither preferable nor harmonious, figural color preference ratings increase as hue contrast with the background increases"
- **[supporting]** Preference for a color combination is partly inherited from how much people already like each component color in isolation — individual color preference is a measurable driver of combination preference, separate from harmony.
  > "pair-level judgments depend heavily on "component color preference""

### Practical interface — navigation, information architecture, usability, layout

#### Source `a0606e74` — quality: **primary** · published: 2026-05-11 (page last updated; WCAG 2.2 Recommendation originally 2023-10-05)
- **[central]** WCAG 2.2 Success Criterion 2.5.8 (Level AA) sets the minimum pointer-target size at 24 by 24 CSS pixels.
  > "The size of the target for pointer inputs is at least 24 by 24 CSS pixels, except when:"
- **[central]** Targets smaller than 24x24 px can still pass via the spacing exception if a 24 CSS pixel diameter circle centered on each target's bounding box does not intersect another target (or another undersized target's circle).
  > "Undersized targets positioned so a 24 CSS pixel diameter circle centered on each target's bounding box does not intersect another target or another undersized target's circle."
- **[supporting]** SC 2.5.8 defines five exemptions to the 24px rule: spacing, equivalent (another control on the page meets it), inline (within a sentence / line-height constrained), user-agent control (browser default unmodified by author), and essential (presentation required, e.g. dense map pins).
  > "User Agent Control: Target size determined by the user agent and not modified by the author (e.g., browser defaults). ... Essential: Target presentation is essential or legally required for information conveyed (e.g., digital maps with necessary pin density)."
- **[supporting]** The rationale for the requirement is motor-accessibility: it targets users with hand tremors, spasticity, and quadriplegia, and the spacing exception works because touch devices use heuristics to pick the nearest control to a touch.
  > "Touchscreen devices and user agents generally have internal heuristics to identify which link or control is closest to a user's touch interaction."

#### Source `a06b8b66` — quality: **primary** · published: 2021-02-05
- **[central]** Visual attention during search is steered by five sources of preattentive guidance: top-down feature goals, bottom-up salience, prior history/priming, reward/value, and scene syntax/semantics — meaning where the eye lands on a page is a combination of designed salience AND the viewer's goals, expectations, and learned page structure, not salience alone.
  > "Top-down guidance represents the implicit or explicit goals of the searcher... Salience is based on coarse representations of a limited number of basic features like color, size, etc... [guidance also comes from] passive exposure to some sequence of stimuli (e.g. priming effects)... If you reward one feature (e.g. red) and/or punish another (e.g. green), items with rewarded features will attract more attention... Guidance from the structure and meaning of scenes."
- **[central]** Bottom-up salience that pre-attentively pulls the eye is computed from only a small, limited set of basic features — color, size, orientation, and motion — so only those few visual attributes can reliably draw attention before the viewer reads anything. This is the actionable basis for steering the eye with color/size/motion contrast.
  > "Salience is based on coarse representations of a limited number of basic features like color, size, etc."
- **[supporting]** Attention is deployed to the most active location in a spatial 'priority map' and items are selected serially at roughly 20 times per second (~20 Hz, varying with task difficulty) — so the layout element with the highest combined priority is sampled first.
  > "Attention will be deployed to the most active location in the map [and] Items are selected by attention at a rate of ~20 Hz, though this will vary with task difficulty."
- **[supporting]** Processing is foveally biased: an item near the current fixation is represented more richly than one further away, which constrains how much of a page is taken in per fixation (the functional visual field).
  > "An item near fixation will be more richly represented than one further away."
- **[supporting]** Search for a distinctive real-world object among a varied set of other objects is essentially unguided — guidance operates on simple features, not on arbitrary complex targets — which explains why visual hierarchy must be built from simple feature contrasts and why complex content can be effectively 'invisible' (relevant to banner blindness).
  > "Search for a distinctive real-world object in a varied set of other objects seems to be essentially unguided."

#### Source `a0e214b3` — quality: **primary** · published: 1990-04 (CHI '90 Proceedings, April 1990)
- **[central]** In four controlled experiments, individual evaluators performing heuristic evaluation found only 20-51% of the known usability problems in an interface (per-experiment averages: 51%, 38%, 26%, 20%), so a single evaluator misses roughly half to four-fifths of problems.
  > "Four experiments showed that individual evaluators were mostly quite bad at doing such heuristic evaluations and that they only found between 20 and 51% of the usability problems in the interfaces they evaluated."
- **[central]** Aggregating the independent reports of about five evaluators finds roughly two-thirds of an interface's usability problems, dramatically more than any single evaluator, because evaluators find largely non-overlapping problem sets (the data does not form a cumulative Guttman scale).
  > "In general, we would expect aggregates of five evaluators to find about two thirds of the usability problems which is really quite good for an informal and inexpensive technique like heuristic evaluation."
- **[central]** The authors recommend using between three and five evaluators for a heuristic evaluation; the number of problems found rises steeply from one to five evaluators and hits diminishing returns around ten, so resources beyond five are better spent on other evaluation methods.
  > "We recommend that heuristic evaluation is done with between three and five evaluators and that any additional resources are spent on alternative methods of evaluation."
- **[supporting]** The heuristics are expert-derived 'obvious' principles distilled from teaching/consulting experience, not empirically validated rules; despite seeming obvious they were hard to apply in practice, supporting the distinction between expert-heuristic and empirically-backed claims.
  > "We have developed this specific list of heuristics during several years of experience with te.aching and consulting about usability engineering [Nielsen and Molich 19891. The nine heuristics can be presented in a single lecture and explain a very large proportion of the problems one observes in user interface designs."
- **[supporting]** The original (1990) heuristic set had NINE principles, not the later famous ten: simple and natural dialogue, speak the user's language, minimize user memory load, be consistent, provide feedback, provide clearly marked exits, provide shortcuts, good error messages, and prevent errors.
  > "Simple and natural dialogue / Speak the user's language / Minimize user memory load / Be consistent / Provide feedback / Provide clearly marked exits / Provide shortcuts / Good error messages / Prevent errors. Table 1. Nine usability heuristics"

#### Source `a0f5e9ba` — quality: **primary** · published: 2007-08-19
- **[central]** Eyetracking across hundreds of pages shows users almost never fixate on anything that looks like an advertisement (banner blindness), so banner-styled elements are reliably skipped regardless of whether they contain ads.
  > "Users almost never look at anything that looks like an advertisement."
- **[central]** Banner blindness is learned and location-based: users have trained themselves to ignore the right-hand rail where ads typically sit, which means placing important content in the right column risks it being overlooked.
  > "people have learned not to look at the right rail"
- **[supporting]** Putting important (non-ad) content in the right-hand column is a layout mistake because of learned ad-avoidance behavior.
  > "putting important content in the right-hand column may be a bad idea"
- **[supporting]** A few visual characteristics can occasionally draw eyes even within ad-like regions: plain text, faces, and body-focused imagery — i.e., the cues that make banners look more like native content.
  > "Three design elements occasionally attract fixations in ads: Plain text, Faces, Body-focused imagery"
- **[tangential]** Banner avoidance persists across all levels of reading engagement, from quick scanning to thorough reading, rather than only when users skim.
  > "avoidance occurs "at all levels of user engagement" (quick scanning, partial reading, thorough reading)"

#### Source `a1f91fe0` — quality: **primary** · published: 2019-08-25
- **[central]** Eyetracking research identifies four distinct text-scanning patterns, ranked worst-to-best for effectiveness: F-pattern, spotted, layer-cake, and commitment.
  > "listed below in increasing order, worst to best, of effectiveness"
- **[central]** In the F-pattern, users fixate most on the beginning of lines and the top of the page, so left/top content in left-to-right languages is read more than right/bottom content.
  > "In left-to-right languages, text on the left and towards the top of the page is read more than text on the right or towards the bottom."
- **[central]** The layer-cake pattern shows fixations concentrated on headings and subheadings with few fixations on body text between them, and users only read body copy once a relevant heading is found — making meaningful subheadings a key scannability lever.
  > "Fixations placed mostly on the page's headings and subheadings"
- **[supporting]** The spotted pattern occurs when users fixate on words that visually stand out (styled differently) or match their task keywords, supporting the use of special visual styling for keywords.
  > "visually stand out in the text because they are styled differently"
- **[supporting]** To support scanning, designers should chunk content into sections and bulleted lists, use meaningful subheadings, and apply special visual styling to keywords.
  > "support them by chunking your content into sections and bulleted lists, by using meaningful subheadings, and by special visual styling for keywords"

#### Source `a21d5b66` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2015-10-25
- **[central]** Navigation must signal not just where a user can go but where they currently are; orientation cues are needed because users frequently land on arbitrary deep pages from search engines (wayfinding: 'where am I').
  > "Navigation should not only show where you can go but also where you are now."
- **[central]** Active-state / current-location indication should be conveyed by giving the active navigation section visual prominence through highlighting or spacing (the 'navigation change' technique).
  > "Navigation change - Visual prominence for active sections via highlighting or spacing"
- **[central]** Breadcrumbs serve as a wayfinding aid by exposing the site's information hierarchy as a linked path, helping users understand location and navigate back up the structure.
  > "Breadcrumbs - "The information hierarchy is exposed in a linked path""
- **[supporting]** There are roughly eight complementary 'you-are-here' mechanisms designers can deploy together: logo/branding (top-left), active-nav highlighting, headings, page titles, human-readable URLs, breadcrumbs, contextual cues (dates/tags/icons), and step/progress indicators.
  > "The article catalogs eight primary "you-are-here" techniques: 1. Logo/branding ... 2. Navigation change ... 3. Headings ... 4. Page titles ... 5. URLs ... 6. Breadcrumbs ... 7. Contextual cues ... 8. Steps"
- **[supporting]** Designers systematically over估计 the clarity of location cues because their strong mental model makes them blind to what users miss — a testable claim validated by showing participants navigation-stripped pages and asking 'Where are you on the website? How can you tell?'
  > "a signal that may seem distractingly obvious to the designer is often not even noticed by the visitor."

#### Source `a24f7b51` — quality: **primary** · published: 
- **[central]** Material Design 3 defines five responsive breakpoints (window size classes) by width in dp: Compact <600, Medium 600-839, Expanded 840-1199, Large 1200-1599, Extra-large 1600+ — designers should target these breakpoints rather than specific devices.
  > "Compact | Under 600dp ... Medium | 600–839dp ... Expanded | 840–1199dp ... Large | 1200–1599dp ... Extra-large | 1600dp+ ... "Breakpoints ensure layouts work across a wide range of devices.""
- **[central]** M3 prescribes pane count by breakpoint: one pane for Compact and Medium, two panes for Expanded and Large, three panes for Extra-large.
  > "Compact & Medium: "A single pane works best" - Expanded & Large: "Two panes are recommended" - Extra-large: "Consider using three panes""
- **[central]** M3 recommends swapping navigation components by window size: navigation bar in compact layouts becomes a navigation rail in medium/expanded layouts, with the swapped components being functionally equivalent.
  > ""Swap a navigation bar in a compact layout for a navigation rail in a medium or expanded layout" ... "The interchangeable components are functionally equivalent""
- **[supporting]** M3 gives a concrete measure (line-length) target of 40-60 characters per line for readable text when resizing layouts across breakpoints.
  > "Resize: Scale cards, feeds, lists, and panes; maintain "40–60 characters per line" for text readability"
- **[supporting]** M3 frames responsive adaptation as five methods applied at breakpoints: reveal, divide, resize, reposition, and swap.
  > "1. Reveal ... 2. Divide ... 3. Resize ... 4. Reposition ... 5. Swap: Exchange functionally equivalent components (e.g., navigation bar to navigation rail)"

#### Source `a2769c97` — quality: **primary** · published: 2026-05-11
- **[central]** WCAG Success Criterion 2.5.5 (Target Size) requires that the target for pointer inputs be at least 44 by 44 CSS pixels, and it is a Level AAA criterion.
  > "The size of the target for pointer inputs is at least 44 by 44 CSS pixels"
- **[supporting]** The 44x44 requirement has four defined exceptions: an equivalent >=44px control exists elsewhere on the page, the target is inline within a sentence/block of text, the target size is set by the user agent and not modified by the author, or a particular presentation is essential.
  > "The target is available through an equivalent link or control on the same page that is at least 44 by 44 CSS pixels"
- **[central]** The rationale for the minimum size is motor-control accessibility for users with hand tremors or limited dexterity, plus the fact that a finger is larger than a mouse pointer and obscures the activation point.
  > "A finger is larger than a mouse pointer, and generally obstructs the user's view"
- **[supporting]** Beyond the minimum, larger targets are recommended specifically when a control is used frequently, when its result cannot be easily undone, when it sits at a screen edge, or when it is part of a sequential task, to reduce unintentional actions.
  > "larger sizes are used to reduce the possibility of unintentional actions"
- **[tangential]** This normative document grounds the 44px figure in motor-impairment and touch rationale rather than in HCI research; it makes no explicit reference to Fitts's law and does not specify a minimum spacing between adjacent targets.
  > "No explicit reference to Fitts's law or specific research citations appear in this document."

#### Source `a2f53516` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2014-09-07
- **[central]** Search alone is insufficient for findability; navigation and search are complementary and a site needs both (the 'synergy' thesis). This is presented as NN/g expert best-practice, not from a controlled study in this article.
  > "users search; not only that, but they actually love the search box"
- **[central]** Search requires users to already know what they are looking for and to understand the search space, which is a precondition that browse/navigation does not impose.
  > "In order to formulate a good search query users need to know fairly well what they are searching for"
- **[supporting]** Navigation lowers cognitive load relative to search because it relies on recognition rather than recall.
  > "replaces the recall with recognition"
- **[supporting]** Search has a high interaction cost because typing is slow and error-prone, an argument for offering browse/navigation paths alongside search.
  > "Typing has high interaction cost; it is error prone and time consuming"
- **[tangential]** On-site search frequently returns irrelevant results, especially on specialized sites, undermining a search-only strategy.
  > "Chances are that the results returned will often be irrelevant"

#### Source `a3d00fae` — quality: **secondary** · published: Wikipedia article (continuously edited); cites Nielsen heuristics final set published 2005, original Nielsen/Molich work 1989-1990
- **[central]** Nielsen's '10 usability heuristics' are expert-derived inspection heuristics (not controlled-study laws): the canonical set is Visibility of system status; Match between system and the real world; User control and freedom; Consistency and standards; Error prevention; Recognition rather than recall; Flexibility and efficiency of use; Aesthetic and minimalist design; Help users recognize, diagnose, and recover from errors; Help and documentation.
  > "1. Visibility of system status 2. Match between system and the real world ... 10. Help and documentation — Final set published in 2005; earlier versions appeared in 1989-1990 papers by Nielsen and Molich."
- **[central]** Heuristic evaluation is normally run with 3-5 evaluators, and adding evaluators past about five yields diminishing returns rather than proportionally more problems found.
  > "According to Nielsen, three to five evaluators are recommended within a study. Beyond five evaluators, insights don't proportionally increase."
- **[central]** A single evaluator is unreliable for heuristic evaluation; aggregating findings across multiple evaluators is required because individual reviewers catch only a fraction of problems.
  > "The original Nielsen/Molich report found individual evaluators were "mostly quite bad" at heuristic evaluations, suggesting multiple evaluators with aggregated results were necessary."
- **[supporting]** Heuristic evaluation produces soft, evaluator-dependent results rather than hard empirical data, making its output sensitive to the skill of the experts used.
  > "Results are highly influenced by the knowledge of the expert reviewer(s) ... Results lack hard data; depend on subjective observations"
- **[supporting]** Heuristic (expert) evaluation and empirical user/performance testing surface different sets of usability problems, so inspection methods do not substitute for user testing.
  > "Results are "one-sided" and "repeatedly has different results than software performance testing," with each uncovering different problem sets."

#### Source `a437d68c` — quality: **primary** · published: 2003
- **[central]** The widely-held '5 users is enough' assumption is unreliable: in Faulkner's controlled resampling of 60 real test users, different random sets of 5 participants ranged from catching 99% of usability problems down to only 55% — so a single 5-user test can miss nearly half the problems. This is the empirical counterweight to Nielsen/Landauer's discount-usability rule.
  > "Some of the randomly selected sets of 5 participants found 99% of the problems; other sets found only 55%."
- **[central]** Increasing sample size raises the worst-case (minimum) problem-detection floor: with 10 users the lowest any set detected rose to 80%, and with 20 users to 95%. This gives concrete numbers for the marginal value of testing beyond 5 users.
  > "With 10 users, the lowest percentage of problems revealed by any one set was increased to 80%, and with 20 users, to 95%."
- **[supporting]** The evidence base is a Monte Carlo resampling study: 60 users were actually tested and random subsets of 5+ were drawn from the full pool to estimate detection-rate variability — a stronger empirical method than the small single-sample studies underlying the original 5-user claim.
  > "In this study, 60 users were tested and random sets of 5 or more were sampled from the whole, to demonstrate the risks of using only 5 participants and the benefits of using more."
- **[supporting]** Across the resampled sets, 5-user tests detected on average about 85% of usability problems (with the wide 55-99% spread), consistent with Nielsen's ~85% average claim but exposing that the average hides large downside risk on any individual test.
  > "tests with 5 users revealed an average of 85% of usability problems, though percentages ranged from nearly 100% down to only 55%"

#### Source `a4a21201` — quality: **primary** · published: No explicit date on page; Material Design 2 (m2.material.io) legacy guideline, content circa 2018-2021 (archive snapshot 2022)
- **[central]** Material Design's normative 8pt spacing system: all components align to an 8dp square baseline grid across mobile, tablet, and desktop (the established design-system convention for spacing, not empirically derived).
  > "All components align to an 8dp square baseline grid for mobile, tablet, and desktop."
- **[central]** Touch targets should be at least 48x48 dp with at least 8dp of clear space between adjacent targets, framed as balancing information density against usability.
  > "touch targets should be at least 48 x 48 dp with at least 8dp of space between targets."
- **[supporting]** Smaller elements (icons and type) may align to a finer 4dp grid rather than the 8dp grid, i.e. the system uses a 4dp sub-increment for typography and iconography.
  > "Icons, type, and some elements within components can align to a 4dp grid."
- **[supporting]** Padding (space between elements within a component) is measured only in increments of 8dp or 4dp, and need not span the full layout height.
  > "Padding is an alternative spacing method to keylines and is measured in increments of 8dp or 4dp."
- **[supporting]** Component heights (e.g., app bars, lists) should align to the 8dp grid, while widths stay responsive to viewport; concrete spec heights given are status bar 24dp, app bar 56dp, list item 88dp.
  > "The heights of these elements should align to the 8dp grid. Their widths are not specified because it’s responsive to a viewport width."

#### Source `a56ae877` — quality: **primary** · published: 2016-06-26
- **[central]** In a controlled study (179 participants, 6 sites, desktop + mobile), hidden navigation (e.g., hamburger menus) reduced content discoverability by more than 20% compared to visible or combo navigation.
  > "More than 20% drop in discoverability on sites with hidden navigation, compared with sites with visible or combo navigation"
- **[central]** On desktop, hidden navigation made task completion at least 39% slower; on mobile it was 15% slower than visible navigation.
  > "People were at least 39% slower when the navigation was hidden ... Mobile: 15% slower when the navigation was hidden"
- **[central]** Hidden navigation was used far less than visible/combo navigation: 27% vs 48-50% of cases on desktop, and 57% vs 86% on mobile.
  > "Desktop hidden navigation used in 27% of cases vs. 48-50% for visible/combo ... Mobile hidden navigation used in 57% vs. 86% for combo navigation"
- **[supporting]** Hidden navigation increased perceived task difficulty by 21% versus visible navigation and 11% versus combo navigation.
  > "21% increase in difficulty ratings for hidden vs. visible navigation; 11% increase compared to combo navigation"
- **[supporting]** NN/G recommends avoiding hidden navigation (hamburger icons) on desktop, and on mobile displaying visible links when there are 4 or fewer top-level items, hiding navigation only when more than 4 items are necessary.
  > "Desktop: Do not use hidden navigation (such as hamburger icons) ... Mobile: Display visible links if 4 or fewer top-level items; hide navigation only if more than 4 items necessary"

#### Source `a6fbafe0` — quality: **primary** · published: 2017-11-12
- **[central]** The F-shaped scanning pattern (two horizontal sweeps across the top, then a vertical scan down the left edge) is confirmed by eye-tracking on both desktop and mobile; it is the default scan when text is unformatted and users are scanning efficiently with low commitment.
  > "The F-pattern is the default pattern when there are no strong cues to attract the eyes towards meaningful information."
- **[central]** Position on the page biases attention: earlier lines and the leftmost words of each line get disproportionately more fixations, so content placed lower or to the right is more likely to be skipped.
  > "First lines of text on a page receive more gazes than subsequent lines of text"
- **[central]** Leftmost words receive more fixations than later words on the same line, which is why information-bearing words should be front-loaded in headings and links.
  > "First few words on the left of each line receive more fixations than subsequent words"
- **[central]** F-pattern scanning is a symptom of weak visual hierarchy rather than a target to design for; adding strong cues (prominent headings, bold key phrases, bullets, front-loaded keywords, trimmed content) redirects the eye away from the inefficient F.
  > "Users may skip important content simply because it appears on the right side of the page."
- **[supporting]** The F-pattern is not the only scan pattern; eye-tracking identifies additional patterns including the layer-cake (eyes fixate on headings and subheadings while skipping body text), spotted, marking, bypassing, and commitment patterns.
  > "The article identifies five additional patterns: layer-cake (headings only), spotted (selective scanning), marking (fixed gaze during scrolling), bypassing (skipping repeated line starters), and commitment (reading entire sections when highly engaged)."

#### Source `a70646d7` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2019-02-03 (updated 2024-12-12)
- **[central]** Inline validation (showing field-level validation immediately after the user completes a field) is the recommended default for form error reporting, letting users fix errors immediately without returning to a field they thought was complete.
  > "Inline validation...allows them to fix errors immediately, without searching or returning to a field they thought was completed correctly."
- **[central]** Error messages should be positioned directly next to (below or beside) the problematic field, and be explicit, human-readable, polite, precise, and give constructive advice.
  > "Messages should be "explicit, human-readable, polite, precise, and give constructive advice" so users see guidance while correcting errors."
- **[central]** Validation should not fire before input is complete; errors should not appear until the user exits the field, because premature validation creates frustration before users finish typing.
  > "Avoid showing errors until users exit a field. Premature validation creates frustration before users finish typing."
- **[supporting]** Color should encode error state (red for errors, orange/yellow for warnings, green/blue for success), and a semitransparent background color on the error field improves visibility on long forms; icons placed left of the message support colorblind users.
  > "Apply red for errors, orange/yellow for warnings, green/blue for success. Add semitransparent background color to error fields to enhance visibility across lengthy forms."
- **[supporting]** A validation summary at the top of a form should never be the sole error indicator, and tooltips should not be used to report errors, because both force users to search for or discover hard-to-notice messages.
  > "Summaries at form tops shouldn't be the sole error signal—they force users to search and memorize messages. Always pair with field-level indicators."

#### Source `a712961e` — quality: **secondary** · published: 1993-01-01
- **[central]** 0.1 second is the threshold below which users feel the system is reacting instantaneously; no special feedback beyond showing the result is needed (matches the Google RAIL ~100ms tap-response guideline).
  > "0.1 second is about the limit for having the user feel that the system is reacting instantaneously, meaning that no special feedback is necessary except to display the result."
- **[central]** 1.0 second is the threshold for keeping a user's flow of thought uninterrupted; delays from 0.1s to 1.0s need no special feedback but the user notices the delay and loses the sense of directly manipulating the data.
  > "1.0 second is about the limit for the user's flow of thought to stay uninterrupted, even though the user will notice the delay. Normally, no special feedback is necessary during delays of more than 0.1 but less than 1.0 second, but the user does lose the feeling of operating directly on the data."
- **[central]** 10 seconds is the threshold for keeping a user's attention on the task; beyond it users switch to other tasks, so the system must show feedback such as a progress indicator with an estimated completion time.
  > "10 seconds is about the limit for keeping the user's attention focused on the dialogue. For longer delays, users will want to perform other tasks while waiting for the computer to finish, so they should be given feedback indicating when the computer expects to be done."
- **[supporting]** These three response-time limits are long-established and stable, derived from prior empirical HCI research rather than newly invented, citing Miller 1968 and Card et al. 1991.
  > "The basic advice regarding response times has been about the same for thirty years [Miller 1968; Card et al. 1991]."

#### Source `a822ac82` — quality: **primary** · published: 1996
- **[central]** In a controlled experiment, increasing the depth and/or breadth of a hierarchical menu significantly increased reaction time, error rates, and perceived complexity — establishing that deeper hierarchies impose measurable performance costs.
  > "As depth and/or breadth increased, reaction time, error rates, and perceived complexity all increased significantly."
- **[central]** The study empirically compared six menu structures defined as breadth^depth — 2^2, 2^3, 2^6, 8^2, 8^3, and 8^6 — measuring response time and accuracy, so it directly tests the breadth-vs-depth trade-off in information architecture.
  > "The experiment tested six structures (2^2, 2^3, 2^6, 8^2, 8^3, and 8^6) for reaction time, error rates, and subjective preference."
- **[supporting]** Depth specifically drove perceived complexity: as menu depth increased, users' perceived complexity of the menu increased significantly, supporting a preference for broader, shallower hierarchies.
  > "As depth of a computerized, hierarchical menu increased, perceived complexity of the menu increased significantly."
- **[supporting]** The proposed cognitive mechanism for the depth penalty was short-term memory load, with deeper hierarchies introducing multiple paths, multiple outcomes, conflicting interdependence among paths, and uncertain linkages.
  > "The cognitive component influencing users' perceptions of task complexity was short-term memory load... four characteristics (multiple paths, multiple outcomes, conflicting interdependence among paths, and uncertain linkages) are present as depth increases."
- **[supporting]** The empirical evidence base is small: only 12 subjects participated, with response time and accuracy as dependent variables and menu depth and breadth as independent variables — a limited sample that tempers how strongly the effect sizes generalize.
  > "12 subjects were asked to use six different hierarchical menus of varying breadth and depth, with the dependent variables being response time and accuracy."

#### Source `a87cb7d1` — quality: **primary** · published: 2024-01-09
- **[central]** Inline form validation should validate most fields on blur (when the user leaves the field), not on each keystroke; validating mid-entry produces premature 'this is wrong' errors before the user has finished typing.
  > "Validate on blur events for most fields—check validity when users leave a field rather than during typing ... One participant stated: "Why are you telling me my email address is wrong, I haven't had a chance to fill it all out yet!""
- **[central]** Error messages should be cleared at the keystroke level the moment input becomes valid, rather than waiting for the next blur event; persistent errors that don't clear on correction cause users to over-scrutinize the feedback.
  > "Remove errors on keystroke level once input becomes valid; don't wait for blur events"
- **[central]** Validating only on form submission (no inline validation) causes significant delays and occasional form abandonment; catching errors early while input is fresh benefits users substantially.
  > "Error messages upon submission cause "significant delays" and occasional abandonment ... Users benefit substantially when errors are caught early, while input remains fresh in memory"
- **[supporting]** Positive validation feedback (checkmarks / green indicators confirming correct entries) reduces cognitive load; absence of it forces users to manually re-verify the form before submission.
  > "Sites failing to show checkmarks or confirmations for correct entries increase cognitive load, forcing users to manually verify form accuracy before submission."
- **[supporting]** 31% of sites lack any inline validation and 4% implement it incorrectly, indicating inline validation is both underused and frequently mis-built across e-commerce sites tested.
  > "31% of sites lack any inline validation, forcing users to discover errors only upon form submission ... 4% of sites implement inline validation incorrectly"

#### Source `a89b3c6d` — quality: **primary** · published: 1992 (CHI '92 — Posters and Short Talks of the 1992 SIGCHI Conference on Human Factors in Computing Systems; ACM DOI 10.1145/1125021.1125117)
- **[central]** Severity ratings from a single usability evaluator are unreliable, but the mean severity rating from four evaluators lands within half a point of the true severity 95% of the time — i.e., reliable severity estimates require aggregating multiple raters, not one expert.
  > "Ratings from single evaluators are very unreliable when usability specialists judge the severity of usability problems found by heuristic evaluation, but the mean severity rating from four evaluators gets within half a rating point of the true severity 95% of the time."
- **[supporting]** Evaluators agree that the usability problems surfaced by heuristic evaluation are genuine problems, even though any single evaluator had originally found only a small fraction of them — supporting the 'pool several evaluators' rationale of discount usability.
  > "The evaluators do agree that usability problems found by heuristic evaluation are all real problems even though each rater had originally only identified a small proportion of the problems."
- **[tangential]** Across case studies of six user interfaces, heuristic evaluation identified 59 major and 152 minor usability problems, giving the empirical base for the severity-rating reliability analysis.
  > "In case studies of six user interfaces (Nielsen 1992), heuristic evaluation identified a total of 59 major usability problems and 152 minor usability problems."

#### Source `a8a2eb9c` — quality: **primary** · published: 2005
- **[supporting]** In a controlled experiment, 20 college-age participants read online news articles set at one of four line lengths — 35, 55, 75, or 95 characters per line (cpl) — on a computer monitor, with reading speed, comprehension, and satisfaction measured.
  > "Twenty college-age students read news articles displayed in 35, 55, 75, or 95 characters per line (cpl) from a computer monitor."
- **[central]** The widest line length tested (95 cpl) produced the FASTEST reading speed — i.e., longer lines read faster, which runs counter to the popular 45–75 cpl 'optimal measure' convention for reading speed.
  > "Results showed that passages formatted with 95 cpl resulted in faster reading speed."
- **[central]** Overall satisfaction did not differ significantly by line length, but readers preferred the EXTREME line lengths (the shortest and longest) rather than a mid-range measure — so speed-optimal and preference-optimal line lengths can diverge.
  > "Overall satisfaction was not affected by line length; however, users indicated a strong preference for the extreme line lengths."
- **[tangential]** Comprehension was assessed across six question types, and higher-level 'structure' questions were harder than factual/lower-level questions overall — a difficulty driven by question type rather than by line length.
  > "Structure questions were found to be more challenging overall when compared to factual and other lower-level questions."

#### Source `aa3ce67d` — quality: **secondary** · published: 1993-04-24
- **[central]** The proportion of usability problems discovered by testing n users follows the model N(1-(1-L)^n), where L (the probability that a single test user finds the average usability problem) averages 31% across many studied projects — i.e. one user surfaces roughly a third of all problems.
  > "The mathematical model is presented as: N(1-(1-L)^n) ... "The typical value of _L_ is 31%, averaged across a large number of projects we studied.""
- **[central]** Because of the diminishing-returns curve, about 5 test users uncover roughly 85% of a design's usability problems — the empirical basis for 'discount usability' / small-sample testing.
  > "5 users discover approximately 85% of usability problems"
- **[supporting]** Finding essentially all usability problems in a design requires about 15 users, far more than the 5 that capture most problems — showing the steep cost of chasing the long tail.
  > "15 users are needed "to discover all the usability problems in the design""
- **[supporting]** Cost-benefit analysis favors several small iterative tests over one large test: running 3 studies of 5 users each beats a single 15-user study, because a fixed planning/start-up cost is better amortized across multiple separate rounds.
  > "Rather than one large 15-user study, Nielsen recommends "3 studies with 5 users each" to enable iterative design improvements. ... "There is always a fixed initial cost associated with planning and running a study: it is better to depreciate this start-up cost across the findings from multiple users.""

#### Source `ab45005b` — quality: **primary** · published: 2001-03-31
- **[central]** Spool & Schroeder (2001) is a peer-reviewed CHI Extended Abstracts paper that directly challenges Nielsen & Landauer's 'five users is enough' rule, arguing five users is far from sufficient to uncover the usability problems on real websites. This is a key [MYTH]-flagging counterpoint to the discount-usability '5 users finds ~85%' claim relevant to topic (2).
  > "Testing web sites: five users is nowhere near enough"
- **[central]** In their study (e-commerce music/CD sites, open-ended browsing tasks across multiple sites), serious usability problems kept being discovered even after dozens of users — the cumulative discovery curve did not plateau near five participants; secondary reporting puts the first-five-users coverage at only ~35% of problems with new problems still appearing through all ~18 participants on a site. This empirically contradicts the '5 users = 85% of problems' rule for large, complex, open-task sites.
  > "Jared Spool & Will Schroeder show that serious problems were still being discovered even after dozens of users (disagreeing with Virzi's but agreeing with Lewis's findings)."
- **[supporting]** The mechanism behind the divergence is methodological: open-ended, free-browsing tasks on large sites drive the per-user problem-discovery rate (p) far below the ~0.31 that the Nielsen/Landauer model assumes, so the geometric '85% with 5 users' projection breaks down. This tells a practical skill that the '5 users' shortcut only holds for small, focused, structured-task tests — not sprawling sites or open exploration.
  > "Unlike most studies, these authors used open-ended tasks allowing users to freely browse up to four websites looking for unique CD's."

#### Source `ab47010b` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2016-05-01
- **[central]** Forms compliant with usability guidelines achieved a 78% one-try (first-attempt) submission success rate versus 42% for non-compliant forms in a controlled CHI study (Seckler et al. 2014).
  > "CHI study by Seckler et al. (2014) showing 78% one-try submissions with guideline-compliant forms vs. 42% with non-compliant forms"
- **[central]** Labels should be placed immediately above the field for mobile and shorter desktop forms, and beside the field only for extremely long desktop forms.
  > "Labels should be close to the fields they describe (immediately above the field for mobile and shorter desktop forms, or next to the field for extremely long desktop forms)"
- **[central]** Forms should use a single-column layout because multiple columns disrupt the user's vertical reading flow down the form (logically related short fields like City/State/Zip may share a row as an exception).
  > "Multiple columns interrupt the vertical momentum of moving down the form"
- **[central]** Error messages must preserve the user's erroneous input and give a specific explanation of the problem, signaling errors through multiple cues (outlining the field, red text, heavier font).
  > "Erroneous input should be preserved so users can correct it, and accompanied by a specific explanation of the problem"
- **[supporting]** Placeholder text should be avoided in form fields because it causes usability problems.
  > "Placeholder text causes many usability problems and is best avoided"
- **[supporting]** Cancel-type buttons (and Reset/Clear buttons generally avoided) should have significantly less visual prominence than the Submit button to prevent accidental data loss.
  > "significantly less visual prominence than the Submit button"

#### Source `ad1dcf85` — quality: **primary** · published: 2004
- **[central]** Fitts' law in HCI is modeled as a linear regression of movement time on index of difficulty, MT = a + b × ID, with the Shannon formulation ID = log₂(2D/W), so movement time increases with target distance D and decreases with target width W.
  > "MT = a + b × ID ... ID = log₂(2D/W)"
- **[central]** Larger targets and shorter distances reduce movement time, motivating concrete layout choices such as placing frequently-used targets near the pointer or enlarging targets for longer movements.
  > "keeping frequently-used targets close to the tracker...or increasing the target-icon size for longer movements"
- **[supporting]** When pointing performance is normalized for accuracy via effective target width, the spread of endpoints is set so the target width equals 4.133 times the standard deviation of movement endpoints, corresponding to a ~4% error rate (about 96% of hits landing inside the target).
  > "the maximum entropy is 4.133 times the standard deviation of the movement end-point positions ... Thus 4.133σ represents a Normal distribution where approximately 4% of the observations occur in the tails."
- **[supporting]** Throughput (an information-theoretic speed-accuracy index in bits/second) for the mouse is consistent across standardized ISO 9241-9 studies, falling in the narrow range of about 3.7–4.9 bps, versus a much wider 2.55–12.5 bps in pre-standard literature.
  > "All of the throughputs fall in the range [3.7 - 4.9]"
- **[tangential]** For valid Fitts'-law experiments the authors recommend spanning index-of-difficulty values from 2 to 8 bits with many repetitions per condition (roughly 15 to 25).
  > "A range of ID values from 2 to 8 bits should suffice for most situations. ... Each condition must be presented to each subject many (perhaps, 15 to 25) times."

#### Source `ae9944c0` — quality: **primary** · published: 2001-04-01
- **[central]** This is a controlled reading experiment (Dyson & Haselgrove, Int. J. Human-Computer Studies 54(4):585-612, April 2001; 200+ citations) that crossed three on-screen line lengths (~25, 55, and 100 characters per line) with two reading speeds (normal and fast). Its central finding is that a MEDIUM line length (~55 cpl) supports the most effective reading at both normal and fast speeds. This is [STRONG] controlled evidence backing a ~50-60 character measure optimum for the practical skill. NOTE: ScienceDirect full text was paywalled/403; quote is from the indexed abstract summary (Semantic Scholar TLDR) and corroborated by Crossref/OpenAlex metadata.
  > "a medium line length appears to support effective reading at normal and fast speeds"
- **[central]** Comprehension was higher at the medium line length than at the long one: documents at 55 characters per line yielded better comprehension scores than the longest 100-cpl lines. This is direct empirical support for keeping measure short rather than letting text run the full width of a wide screen, and concretely places the optimum near ~55 cpl (within the conventional 45-75 char range). Quote sourced from a search-engine synthesis of the paper's results (full text paywalled), consistent with the abstract's 'medium line length' conclusion.
  > "documents at 55 characters per line produced better comprehension scores than the longest line length (100 characters per line)"
- **[supporting]** Lines that are too SHORT also hurt: reading was slower at the shortest line length than at the medium one (a ~4-inch line was read faster than a ~1.7-inch line). This establishes line length as a U-shaped tradeoff (both too-short and too-long are worse than medium), not 'shorter is always safer.' Quote is from humanfactors.com explicitly attributing this result to Dyson & Haselgrove (2001), since the source full text was inaccessible.
  > "a 4-inch line length resulted in faster reading than the shorter line length of 1.7 inches"
- **[tangential]** Reading at a fast (skim) rate reduced overall comprehension, but did NOT change which structural level of information was recalled (the type/importance level of recalled content was independent of speed). This is tangential to layout geometry but relevant to interaction/scanning claims: speeding up reading trades total recall but not what kinds of points stick.
  > "A reduction in overall comprehension is found when reading fast, but the type of information recalled was not dependent on speed"

#### Source `af44e852` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2018-12-23
- **[central]** Breadcrumbs are a wayfinding aid that show the user's current location within the site's hierarchy (the page plus its ancestors), and are warranted when a site is more than 2 levels deep but unnecessary for flat 1-2 level or linear sites.
  > "Breadcrumbs are necessary for sites with hierarchies deeper than 2 levels, but not necessary for "Sites with flat hierarchies that are only 1 or 2 levels deep, or sites that are linear in structure""
- **[central]** Breadcrumbs should be placed at the top of the page just below the global navigation, and supplement rather than replace primary navigation.
  > "Display "at the top of the page, usually just below the global navigation"; should "supplement other navigational components" and not replace primary navigation"
- **[supporting]** The current (last) page in the breadcrumb trail should not be a clickable link, while ancestor items should be visually differentiated as links (e.g. underlined or blue text), with > as the standard separator.
  > "Current page should appear last but "should not be a link"; Visually differentiate linked items "preferably using underlined or blue text"; Separator: Use ">" character (though "/" is acceptable)"
- **[supporting]** Breadcrumbs reflect site hierarchy (location-based), not the user's browsing history during the session — that distinction is what differentiates them from the browser back button.
  > "breadcrumbs "show the hierarchical structure of the site" not "the history of pages traversed during a session" like a back button"
- **[supporting]** On mobile, breadcrumbs must not wrap to multiple lines, tap targets need a minimum of ~1cm x 1cm, and the trail should be shortened to only the last level(s).
  > ""Don't use breadcrumbs that wrap to multiple lines"; Avoid links "too small or too crowded together" (tap targets need minimum 1cm × 1cm); "Consider shortening the breadcrumb trail to include only the last level(s)" on mobile"

#### Source `af639a08` — quality: **blog** · published: 2019-06-08 (updated 2024-07-24)
- **[central]** WCAG 2.5.5 Target Size (Enhanced) is a Level AAA criterion requiring pointer targets of at least 44×44 CSS pixels (about a 0.9372-degree visual angle), with exceptions for equivalent controls, inline/text-flow controls, unstyled default browser controls, and cases where a smaller size is essential.
  > "44 × 44 CSS pixels ... equates to a visual angle of about 0.9372 degrees. Exceptions: Duplicate/equivalent control at minimum size; Control within text flow; Default browser control without styling; Smaller size essential to convey information accurately"
- **[supporting]** A fixed 44 CSS pixel target maps to different physical sizes across devices: a 44px square is 8.25mm (68mm²) on an iPad but only 6.75mm (45mm²) on an iPad Mini, so CSS-pixel rules do not guarantee a consistent physical touch size.
  > "A 44 pixel square on an iPad comes in at 8.25 millimeters (left), while on an iPad Mini comes in at 6.75 millimeters (middle)."
- **[central]** Platform target-size guidance clusters near 44-48px: Apple iOS 44×44pt, Android/Material 48×48dp, Microsoft Fluent 40×40 effective px, BBC GEL 44px (32px min) / 7mm, and Nielsen Norman ~1cm (0.4in).
  > "Apple iOS | 44 × 44 | points ... Android | 48 × 48 | device pixels ... Microsoft Fluent | 40 × 40 | effective pixels (epx) ... BBC GEL | 44 (min: 32) | pixels / 7mm ... Nielsen Norman | 1cm (0.4 inches)"
- **[central]** 44 pixels is a defensible practical minimum target size because that value, or one near it, recurs across expert recommendations, standards, and platform guidelines.
  > "44 pixels is probably a good minimum, given that it or a value near it is consistently recommended across experts, standards, and platforms."
- **[supporting]** Because 2.5.5 is Level AAA, it is effectively ignored by organizations targeting the more common AA conformance level, where the weaker 2.5.8 Target Size (Minimum) applies instead.
  > "organizations targeting AA compliance (essentially all of them) are likely to ignore it"

#### Source `af83189c` — quality: **primary** · published: 2006-09-12
- **[central]** For one-handed thumb use on small touchscreen handhelds, controlled study recommends minimum target size of 9.2 mm for single-target (discrete) tasks like buttons/checkboxes and 9.6 mm for multi-target (serial) tasks like numeric/text entry.
  > "we recommend that target sizes should be at least 9.2 mm for single-target tasks and 9.6 mm for multi-target tasks in order to keep the dimensions of the targets as small as possible without decreasing performance and user preference."
- **[central]** Error rate stops improving above a threshold: there was no statistically significant reduction in error rate for target sizes >= 9.6 mm in discrete tasks and >= 7.7 mm in serial tasks (n=20, RM-ANOVA, Bonferroni-corrected post-hoc).
  > "there were no significant differences in error rate between target sizes ≥ 9.6 mm in discrete tasks and targets ≥ 7.7 mm in serial tasks"
- **[supporting]** Speed (task time) and accuracy decouple at larger sizes: tap speed kept improving significantly as discrete targets grew from 9.6 mm to 11.5 mm, but error rate did not improve over that range, so accuracy plateaus before speed does.
  > "So while speed improves significantly as targets grow from 9.6 mm to 11.5 mm, error rate does not."
- **[supporting]** Because thumb taps drifted toward the screen edge on the dominant-hand side (right-leaning hits for right-handers), targets on that side should be extended flush to the screen edge to exploit the edge as an effective boundary.
  > "we recommend that targets on the right side of the screen for right-handed users (and left for left-handed people) should extend all the way to the edge"
- **[tangential]** Spacing between adjacent touch targets did not need to be added for performance: the study used 0 mm edge-to-edge spacing between targets and distractors, citing prior evidence that performance is unaffected by key spacing (Colle & Hiszem 2004).
  > "Because of limited screen space and evidence that performance is unaffected by key spacing (e.g., [3]), we used 0 mm edge-to-edge spacing between targets and distractors."

#### Source `afaa6afd` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2017-03-26
- **[central]** Mega menus improve navigation over traditional dropdown menus by making all options visible at once without scrolling, supporting visual grouping/hierarchy, and accommodating rich content (icons/images) that aids selection.
  > "everything visible at once — no scrolling"
