- **[supporting]** Hover-activated mega menus should require the pointer to stay stationary for 0.5 seconds before opening (to avoid accidental triggering during mouse traversal), then appear within 0.1 seconds.
  > "the mouse should remain stationary for 0.5 seconds"
- **[supporting]** Mega-menu groupings should be derived from card-sorting studies, organized into related sets with concise, information-rich labels rather than arbitrary or overly fragmented groups.
  > "Organize options into related sets discovered through card-sorting studies, using "related sets" and avoiding overly large or fragmented groups."
- **[supporting]** Mega menus create accessibility problems for low-vision users using screen magnifiers, who can only see part of the menu; top-level items should therefore link to accessible HTML pages or use accessible scripting.
  > "Low-vision users with screen magnifiers see only partial menus."

---

## Part B — Adversarial verifications (225)

### UX evidence — designing interfaces (fluency, first impressions, scanning, a11y)

#### V1 `a020fa81` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The layer-cake pattern, in which users fixate mostly on headings and subheadings while skipping body text, is the most effective way for users to scan pages (short of reading every word)."

**Source:** https://www.nngroup.com/articles/text-scanning-patterns-eyetracking/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "by far the most effective way in which users can scan pages"

*Evidence:* Claim is faithfully supported by the cited primary source verbatim. NN/g (Pernice, "Text Scanning Patterns: Eyetracking Evidence," 2019) states: "Aside from reading almost every word, the layer-cake pattern is by far the most effective way in which users can scan pages." The claim's qualifier "(short of reading every word)" exactly matches "Aside from reading almost every word," so it is not an overreach or dropped-caveat misread. Source quality is sufficient — NN/g is the authoritative primary source on web eye-tracking and scanning patterns; not outdated (foundational, 2019); not marketing. No credible contradicting source found; the companion NN/g article ("Scanning Patterns on the Web Are Optimized for the Current Task") adds task-dependence nuance but does not dispute the ranking for information-seeking. Honest caveat (does not refute): "most effective" is NN/g's qualitative characterization from observational gaze plots/heatmaps, not a randomized controlled head-to-head trial, so it merits a [STRONG]-leaning/[CONVENTION] label rather than a hard RCT-grade [STRONG] — but the claim as written restates the source accurately without asserting RCT backing.
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source. Partial qualifier: NN/g "Scanning Patterns on the Web Are Optimized for the Current Task" (https://www.nngroup.com/articles/eyetracking-tasks-efficient-scanning/) notes scanning effectiveness is task-dependent, but this qualifies rather than refutes.

#### V2 `a027927f` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The earliest empirical HCI validation (Landauer & Nachbar 1985, touch-menu selection) found selection time fit a logarithmic (Hick/Fitts) function, and because of that logarithmic relation the number of alternatives per screen does not affect total selection time — only the number of screens does — so broad, shallow menu trees beat narrow, deep ones."

**Source:** https://web.ics.purdue.edu/~dws/pubs/ProctorSchneider_2018_QJEP.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Landauer and Nachbar concluded, "Broader, shallower menu trees yield faster search than narrower, deeper ones" (p. 76)."

*Evidence:* The claim's load-bearing clause — "because of the logarithmic relation the number of alternatives per screen does not affect total selection time, only the number of screens does" — fails on two independent grounds.

(1) UNSUPPORTED BY QUOTE. The cited Proctor & Schneider (2018) quote says only "Broader, shallower menu trees yield faster search than narrower, deeper ones (p. 76)." It contains no assertion that alternatives-per-screen has zero effect on total time. The mechanistic clause is grafted on, not in the source — an overreach.

(2) EMPIRICALLY CONTRADICTED. Total acquisition time is a U-shaped function of breadth with a minimum near 8 items/level, not flat. Snowberry, Parkinson & Sisson (1983) varied depth 1-6 and breadth 2-64 and found the minimum at 2 levels x 8 items; the broadest config (1 level x 64 items, "CAT64") was SLOWER than 8x2. If alternatives-per-screen had no effect on total time the curve would be flat in breadth; instead it has a pronounced minimum. Per-panel time is itself a logarithmic (non-zero) function of breadth (visual-search literature). The math agrees: with N=B^D fixed, total time ≈ a·ln(N)/ln(B) + b·log2(N) — the first term changes with breadth B, so breadth affects total time rather than leaving it invariant. The legitimate kernel ("breadth's effect is sub-linear/logarithmic, so depth's fixed per-screen overhead dominates") was mangled into "breadth has no effect," a category error.

(3) The conclusion clause is also overstated as monotonic: the optimum is intermediate (~8 wide x 2 deep), and excessively broad menus are worse, contradicting an unqualified "broad-shallow always beats narrow-deep."

What IS accurate: Landauer & Nachbar (1985, CHI '85) did model selection as Hick-Hyman + Fitts (both logarithmic) and did favor breadth over depth within their range. But the specific mechanistic and absolute wording in the claim is both unsupported by the quote and contradicted by the breadth-depth empirical record.
*Counter-source:* Snowberry, Parkinson & Sisson (1983), "Computer display menus," Ergonomics — total acquisition time U-shaped in breadth, minimum at 2 levels x 8 items, broadest (64x1) slower than 8x2; Miller (1981) SAGE; in-vehicle depth/breadth modelling (ScienceDirect S0141938213000462). These show alternatives-per-screen strongly affects total selection time, refuting the claim's invariance clause.

#### V3 `a049a13c` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Color must never be the sole carrier of information; redundant non-color encoding (shapes/icons) is required because color-blind users have difficulty with red-green and blue-orange pairings. This reinforces the data-viz direct-labeling / redundant-encoding principle."

**Source:** https://developer.apple.com/design/human-interface-guidelines/accessibility (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Convey information with more than color alone. Some people have trouble differentiating between certain colors and shades. For example, people who are color blind may have particular difficulty with pairings such as red-green and blue-orange. Offer visual indicators, like distinct shapes or icons, in addition to color"

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by primary normative sources and faithful to the quote. (1) WCAG 2.x Success Criterion 1.4.1 "Use of Color" (Level A, W3C/WAI normative): "Color is not used as the only visual means of conveying information, indicating an action, prompting a response, or distinguishing a visual element" — this makes "must never be sole carrier / redundant encoding required" a normative requirement, not overreach. (2) Apple HIG quote directly states convey info with more than color alone and offer distinct shapes/icons; claim maps onto it faithfully, including the red-green / blue-orange pairing wording. (3) CVD prevalence (~1 in 12 men, 1 in 200 women) confirms the rationale. (4) Synergy with data-viz redundant-encoding/direct-labeling is valid and consistent (ColorBrewer CVD-safe palettes, direct labeling). No credible contradiction found. Only nuance, non-refuting: research on which redundant channel is most effective is "mixed but generally positive" and task-dependent (shape less robust than color as categories grow; CatPAW arXiv 2602.06792), and Apple's "blue-orange" is looser than the standard red-green-common / blue-yellow-rare framing — but neither weakens the core requirement. Sources: https://www.w3.org/WAI/WCAG21/Understanding/use-of-color.html ; https://developer.apple.com/design/human-interface-guidelines/accessibility
*Counter-source:* No credible refuting source. Closest qualifier: CatPAW (arXiv 2602.06792) shows redundant shape benefit is task- and category-count-dependent — an optimization caveat, not a refutation of the no-color-alone requirement.

#### V4 `a04b5118` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Color must never be the sole carrier of information; redundant non-color encoding (shapes/icons) is required because color-blind users have difficulty with red-green and blue-orange pairings. This reinforces the data-viz direct-labeling / redundant-encoding principle."

**Source:** https://developer.apple.com/design/human-interface-guidelines/accessibility (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Convey information with more than color alone. Some people have trouble differentiating between certain colors and shades. For example, people who are color blind may have particular difficulty with pairings such as red-green and blue-orange. Offer visual indicators, like distinct shapes or icons, in addition to color"

*Evidence:* The claim faithfully restates the cited Apple HIG quote (which literally says "Convey information with more than color alone," names "red-green and blue-orange" pairings, and prescribes "distinct shapes or icons, in addition to color") AND is independently corroborated by a stronger normative source: W3C WCAG 2.x Success Criterion 1.4.1 "Use of Color" (Level A), which requires that "color is not used as the only visual means of conveying information, indicating an action, prompting a response, or distinguishing a visual element." Two independent normative standards converge, so the strong "must never" phrasing is justified and this qualifies as [STRONG], not mere [CONVENTION]. No contradicting evidence found in WebSearch — the only literature caveat is an efficiency tradeoff (redundant encoding consumes visual channels), which is a design-cost note, not a refutation. Not outdated (WCAG 1.4.1 stable since WCAG 2.0/2008, current through 2.1/2.2; Apple HIG current). Not marketing. Consistency with companion data-viz research holds: aligns with WCAG (required by consistency point a), does not touch the hue/luminance or blue-light myths, and genuinely reinforces the redundant-encoding/direct-labeling principle (a real synergy, confirmed by CVD-accessibility sources on color+shape encoding). Sources: https://developer.apple.com/design/human-interface-guidelines/accessibility ; https://www.w3.org/WAI/WCAG21/Understanding/use-of-color.html

#### V5 `a062065a` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The F-pattern is one of four scanning patterns and reflects that, in left-to-right languages, users read text on the left and near the top of a page more than text on the right or near the bottom."

**Source:** https://www.nngroup.com/articles/text-scanning-patterns-eyetracking/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "text on the left and towards the top of the page is read more than text on the right or towards the bottom"

*Evidence:* Both parts of the claim are confirmed by the cited primary source (NNG, Kara Pernice, "Text Scanning Patterns: Eyetracking Evidence," 2019). (1) The article explicitly frames the F-pattern as one of FOUR text-scanning patterns people use on the web: F-pattern, Spotted, Layer-cake, and Commitment — confirmed by direct WebFetch of the source AND an independent corroborating search ("there are 4 main patterns... F-pattern, spotted pattern, layer-cake pattern, and commitment pattern"). (2) The reading-priority statement is a near-verbatim paraphrase of the article's own sentence: "In left-to-right languages, text on the left and towards the top of the page is read more than text on the right or towards the bottom of the page." The supporting quote thus directly supports the claim; no overreach. Source quality (NNG primary eye-tracking research) matches the modest, convention-level strength of the claim, and the finding (left/top reading bias) is long-replicated and current (2019 article). The only quibble — broader NNG content sometimes lists 6 patterns (adding Marking and Bypassing) — does not refute the claim because it is faithful to the specific cited article, which headlines exactly 4, and the claim does not overstate the F-pattern as a universal reading law.

#### V6 `a0674a48` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "WCAG contrast is computed from luminance, not hue: color/hue is explicitly NOT a key factor in the contrast calculation, so meeting the requirement is independent of color perception."

**Source:** https://www.w3.org/WAI/WCAG21/Understanding/contrast-minimum.html (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "contrast is calculated in such a way that color (hue) is not a key factor ... Requiring good luminance contrast accommodates this by requiring contrast that is independent of color perception"

*Evidence:* Quote verified verbatim on the primary source (W3C WCAG 2.1 Understanding 1.4.3): "contrast is calculated in such a way that color (hue) is not a key factor" and "Requiring good luminance contrast accommodates this by requiring contrast that is independent of color perception." The page confirms the formula (L1+0.05)/(L2+0.05) operates on relative luminance only; the WCAG 2.x relative-luminance definition (0.2126R+0.7152G+0.0722B) contains no hue term, so two equal-luminance colors of differing hue score 1:1 (fail) — confirming "hue is not a key factor" is factually accurate, not an overreach. The strongest counter-literature (APCA / WCAG 3 critique, Somers) argues luminance-only is a poor perceptual model (ignores hue, saturation, polarity, font weight, dark-mode halation) — but this CONFIRMS the claim's factual core (WCAG 2.x is luminance-based, hue-excluded) and only calls it a limitation; it does not dispute the calculation is hue-independent. Source is primary and normative (W3C); WCAG 2.2 (2023, current Recommendation) leaves the contrast formula unchanged, so the claim is current; APCA/WCAG 3 is still a non-normative draft. One qualification: the claim describes the WCAG calculation/requirement, not a perceptual law — real perceived readability DOES depend on hue/saturation/polarity (the reason APCA exists). As scoped, the claim is faithful to the source and reinforces the companion data-viz finding that luminance, not hue, is the operative contrast variable.
*Counter-source:* https://git.apcacontrast.com/documentation/APCAeasyIntro.html (APCA critique of WCAG 2.x — confirms luminance-based/hue-excluded mechanism while arguing it is a perceptually imperfect model; does not refute the claim's factual core)

#### V7 `a06e1b8a` — **upheld** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "The layer-cake scanning pattern is more efficient than the F-pattern: it reliably leads users to the information they seek (if present), whereas the F-pattern causes users to inadvertently miss meaningful information without realizing it."

**Source:** https://www.nngroup.com/articles/layer-cake-pattern-scanning/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "aside from reading every (or almost every) word, the layer-cake pattern is by far the most effective way to scan pages: most of the time, it ensures that users will find the information they are looking for (if it is present on the page)"

*Evidence:* The claim accurately represents its primary source (NN/g, Kara Pernice, "The Layer-Cake Pattern of Scanning Content on the Web," Aug 4 2019). The article's comparison table rates F-pattern efficiency "Low" and layer-cake "High," states layer-cake is "by far the most effective way to scan pages," that it "can guide users to the right place on a page," and that under the F-pattern users "inadvertently miss meaningful information" — near-verbatim matches to both parts of the claim. The claim's "reliably... if present" closely tracks the source's "most of the time... if it is present on the page" (minor strengthening, within bounds). Web search found NO credible source contradicting this; NN/g's own follow-up framing (F-pattern is a "failure state" of unstructured pages; layer-cake emerges from good headings) reinforces rather than refutes it. Source is the gold-standard primary source for web scanning patterns; claim is current (slow-moving field) and not marketing. CAVEAT lowering confidence to medium: evidence is observational eye-tracking with thin reported statistics (e.g., "9 participants," no head-to-head controlled comparison), and the pattern distinction is confounded with page-formatting quality (layer-cake is largely a consequence of descriptive headings, not an independent user strategy). This warrants a [CONVENTION]/medium label rather than [STRONG], but does not refute the claim.
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source found. Nuance (F-pattern as formatting-dependent "failure state") comes from NN/g's own corpus: https://www.nngroup.com/articles/text-scanning-patterns-eyetracking/ — and it supports, not refutes, the claim.

#### V8 `a07a4306` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The F-pattern is a fallback scanning behavior that emerges when content lacks formatting cues, not a desirable reading mode; it causes users to miss important content."

**Source:** https://www.nngroup.com/articles/f-shaped-pattern-reading-web-content/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The F-pattern is the default pattern when there are no strong cues to attract the eyes towards meaningful information."

*Evidence:* All three parts of the claim are directly stated in the cited primary source (Kara Pernice, NNGroup, 2017). (1) Fallback when formatting absent: the article says F-pattern occurs when a page "has a 'wall of text' but no bolding, bullets, or subheadings," and is "the default pattern when there are no strong cues to attract the eyes toward meaningful information." (2) Not desirable: "the F-shaped scanning pattern is bad for users and businesses." (3) Misses content: "they miss big chunks of content... The skipped phrases and words are often as important — or even more important — as those words that are read." The supporting quote is faithful, not an overreach. Adversarial check: the strongest skeptic (EyeQuant, which reports no evidence of an F-shape bias) challenges a DIFFERENT proposition — that F is the dominant/universal reading mode — and therefore aligns with, rather than refutes, this claim's framing of F as a mere conditional fallback. NNGroup's 2020 "Text Scanning Patterns" follow-up reinforces it by documenting four patterns (F, spotted, layer-cake, commitment), with better-formatted content yielding the preferable layer-cake. Source is the canonical primary research, current (2017, reaffirmed 2020, cited through 2024), and matches the claim's strength. No valid refutation found.
*Counter-source:* EyeQuant (https://www.eyequant.com/resources/eye-tracking-studies-does-the-famous-f-shape-pattern-really-exist) disputes whether an F-shape bias exists at all, but this targets the "F is universal/dominant" claim, not the "F is an undesirable fallback" claim under review — so it does not refute it.

#### V9 `a0896c5e` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled experiment that manipulated aesthetics and usability as separate (independent) variables, aesthetics had NO significant effect on participants' judgments of usability; the significant effects came from manipulated usability and from occasion (repeated use), not aesthetics. This directly qualifies/contradicts the strong form of the aesthetic-usability effect (Kurosu & Kashimura 1995; Tractinsky 1997)."

**Source:** https://uxpajournal.org/boundary-conditions-aesthetics-usability/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Results failed to show the hypothesized effect of aesthetics on participants' judgments of usability, and analyses showed a significant effect of occasion and manipulated usability, rather than aesthetics, on participants' judgments of usability."

*Evidence:* Primary source verified: Grishin & Gillan, "Exploring the Boundary Conditions of the Effect of Aesthetics on Perceived Usability," Journal of Usability Studies (UXPA), Feb 2019. Methodology matches the claim exactly — usability and aesthetics were INDEPENDENTLY manipulated in a 2x2 design (four website versions), with an "occasion" within-subject factor (four repeated sessions). Article body (confirmed via independent search): "The results failed to show an effect of manipulation of interface aesthetics on participants' judgments of usability. Instead, the results showed a significant effect of occasion and manipulation of interface usability on participants' judgments of usability." This is near-verbatim with the claim's supporting quote and supports every element: aesthetics manipulation had no significant effect; occasion + manipulated usability did. Convergent corroboration: Tuch et al. (2012) "Is beautiful really usable?" (Computers in Human Behavior), also an independent 2x2 manipulation with pre/post measurement, found that before use aesthetics did not affect perceived usability and the relation reversed after use — so the 2019 null is not an outlier. Source quality is sufficient (peer-reviewed journal; Gillan is an established HCI researcher). Not outdated (2019, slow-moving field). Not marketing. The claim is appropriately hedged ("qualifies/contradicts the STRONG form"), not an overreach — it does not claim the whole aesthetic-usability effect is debunked. One nuance worth carrying into the skill, not a refutation: the classic Kurosu/Tractinsky effect concerns PRE-use first impressions (still supported by NN/g and Tuch before-use data), whereas this study measured perceived usability AFTER task performance — which is precisely the boundary condition, making "qualifies" more precise than "contradicts."
*Counter-source:* No refuting source found. Note for nuance: the aesthetic-usability effect remains supported in the PRE-use/first-impression context (Nielsen Norman Group; Tuch et al. 2012 before-use measurement), which is why the 2019 study is best read as identifying a boundary condition (post-use measurement) rather than a wholesale contradiction — consistent with the claim's "qualifies" framing.

#### V11 `a140e372` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "About 1.0 second is the limit for keeping a user's flow of thought uninterrupted; the user notices the delay but does not lose their sense of operating directly on the data."

**Source:** https://www.nngroup.com/articles/response-times-3-important-limits/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "1.0 second is about the limit for the user's flow of thought to stay uninterrupted, even though the user will notice the delay"

*Evidence:* The claim says at the 1.0s limit "the user notices the delay but does NOT lose their sense of operating directly on the data." The primary source (Nielsen, nngroup.com, "Response Times: The 3 Important Limits," drawn from Usability Engineering 1993, citing Miller 1968 and Card et al. 1991) states the OPPOSITE: during delays of 0.1–1.0s "the user does LOSE the feeling of operating directly on the data." The "operating directly on the data" sensation is tied to the 0.1s threshold (instantaneous reaction), not 1.0s. The supplied supporting quote only covers the first half ("flow of thought to stay uninterrupted... user will notice the delay") and does NOT support the appended second clause. The claim conflates two thresholds and inverts the source on a checkable point. First half accurate; second half contradicted by source = overreach/misread.
*Counter-source:* https://www.nngroup.com/articles/response-times-3-important-limits/ (Nielsen) — verbatim: "the user does lose the feeling of operating directly on the data"

**Supporting quote:** "The F-pattern is the default pattern when there are no strong cues to attract the eyes towards meaningful information."

#### V13 `a2487ccf` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Hick's law is not invariant: the slope depends on stimulus-response compatibility and can be driven to zero. In highly compatible tasks (e.g., responding to the finger that vibrates, saccading toward a stimulus) set size has no effect, so the slope of the RT-vs-information function ranges from zero up to several hundred milliseconds. This contradicts the popular UI framing that adding choices reliably and uniformly slows users."

**Source:** https://web.ics.purdue.edu/~dws/pubs/ProctorSchneider_2018_QJEP.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "the slope of the function can vary between zero and several hundreds of milliseconds."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by the cited primary source and convergent literature. Proctor & Schneider (2018, Quarterly Journal of Experimental Psychology — peer-reviewed review) explicitly covers stimulus-response compatibility effects on the Hick's-law slope. Leonard (1959), the exact 'vibrating finger' example in the claim, used vibrotactile stimuli to the respective fingers and found NO difference in RT across 2/4/8 choices — a flat (zero-slope) function. Saccades toward a stimulus likewise violate Hick's law (high S-R compatibility; RT does not rise with set size). The magnitude range is corroborated: the review reports RT ~500 ms longer at 8 vs 2 choices in session 1, falling to ~300 ms by session 5 — i.e. the function spans ~zero to several hundred ms depending on compatibility/practice. The UX-framing flag (popular claim that more choices 'reliably and uniformly' slow users) is a fair and accurate contrast with the conditional reality in the research. Source quality matches claim strength: an authoritative 2018 peer-reviewed review by a leading S-R-compatibility researcher; not marketing, not outdated, not contradicted. Caveats (do not refute): I could not byte-verify the verbatim quote string from the PDF (no pdftotext/pdfminer/pip available in this environment), and Leonard's zero-slope result was 'qualified' by Ten Hoopen et al. (1982) — but the core proposition (slope is not invariant and approaches zero under high compatibility, cleanly for saccades) is robustly established.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. Searches surfaced only corroboration: Leonard (1959) flat vibrotactile-finger RT function; saccade studies violating Hick's law; ScienceDirect (2023) pigeons failing to show predicted Hick's increase. Only nuance is Ten Hoopen, Akerboom & Raaymakers (1982) qualifying Leonard's specific finding — a qualification, not a refutation.

#### V14 `a269bd2b` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "About 1.0 second is the limit for keeping a user's flow of thought uninterrupted; the user notices the delay but does not lose their sense of operating directly on the data."

**Source:** https://www.nngroup.com/articles/response-times-3-important-limits/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "1.0 second is about the limit for the user's flow of thought to stay uninterrupted, even though the user will notice the delay"

*Evidence:* The claim contradicts its own primary source on a load-bearing clause. The 0.1-second limit is where "the user feels the system is reacting instantaneously" and is "directly manipulating" / operating directly on the data. The 1.0-second limit is where "the user's flow of thought stays uninterrupted, even though the user will notice the delay" — and the very next sentence in the source states "the user DOES LOSE the feeling of operating directly on the data." The claim asserts the user "does NOT lose their sense of operating directly on the data" at 1.0s — the exact inverse of the source, and a misattribution of the 0.1-second property to the 1.0-second limit. WebFetch of the source confirmed verbatim: at 1.0s "the user does lose the feeling of operating directly on the data." The supporting quote was truncated right before that contradicting sentence, which is where the misread originated. The first half of the claim (1.0s = flow uninterrupted, delay noticed) is accurate; the appended "directly on the data" clause is refuted.
*Counter-source:* https://www.nngroup.com/articles/response-times-3-important-limits/ (same primary source; verbatim: at 1.0s "the user does lose the feeling of operating directly on the data" — the "operating directly" feeling belongs to the 0.1s limit)

#### V16 `a2eb2d3a` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled experiment that manipulated aesthetics and usability as separate (independent) variables, aesthetics had NO significant effect on participants' judgments of usability; the significant effects came from manipulated usability and from occasion (repeated use), not aesthetics. This directly qualifies/contradicts the strong form of the aesthetic-usability effect (Kurosu & Kashimura 1995; Tractinsky 1997)."

**Source:** https://uxpajournal.org/boundary-conditions-aesthetics-usability/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Results failed to show the hypothesized effect of aesthetics on participants' judgments of usability, and analyses showed a significant effect of occasion and manipulated usability, rather than aesthetics, on participants' judgments of usability."

*Evidence:* Claim verified against the primary source and independent mirrors. The study is Grishin & Gillan (2019), "Exploring the Boundary Conditions of the Effect of Aesthetics on Perceived Usability," Journal of Usability Studies 14(2):76-104 (JUS = the UXPA/uxpajournal.org journal in the cited URL; the bare URL 404s now but the article is live at the /-suffixed path and indexed on ACM DL 10.5555/3532689.3532692, Semantic Scholar, ResearchGate 332351066, and the NCSU thesis repository). Three points confirm the claim: (1) Design — a website was built and usability and aesthetics were manipulated as separate independent variables in a 2x2 (Higher/Lower Aesthetics x Higher/Lower Usability); the paper's stated motivation is the "shortage of experiments that have manipulated aesthetics and usability as separate variables." (2) Results — "no effect of aesthetics on usability," with a "significant effect of occasion" (repeated experience affected usability perception but not aesthetic perception) and manipulated usability driving usability judgments; usability and aesthetics were perceived separately with no joint effect. (3) The claim is properly hedged as qualifying/contradicting the STRONG FORM of the aesthetic-usability effect (Kurosu & Kashimura 1995; Tractinsky 1997), which is correct — the original effect rests on correlational/perceived-usability data, whereas this is a causal-manipulation null, i.e., a legitimate boundary condition, not a wholesale refutation. Source quality matches the hedged strength (peer-reviewed, established HCI author Douglas J. Gillan/NCSU, 2019, a null result not subject to benchmark cherry-picking). I could not extract the exact verbatim quote string from the saved PDF (no pdftotext; compressed object streams), but the quote's substance is corroborated by multiple independent abstract summaries. Note for synthesis: present this as ONE controlled study's null result that bounds the effect, not as overturning the broadly replicated aesthetic-usability phenomenon.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found; broader literature (Tractinsky, Tuch et al.) supports an aesthetics-usability link generally, which is consistent with the claim's narrow "strong form" qualification rather than contradicting it.

So the claim is accurate about what the accessibility table documents, but a skill using it must keep the 44pt minimum HIT-TARGET guidance intact rather than treating 28pt as an acceptable touch target. The claim's own wording ("minimum CONTROL size," hedged "nuances") stays on the right side of this line.
*Counter-source:* Apple HIG Buttons page ("a button needs a hit region of at least 44x44 pt") and LogRocket "All accessible touch target sizes" — both keep 44pt as the minimum HIT target; this qualifies the claim's framing but confirms, not contradicts, the table values.

#### V21 `a3d0d2dd` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "About 10 seconds is the limit for keeping a user's attention focused on the interaction; beyond it users want to switch to other tasks while waiting."

**Source:** https://www.nngroup.com/articles/response-times-3-important-limits/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "10 seconds is about the limit for keeping the user's attention focused on the dialogue"

*Evidence:* The claim is a near-verbatim, faithful restatement of the primary source. NN/g (Jakob Nielsen) states: "10 seconds is about the limit for keeping the user's attention focused on the dialogue" and, for longer delays, "users will want to perform other tasks while waiting for the computer to finish, so they should be given feedback indicating when the computer expects to be done." Both halves of the claim (10s attention limit + wanting to switch tasks beyond it) are directly supported. Source quality is high: NN/g is the canonical primary usability source; the threshold derives from Miller, R.B. (1968) "Response time in man-computer conversational transactions" (AFIPS) and Card, Robertson & Mackinlay (1991) "The information visualizer" (CHI '91). No credible source contradicts the 10s attention-focus figure. CAVEAT (does not refute, but qualifies strength): this is a heuristic/convention, not a controlled-study finding. Miller himself called these thresholds "the best calculated guesses by the author" and urged real-world verification; commentators note there is "no clear empirical evidence supporting any of these magic numbers." Nah (2004), "A Study on Tolerable Waiting Time," measures a different metric (~2s tolerable page-load wait), so it does not contradict the 10s attention threshold. Net: the claim faithfully represents the source and is uncontradicted, so it survives; but for the skill it should carry a [CONVENTION] label (widely taught, soft empirical support), not [STRONG] (controlled studies), since the underlying number is an expert estimate rather than a lab-validated constant. The companion 0.1s/1s limits are better grounded than the 10s one.
*Counter-source:* Miller, R.B. (1968) acknowledged thresholds were "best calculated guesses"; Nah, F. (2004) "A Study on Tolerable Waiting Time" (Behaviour & Information Technology) found ~2s tolerable wait — a different metric, not a contradiction of the 10s attention figure.

*Evidence:* The scanning half of the claim is strongly supported by the primary source. The "first two paragraphs" rider is NOT in the exact quote, but it faithfully operationalizes NN/g's separately-documented "inverted pyramid" recommendation (front-load the conclusion; users skip ideas not caught by a paragraph's first few words). Two caveats, both qualifying rather than refuting: (1) the specific number "two" is convention, not a measured threshold; (2) the scanning generalization needs the boundary condition that highly motivated users do read fully and good formatting (subheads, bullets) alters the scan path — per NN/g's own 2017 layer-cake/commitment-pattern work. Recommend labeling: scanning behavior [STRONG]; "front-load important info / inverted pyramid / first ~two paragraphs" [CONVENTION].

#### V24 `a508dcd0` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "WCAG contrast is computed from luminance, not hue: color/hue is explicitly NOT a key factor in the contrast calculation, so meeting the requirement is independent of color perception."

**Source:** https://www.w3.org/WAI/WCAG21/Understanding/contrast-minimum.html (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "contrast is calculated in such a way that color (hue) is not a key factor ... Requiring good luminance contrast accommodates this by requiring contrast that is independent of color perception"

*Evidence:* Claim verified against the primary W3C source (Understanding WCAG 1.4.3, contrast-minimum.html). The document states verbatim: "contrast is calculated in such a way that color (hue) is not a key factor" and "Requiring good luminance contrast accommodates this by requiring contrast that is independent of color perception." The claim restates these near word-for-word, so it is supported by the quote with no overreach. The contrast-ratio formula (L1+0.05)/(L2+0.05) uses only relative luminance. Adversarial search for disputes surfaced the APCA critique literature, but that critique CONFIRMS the mechanism ("the WCAG formula treats all hues equally...the eye is less sensitive to blue than green or red") — it attacks the accuracy/sufficiency of a luminance-only model, not the fact that hue is excluded. No credible source asserts hue IS a key factor in WCAG 2 contrast. Source quality (primary W3C) matches the claim's strength; not outdated (WCAG 2.x remains operative; APCA/WCAG 3 is still draft). One minor, non-refuting nuance: relative luminance uses color-channel coefficients (0.2126R/0.7152G/0.0722B), so the formula is not literally color-free, but it collapses color into a single luminance scalar, removing hue as an independent variable — exactly what the W3C means by "independent of color perception." Consistent with the data-viz finding that contrast/eye-strain is luminance- not hue-driven.
*Counter-source:* APCA critique (e.g. accessibilitychecker.org APCA explainer; Datawrapper "more sophisticated color contrast check") confirms WCAG2 is luminance-only and treats hues equally — corroborates rather than refutes the claim.

#### V30 `a6276d6e` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Hick's law states choice reaction time is a logarithmic function of the number of equally likely alternatives: RT = a + b·log2(n), where a is the intercept and b the slope; it can also be written RT = a + bH where H is the average information/uncertainty in bits."

**Source:** https://web.ics.purdue.edu/~dws/pubs/ProctorSchneider_2018_QJEP.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The more widely accepted version of Hick's law for n equally likely alternatives is RT = a + b log 2n, in which a is the intercept and b the slope of the function relating RT to information. Log 2 has come to be used because of its link to binary digits (bits) of information... the equation for Hick's law is sometimes expressed in terms of the average information or uncertainty (H) in a series of trials, in the form RT = a + bH."

*Evidence:* The claim faithfully restates the cited primary source. Proctor & Schneider (2018, Quarterly Journal of Experimental Psychology, "Hick's law for choice reaction time: A review") explicitly state "RT = a + b log 2n, in which a is the intercept and b the slope of the function relating RT to information" and "the equation for Hick's law is sometimes expressed in terms of the average information or uncertainty (H)... in the form RT = a + bH" — exactly the claim. Independent corroboration: WebSearch confirms the standard form RT = a + b·log₂(N) with a=baseline/intercept and b=slope (time per bit of information), and the entropy form RT = a + bH with H in bits (Hyman 1953, derived from Shannon entropy H = log₂(N) for equally probable alternatives). Source quality is high: a peer-reviewed authoritative review by established human-factors researchers, matching a non-extraordinary, textbook-settled psychophysics claim (Hick 1952 / Hyman 1953). One nuance considered: Hick's ORIGINAL 1952 form was RT = b·log₂(n+1) (the "+1" for response/no-response uncertainty, no separate intercept), which Wikipedia still shows. But this does not refute the claim — Proctor & Schneider themselves call RT = a + b·log₂(n) "the more widely accepted version," and the claim correctly attributes it to that source rather than to Hick's 1952 original. Both forms legitimately coexist. Not outdated (2018 review of a settled 1950s law), not marketing/cherry-picked.
*Counter-source:* en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hick's_law shows the original Hick form T = b·log₂(n+1) with the "+1" term and no explicit intercept — a coexisting variant, but it does not contradict the claim, which is attributed to Proctor & Schneider's "more widely accepted version."

#### V32 `a7556c90` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The earliest empirical HCI validation (Landauer & Nachbar 1985, touch-menu selection) found selection time fit a logarithmic (Hick/Fitts) function, and because of that logarithmic relation the number of alternatives per screen does not affect total selection time — only the number of screens does — so broad, shallow menu trees beat narrow, deep ones."

**Source:** https://web.ics.purdue.edu/~dws/pubs/ProctorSchneider_2018_QJEP.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Landauer and Nachbar concluded, "Broader, shallower menu trees yield faster search than narrower, deeper ones" (p. 76)."

*Evidence:* The claim faithfully restates Proctor & Schneider (2018, QJEP) — I verified the source verbatim from the PDF: P&S do write that "a property of the logarithmic relation is that the difference in number of alternatives per screen has no effect on the overall time...the cost associated with number of screens is the only factor," and quote L&N's conclusion "Broader, shallower menu trees yield faster search than narrower, deeper ones" (p. 76). So the attribution is accurate. BUT as a STRONG, unconditional design rule the claim overreaches the evidence in two ways: (1) MECHANISM IS AN IDEALIZATION. "Alternatives per screen has no effect on total time" only holds for a zero-intercept pure-log function: total = depth × c·log(b) = [log(N)/log(b)]·c·log(b) = c·log(N), independent of breadth b. Real per-screen time has a fixed overhead, and — as P&S themselves note in the same passage — visual search "basically proceeds in a serial mode," i.e. LINEAR, so scanning more on-screen alternatives DOES add per-screen time. L&N tested only breadth 2/4/8/16 with N=4096; the "no breadth effect" property does not generalize to the wider menus real UIs use. (2) STRONG CONCLUSION CONTRADICTED BY LATER CONTROLLED STUDIES. Convergent research finds a MODERATE breadth/depth optimum beats the broadest-shallowest, not "broadest wins": Larson & Czerwinski (1998) found a medium 16×16 structure outperformed the broadest shallow web structure; Kiger (1984), Jacko & Salvendy (1996), and Zaphiris (2000/2001) replicate that extreme breadth incurs visual-search/satisficing costs and that ~16 top-level links over 2-3 levels is optimal. Taken to its logical end the claim implies a single 4096-wide screen ties a tree — absurd and refuted by every study showing extreme breadth hurts. So the directional finding ("broad/shallow generally beats narrow/deep") is real, but the claim's strong mechanistic version ("alternatives per screen do NOT affect total time, only screens do") is a [CONVENTION]-grade idealization presented as a [STRONG] law, which the skill should flag rather than adopt.
*Counter-source:* Larson & Czerwinski (1998), "Web Page Design: Implications of Memory, Structure and Scent for Information Retrieval," CHI '98 — moderate breadth (16×16) outperformed broadest-shallowest; summarized at humanfactors.com (Breadth vs. Depth) alongside Kiger (1984), Jacko & Salvendy (1996), Zaphiris (2000/2001) converging on a moderate-breadth optimum. Also Proctor & Schneider (2018, QJEP, p.1295) note visual search proceeds serially/linearly, undercutting the zero-effect-of-breadth idealization at realistic breadths.

#### V33 `a75966e2` — **upheld** · confidence: high

Verbatim result: "the aesthetics manipulation was effective in that the high aesthetics group provided higher ratings on two aesthetics scales... however, differences in aesthetics had no significant effect on usability as measured by the System Usability Scale (SUS)." Analyses showed "a significant effect of occasion and manipulated usability, rather than aesthetics, on participants' judgments of usability." Authors' own framing: "Findings support the idea that users make independent judgments of usability and aesthetics." Every element of the claim maps directly. Crucially, the claim is correctly scoped with "in this study" and does NOT overreach into denying the aesthetic-usability effect — it is presented as a boundary-condition counterexample, which is the study's literal title and purpose ("Exploring the Boundary Conditions..."). Source quality (peer-reviewed primary HCI study, 2019) matches the narrow, hedged claim strength.

#### V35 `a7ccccb8` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Users do not read web text word-by-word; they scan, so the most important information should appear in the first two paragraphs."

**Source:** https://www.nngroup.com/articles/f-shaped-pattern-reading-web-content-discovered/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Users won't read your text thoroughly in a word-by-word manner."

*Evidence:* The cited NN/g article (Nielsen, "F-Shaped Pattern For Reading Web Content," April 16, 2006; 232 users, thousands of pages) supports BOTH halves of the claim near-verbatim, not just the supplied quote. Scanning part: "Users won't read your text thoroughly in a word-by-word manner. Exhaustive reading is rare." Front-loading part: the article literally states "The first two paragraphs must state the most important information," noting users read more of the first paragraph than the second. So the "first two paragraphs" recommendation is the article's own wording, not an overreach. Backed by convergent primary research: Morkes & Nielsen 1997 (79% scan); the inverted-pyramid / "readership declines with each paragraph" finding; and the Eyetracking Web Usability book (Nielsen & Pernice, ~1.5M eye fixations). NN/g's 2017 update (Pernice, "F-Shaped Pattern... Misunderstood, But Still Relevant") qualifies the F-pattern but reinforces front-loading rather than contradicting it. No credible contradicting source found. Source quality (NN/g eye-tracking, the normative source for web reading) matches claim strength; finding is reaffirmed across 13+ years, not outdated. Caveat that does not refute: "first two paragraphs" is a heuristic boundary; readership actually declines continuously per paragraph rather than at a sharp cutoff.

Source confirmed: Grishin & Gillan, Journal of Usability Studies (UXPA), 2019; indexed in ACM DL (DOI 10.5555/3532689.3532692) and Semantic Scholar. Design confirmed: 2x2 within-subjects (Higher/Lower Aesthetics x Higher/Lower Usability website versions), tasks repeated over four occasions; analyses found a significant effect of occasion and manipulated usability — not aesthetics — on SUS judgments. The journal URL returned 404 to the fetch tool (bot block), but quote authenticity is established via two independent search reproductions plus ACM/Semantic Scholar indexing. Caveat for skill use: must be labeled a boundary condition (single counter-study), never as proof that beauty doesn't affect perceived usability — the claim's own scoping ("in this study") already respects this.
*Counter-source:* Tractinsky et al. 2000 (replicated aesthetic-usability effect, r≈0.6); 2023 CHI "Statistically Controlling for Processing Fluency Reduces the Aesthetic-Usability Effect" (r≈0.79 → .34) — these qualify/contextualize but do not contradict the scoped single-study claim

#### V39 `a8345030` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Hick's law is not invariant: the slope depends on stimulus-response compatibility and can be driven to zero. In highly compatible tasks (e.g., responding to the finger that vibrates, saccading toward a stimulus) set size has no effect, so the slope of the RT-vs-information function ranges from zero up to several hundred milliseconds. This contradicts the popular UI framing that adding choices reliably and uniformly slows users."

**Source:** https://web.ics.purdue.edu/~dws/pubs/ProctorSchneider_2018_QJEP.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "the slope of the function can vary between zero and several hundreds of milliseconds."

*Evidence:* Claim is fully supported by a top-tier primary source. Proctor & Schneider (2018), "Hick's law for choice reaction time: A review," Quarterly Journal of Experimental Psychology, states verbatim: "the slope of the function can vary between zero and several hundreds of milliseconds" — verified by direct fetch of the cited PDF. The review explicitly attributes this variation to stimulus-response compatibility, noting set size can have "little or no effect when compatibility is high." Both high-compatibility examples in the claim are independently corroborated: (1) Leonard (1959) — fingertips stimulated by 50 Hz vibration, respond by pressing with the stimulated finger; RT did not increase across 2/4/8 choices (the "respond to the finger that vibrates" case); (2) saccades — Kveraga et al., "Saccades operate in violation of Hick's Law," show zero or even negative slope with increasing set size. The claim does not overreach the quote; it reproduces both the upper/lower bounds and the compatibility mechanism. The UI interpretation ("contradicts the popular framing that adding choices reliably and uniformly slows users") is accurate and consistent with the CHI 2020 critique "How Relevant is Hick's Law for HCI?" Only minor caveat: Leonard's tactile zero-slope was not always replicated (some later studies found an increase), but this does not refute the core claim because (a) the variable/zero slope is stated directly in the authoritative review and (b) the saccade evidence is robust. Source is current (2018), peer-reviewed, authored by a recognized authority on S-R compatibility — quality matches claim strength.

Large-scale text and images of large-scale text have a contrast ratio of at least 3:1." It is Level AA (the 7:1/4.5:1 enhanced version is the separate AAA SC 1.4.6, which the claim correctly does NOT cite). Large-scale is defined as "at least 18 point or 14 point bold" — matching the claim verbatim. No overreach vs. the quote: every figure (4.5:1, 3:1, 18pt, 14pt bold, AA) is present in the normative spec. It is a normative standard (strongest source class), current (WCAG 2.1 operative; WCAG 2.2 carries 1.4.3 forward unchanged), and not marketing/speculation. Minor non-defeating nuances: full definition adds a CJK-equivalent clause and the SC has exemptions (logotypes, incidental/decorative, inactive components), but the claim's simplified statement of the thresholds is accurate and the claim does not deny those exemptions.

(2) Convergent evidence, not contradiction: Smashing Magazine (2024), NN/g's 2017 reassessment, and other UX sources agree the F-pattern is a fallback/symptom of poor formatting that leaves text unread, while layer-cake (enabled by clear headings) guides users to relevant content. NN/g is nonetheless the field's leading authority on web eye-tracking (it coined both the F-pattern and layer-cake concepts), so the source quality is appropriate for a CONVENTION-strength claim. Not outdated (F-pattern 2006, reaffirmed 2017; layer-cake 2004/2020), not marketing, not cherry-picked.

*Claim under review:* "Hick's law is not invariant: the slope depends on stimulus-response compatibility and can be driven to zero. This contradicts the popular UI framing that adding choices reliably and uniformly slows users."

**Source:** https://web.ics.purdue.edu/~dws/pubs/ProctorSchneider_2018_QJEP.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "the slope of the function can vary between zero and several hundreds of milliseconds."

*Evidence:* Verified the exact quote verbatim by extracting the PDF text of the primary source (Proctor & Schneider 2018, Quarterly Journal of Experimental Psychology, "Hick's law for choice reaction time: A review"). The source reads: "Teichner and Krebs' statement, along with the influence of S-R compatibility mentioned earlier, makes clear that the slope of the function can vary between zero and several hundreds of milliseconds." Every element of the claim is directly supported in the same paper: (1) slope can be zero under high compatibility — "Leonard (1959) provided an initial demonstration that an RT function that does not increase as set size is increased can be obtained when stimuli and responses are highly compatible. Using vibrotactile stimuli to the respective fingers, he found no difference in RTs between two, four and eight choices; that is, the function relating number of alternatives and RT was flat"; (2) the saccade example is directly cited — "Kveraga, K., Boucher, L., & Hughes, H. C. (2002). Saccades operate in violation of Hick's law. Experimental Brain Research, 146, 307–314." Source quality is excellent: peer-reviewed 2018 review by Robert Proctor, a leading authority on stimulus-response compatibility; underlying findings (Leonard 1959, Kveraga 2002) are classic and uncontested; not outdated, not marketing. The interpretive conclusion (contradicts the UI framing that more choices uniformly slow users) is further corroborated by Cockburn et al., "How Relevant is Hick's Law for HCI?" (CHI 2020, dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/3313831.3376878). No contradicting evidence found across multiple searches. Only minor soft spot: "reliably and uniformly" loosely characterizes UX popularizations, but the empirical core (contingent, can-be-zero slope) is solidly supported.

#### V44 `a9a44c2c` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Roughly 0.1 second is the upper limit for a user to feel that a UI is reacting instantaneously, meaning no special feedback (beyond showing the result) is needed below this threshold."

**Source:** https://www.nngroup.com/articles/response-times-3-important-limits/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "0.1 second is about the limit for having the user feel that the system is reacting instantaneously"

*Evidence:* Claim is fully supported by the primary source, verbatim. The NN/g article (Nielsen, restating Miller 1968 "Response time in man-computer conversational transactions" and Card, Robertson & Mackinlay 1991) states: "0.1 second is about the limit for having the user feel that the system is reacting instantaneously, meaning that no special feedback is necessary except to display the result." Both clauses of the claim — the instantaneous-feel threshold AND "no special feedback beyond showing the result needed below this threshold" — are taken directly from the text, so it is not an overreach. Source quality matches the claim's strength: this is a normative human-factors standard rooted in foundational primary research (Miller 1968, Card 1991), not marketing or speculation, and it has held ~50+ years. Searched for contradicting evidence: latency-perception studies (Springer 2017 "Are 100ms Fast Enough?" finding ~60ms thresholds for mouse interaction; Tactual Labs CHI 2015 finding single-digit-ms detection for touch dragging) show users can DETECT lag below 100ms — but only in CONTINUOUS direct-manipulation tasks (dragging/tracking), a different perceptual regime from the DISCRETE action→result reaction the claim addresses. That research refines, not refutes, the guideline. Caveat to carry into the skill (not a refutation): the 100ms figure is an order-of-magnitude rule of thumb; for real-time direct manipulation the perceptual threshold is measurably lower, so label as normative convention/standard for discrete response with a lower-threshold note for continuous manipulation.
*Counter-source:* https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-319-58475-1_4 (Are 100ms Fast Enough? Latency Perception Thresholds in Mouse-Based Interaction, 2017) and https://www.tactuallabs.com/papers/howMuchFasterIsFastEnoughCHI15.pdf (CHI 2015) — these refine the threshold downward for continuous direct manipulation but do not contradict the discrete-response instantaneous-feel guideline.

#### V49 `aa6e598c` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Material Design 3 instructs designers to use spacing and proximity to group related elements and to separate unrelated ones — a direct application of the Gestalt proximity principle to UI layout."

**Source:** https://m3.material.io/foundations/layout/understanding-layout/spacing (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Use spacing to visually tie elements together and establish boundaries between unrelated items."

*Evidence:* PRIMARY SOURCE CONFIRMS THE CLAIM. The Material Design 3 spacing page (m3.material.io/foundations/layout/understanding-layout/spacing) states: "Grouping connects related elements that share context, such as an image and its caption, using spacing to visually tie elements together and establish boundaries between unrelated items." It explicitly distinguishes "implicit grouping" — using "close proximity and open space" to group related items — from "explicit grouping" (outlines/dividers/shadows). The supporting quote is faithful (only "Use" vs "using" differs; substance identical). The characterization as a "direct application of the Gestalt proximity principle" is accurate, not overreach: NN/G's authoritative article (nngroup.com/articles/gestalt-proximity) defines proximity as objects near each other being automatically perceived as a group, with related items close and unrelated items farther apart — exactly what MD3 prescribes, and MD3 itself uses the word "proximity." Checklist: (1) quote supports claim; (2) no contradicting source, NN/G corroborates; (3) source quality matches a modest descriptive claim — MD3 is the primary source for what MD3 instructs; (4) current (MD3 live; Gestalt proximity, Wertheimer 1923, is foundational, not fast-moving); (5) not marketing/cherry-picked. Consistent with the companion data-viz skill, which lists Gestalt grouping as a carryover principle — synergy, no conflict.

The claim's "no special feedback (beyond showing the result) is needed below this threshold" maps directly onto "no special feedback is necessary except to display the result." #### V51 `aa94345a` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Tractinsky (1997) replicated Kurosu & Kashimura's finding of a strong relationship between an interface's perceived aesthetics and its perceived usability, running three controlled experiments in a different culture (Israel) and finding very high correlations between perceived aesthetics and a priori perceived ease of use. This is a foundational primary source for the aesthetic-usability effect."

**Source:** https://www.semanticscholar.org/paper/Aesthetics-and-apparent-usability:-empirically-and-Tractinsky/536a5136eaf69bea96ba015d0a36b327c16909af (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Three experiments were conducted to validate and replicate, in a different cultural setting, the results of a study by Kurosu and Kashimura concerning the relationships between users' perceptions of interface aesthetics and usability."

*Evidence:* Every element confirmed against the primary source abstract (Tractinsky 1997, CHI '97, "Aesthetics and apparent usability: empirically assessing cultural and methodological issues"; verified via the official Ben-Gurion University research portal and Semantic Scholar/ACM DL). The abstract states verbatim: "Three experiments were conducted to validate and replicate, in a different cultural setting, the results of a study by Kurosu and Kashimura concerning the relationships between users' perceptions of interface aesthetics and usability"; "Very high correlations were found between perceived aesthetics of the interface and a priori perceived ease of use of the system"; and "The results support the basic findings by Kurosu and Kashimura." The "different cultural setting" was Israel (Tractinsky/Ben-Gurion University; Israeli student participants). Secondary corroboration: r ≈ 0.92 pre-experimental in Israel vs. r ≈ 0.59 in the original 1995 Japanese study; Experiment 2 (n=81) controlled question order and still found r = 0.83. Wikipedia and multiple HCI sources treat this as a/the foundational primary source for the aesthetic-usability effect. Source is a peer-reviewed CHI '97 paper (primary), matching the claim's strength. The 1997 date is appropriate for a foundational historical citation. No contradicting evidence found. Minor non-undermining nuance: the cross-cultural difference was opposite to Tractinsky's hypothesis (expected weaker, found stronger), but the claim does not misstate this.

#### V52 `ab91faf5` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Hick's law states choice reaction time is a logarithmic function of the number of equally likely alternatives: RT = a + b·log2(n), where a is the intercept and b the slope; it can also be written RT = a + bH where H is the average information/uncertainty in bits."

**Source:** https://web.ics.purdue.edu/~dws/pubs/ProctorSchneider_2018_QJEP.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The more widely accepted version of Hick's law for n equally likely alternatives is RT = a + b log 2n, in which a is the intercept and b the slope of the function relating RT to information. Log 2 has come to be used because of its link to binary digits (bits) of information... the equation for Hick's law is sometimes expressed in terms of the average information or uncertainty (H) in a series of trials, in the form RT = a + bH."

*Evidence:* The claim is a direct, accurate paraphrase of the cited primary source. Proctor & Schneider (2018, "Hick's Law for Choice Reaction Time: A Review," Quarterly Journal of Experimental Psychology — a peer-reviewed review in a respected journal) states verbatim: "The more widely accepted version of Hick's law for n equally likely alternatives is RT = a + b log 2n, in which a is the intercept and b the slope of the function relating RT to information" and "the equation for Hick's law is sometimes expressed in terms of the average information or uncertainty (H) ... in the form RT = a + bH." Both halves of the claim (the log2(n) form with a=intercept, b=slope; and the equivalent RT=a+bH form) match the quote exactly. Independent corroboration: the formulation is the standard textbook Hick-Hyman law (Hick 1952; Hyman 1953); Wikipedia and Oxford Reference confirm RT = a + b·log2 with a/b as intercept/slope and the H/entropy generalization for unequal probabilities. Source quality (primary peer-reviewed review) matches the modest, well-established strength of the claim; not outdated, not marketing. One non-fatal nuance: a competing common form is RT = a + b·log2(n+1) (the +1 capturing stimulus-occurrence uncertainty), but the claim faithfully reports the specific source's stated "more widely accepted version" and does not assert exclusivity, so it is not an overreach.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. The only qualification is that some sources (Wikipedia, Oxford Reference) prefer the RT = a + b·log2(n+1) variant, but this does not refute the claim since the claim correctly tracks what Proctor & Schneider 2018 explicitly label the more widely accepted version and includes the bH generalization.

**Source:** https://www.nngroup.com/articles/f-shaped-pattern-reading-web-content-discovered/ (primary)

*Evidence:* Claim is verbatim-confirmed against the primary source (NNG, Jakob Nielsen, 2006, https://www.nngroup.com/articles/f-shaped-pattern-reading-web-content-discovered/). Exact text: "We recorded how 232 users looked at thousands of Web pages" — confirming both "232 users" and "thousands of web pages." The three movements match exactly: (1) "Users first read in a horizontal movement, usually across the upper part of the content area"; (2) "read across in a second horizontal movement that typically covers a shorter area than the previous movement"; (3) "users scan the content's left side in a vertical movement." The claim is strictly descriptive of the study's finding and does not overreach into prescription. Therefore that critique does not refute this narrowly-scoped descriptive claim. Source quality (the actual primary research report) matches the claim's strength. Caveat for skill use: the descriptive finding is well-supported, but the F-pattern is a DEFAULT scanning behavior tied to unformatted/unmotivated reading and is often a sign of poor formatting — label any prescriptive "design for the F-pattern" advice as [CONVENTION], not [STRONG].
*Counter-source:* https://www.nngroup.com/articles/f-shaped-pattern-reading-web-content/ (NNG 2017 "Misunderstood, But Still Relevant") — qualifies application but reaffirms the pattern itself; does not contradict the descriptive claim.

#### V58 `acd67c1a` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "When usability and aesthetics are independently manipulated, users render independent judgments of the two, and aesthetic differences produced no significant difference on the System Usability Scale (SUS) — meaning beauty did not inflate measured perceived usability in this study."

**Source:** https://uxpajournal.org/boundary-conditions-aesthetics-usability/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Findings support the idea that users make independent judgments of usability and aesthetics, as differences in aesthetics had no significant effect on usability as measured by the System Usability Scale (SUS)."

*Evidence:* Claim is faithful to its primary source: Grishin & Gillan (2019), "Exploring the Boundary Conditions of the Effect of Aesthetics on Perceived Usability," Journal of Usability Studies (UXPA), Vol. 14, pp. 76-104. Primary page blocks bot fetch (404/403), but abstract content confirmed via convergent search snippets + a third-party commentary (kevinrichard.ch). The study crossed higher/lower usability x higher/lower aesthetics (independent manipulation, 4 versions); found "before use, interface aesthetics did not affect perceived usability," significant effects were of occasion and manipulated usability NOT aesthetics, and the paper's own conclusion is "findings support the idea that users make independent judgments of usability and aesthetics." All four claim components (independent manipulation; independent judgments; no significant aesthetics effect on SUS; beauty did not inflate measured usability "in this study") check out. The claim is correctly scoped with "in this study," so the large contrary aesthetic-usability-effect literature (Kurosu & Kashimura 1995; Tractinsky 1997; Tractinsky et al. 2000) does not refute it — that literature disputes the general proposition, not this single null result. Source quality (peer-reviewed JUS) matches the modest claim strength; current enough (2019); not marketing. CAVEATS (not refuting): (1) the verbatim supporting quote most precisely matches the sibling paper Alharoon & Gillan (2020, HFES Proc., DOI 10.1177/1071181320641452), though both same-lab papers reach the identical finding so the cited source still supports the claim; (2) the same study found a REVERSE effect — after use, low usability lowered aesthetics ratings — so "independence" holds aesthetics->usability but not usability->aesthetics. For the skill: present as a documented boundary condition to the aesthetic-usability effect, NOT as evidence the effect is a myth.
*Counter-source:* Aesthetic-usability effect literature (Kurosu & Kashimura 1995; Tractinsky 1997; Tractinsky, Katz & Ikar 2000) shows positive aesthetics->perceived-usability correlation at the field level — but does not refute this study's scoped null result.

#### V62 `ad1c2894` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled experiment that manipulated aesthetics and usability as separate (independent) variables, aesthetics had NO significant effect on participants' judgments of usability; the significant effects came from manipulated usability and from occasion (repeated use), not aesthetics. This directly qualifies/contradicts the strong form of the aesthetic-usability effect (Kurosu & Kashimura 1995; Tractinsky 1997)."

**Source:** https://uxpajournal.org/boundary-conditions-aesthetics-usability/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Results failed to show the hypothesized effect of aesthetics on participants' judgments of usability, and analyses showed a significant effect of occasion and manipulated usability, rather than aesthetics, on participants' judgments of usability."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED, not refuted. Primary source: Grishin, J. & Gillan, D. (2019), "Exploring the Boundary Conditions of the Effect of Aesthetics on Perceived Usability," Journal of Usability Studies (UXPA), vol. 14, pp. 76–104 (https://uxpajournal.org/boundary-conditions-aesthetics-usability/). The article's own text confirms all three pillars of the claim verbatim: (1) aesthetics and usability were manipulated as SEPARATE variables — "The absence of main effects of the interface aesthetics manipulation on SUS ratings or of the interface usability manipulation on VisAWI/CA/CE ratings suggest that usability and aesthetics were perceived separately in this experiment"; (2) NO significant aesthetics effect on usability judgments — "results failed to show the hypothesized effect of aesthetics on participants' judgments of usability... SUS ratings were not influenced by aesthetics"; (3) significant effects came from occasion + manipulated usability — "analyses showed a significant effect of occasion and manipulated usability, rather than aesthetics, on participants' judgments of usability" (within-subjects, four occasions). Checklist: quote supports claim exactly; source is peer-reviewed primary research (sufficient for a qualifying claim); current (2019, live debate); not marketing. Adversarial caveat that does NOT refute: the broader literature still supports the aesthetic-usability effect (Kurosu & Kashimura 1995; Tractinsky 1997; multiple positive replications per Wikipedia), so this single null result establishes a BOUNDARY CONDITION rather than overturning the phenomenon. The claim's hedged wording — "qualifies/contradicts the STRONG form" — matches exactly what the evidence supports (the study title is literally "Boundary Conditions"). It would be an overreach only if it claimed the effect was disproven outright, which it does not. Claim stands as written.
*Counter-source:* Broader literature supporting the aesthetic-usability effect (Kurosu & Kashimura 1995; Tractinsky 1997; positive replications summarized at https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aesthetic%E2%80%93usability_effect) shows the effect is generally observed — but this qualifies the strong/universal form rather than refuting the claim, which is itself only a qualification.

*Claim under review:* "Apple specifies 44x44 pt as the DEFAULT control size for iOS/iPadOS and watchOS — not the minimum; the documented minimum control size is 28x28 pt (iOS/iPadOS, watchOS, visionOS), 20x20 pt (macOS), and 56x56 pt (tvOS). This nuances the widely-cited '44pt minimum touch target' claim."

**Source:** https://developer.apple.com/design/human-interface-guidelines/accessibility (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Strive to meet the recommended minimum control size for each platform... Platform Default control size Minimum control size iOS, iPadOS 44x44 pt 28x28 pt macOS 28x28 pt 20x20 pt tvOS 66x66 pt 56x56 pt visionOS 60x60 pt 28x28 pt watchOS 44x44 pt 28x28 pt"

*Evidence:* The claim's table values are accurate and primary-sourced (corroborated: LogRocket confirms iOS 44pt "hit target" and visionOS 60pt; the supplied Apple HIG quote matches). But the load-bearing conclusion — that 44pt is "the DEFAULT control size, NOT the minimum" and that this "nuances the 44pt minimum touch target claim" — conflates two distinct Apple metrics. "Control size" is the control's VISUAL footprint (table: iOS default 44 / min 28); "tappable area / hit target" is the MINIMUM touchable region, which Apple documents separately and still sets at 44x44 pt. Every independent source describes 44pt specifically as the minimum HIT/TOUCH target, not a default: LogRocket ("Apple's iOS asks 'hit target' to be at least 44x44 pt"); DesignMonks ("Apple suggests a minimum 44x44 points touch target"); and the UX consensus that "a button may have a visual size of 24x24 px, but guidelines recommend a minimum touch target size of 44x44 px... the tappable/hit target area is a separate concept from the visual control size." A control can render visually at 28pt yet still require a 44pt tappable area, so the 28pt "minimum control size" does NOT relax the 44pt minimum touch target — they coexist on different axes. The claim's assertion that 44pt is "not the minimum" is therefore contradicted by Apple's own still-current minimum-tappable-area guidance. The factual table survives; the interpretive takeaway is an overreach that merges control size with touch target.
*Counter-source:* Apple HIG long-standing "minimum tappable area of 44x44 pt for all controls" (distinct from the control-size table); corroborated by LogRocket (blog.logrocket.com/ux-design/all-accessible-touch-target-sizes) and DesignMonks (designmonks.co/blog/perfect-mobile-button-size), both calling 44pt the minimum touch/hit target, not a default.

#### V65 `ae672c7c` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "About 10 seconds is the limit for keeping a user's attention focused on the interaction; beyond it users want to switch to other tasks while waiting."

**Source:** https://www.nngroup.com/articles/response-times-3-important-limits/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "10 seconds is about the limit for keeping the user's attention focused on the dialogue"

*Evidence:* Claim is faithfully sourced and not contradicted, so it survives — but only at CONVENTION strength, not STRONG. (1) Quote match: NN/g (Nielsen, 1993) states verbatim "10 seconds is about the limit for keeping the user's attention focused on the dialogue," and the article also says "For longer delays, users will want to perform other tasks while waiting" — both halves of the claim are directly supported; not an overreach. (2) Source: primary, respected (NN/g), crediting Miller 1968 and Card et al. 1991; claim is hedged ("about 10 seconds"). (3) Caveat = labeling, not refutation: the empirical base is weak — Miller (1968) called the thresholds "calculated guesses" needing real-world verification, critics note "no clear empirical evidence supports these magic numbers," and Nielsen himself later (1995/96) loosened web tolerance to ~15s while Nah (2004) measured ~2s web abandonment. These address different metrics (tolerance/abandonment), so they QUALIFY but do not CONTRADICT the 10s attention-focus heuristic. Conclusion: keep the claim but tag it [CONVENTION]; tagging it [STRONG]/controlled-study-proven would be incorrect.
*Counter-source:* Miller (1968) self-described "calculated guesses"; Nielsen (1995/96) 15s web tolerance; Nah (2004) Tolerable Waiting Time ~2s abandonment; critiques noting no clear empirical support for the magic numbers
