#### V69 `af03a25c` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The earliest empirical HCI validation (Landauer & Nachbar 1985, touch-menu selection) found selection time fit a logarithmic (Hick/Fitts) function, and because of that logarithmic relation the number of alternatives per screen does not affect total selection time — only the number of screens does — so broad, shallow menu trees beat narrow, deep ones."

**Source:** https://web.ics.purdue.edu/~dws/pubs/ProctorSchneider_2018_QJEP.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Landauer and Nachbar concluded, "Broader, shallower menu trees yield faster search than narrower, deeper ones" (p. 76)."

*Evidence:* The claim's final clause (broad-shallow menu trees beat narrow-deep) is correct and matches the quote, but its load-bearing middle clause is a misread that contradicts the law it invokes. Landauer & Nachbar (1985) modeled menu selection as Hick-Hyman decision time dt = c + k·log(n) where n = alternatives per screen, plus Fitts movement (confirmed via search: "RT = a + b log2(n)... n is the number of stimuli"). By that formula the number of alternatives per screen DOES affect selection time — logarithmically. The claim's assertion that "the number of alternatives per screen does not affect total selection time — only the number of screens does" directly negates the k·log(n) term that is the entire mechanism. Broad-shallow wins because per-screen cost grows slowly (log) while depth adds whole screens linearly — a tradeoff, not because breadth is "free." The supplied quote ("Broader, shallower menu trees yield faster search than narrower, deeper ones," p. 76) supports only the conclusion; it says nothing about alternatives-per-screen having zero effect, so that intermediate step is unsupported overreach. Additional refutation: (a) visual search time is LINEAR in item count, distinct from choice RT, so more items per screen add real cost the claim erases (per the same ScienceDirect "Choice Reaction Time" source); (b) modern reviews including Proctor & Schneider (2018) themselves and Liu/Gori/Rioul "How Relevant is Hick's Law for HCI?" caution that Hick's law does not cleanly govern menu/UI selection and that broad-shallow holds within an optimal-breadth range, not unboundedly; (c) "earliest empirical HCI validation" is dubious — Miller (1981), Snowberry et al. (1983), and Kiger (1984) breadth/depth menu studies predate it.
*Counter-source:* Hick-Hyman law formulation dt = c + k·log(n) / RT = a + b·log2(n), confirmed via ScienceDirect "Choice Reaction Time" overview (sciencedirect.com/topics/computer-science/choice-reaction-time) and Proctor & Schneider 2018 (journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1080/17470218.2017.1322622); Liu, Gori, Rioul et al. "How Relevant is Hick's Law for HCI?" (perso.telecom-paristech.fr/rioul/publis/202001liugoririoulbeaudouinlafonguiard.pdf)

#### V70 `af0d9e4b` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "WCAG contrast is computed from luminance, not hue: color/hue is explicitly NOT a key factor in the contrast calculation, so meeting the requirement is independent of color perception."

**Source:** https://www.w3.org/WAI/WCAG21/Understanding/contrast-minimum.html (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "contrast is calculated in such a way that color (hue) is not a key factor ... Requiring good luminance contrast accommodates this by requiring contrast that is independent of color perception"

*Evidence:* VERIFIED, not refuted. The primary source (W3C WCAG 2.1 Understanding SC 1.4.3) states verbatim: "contrast is calculated in such a way that color (hue) is not a key factor," and computes contrast as (L1+0.05)/(L2+0.05) from relative luminance L = 0.2126·R + 0.7152·G + 0.0722·B. The "independent of color perception" wording is taken directly from the spec's color-vision-deficiency reasoning ("Requiring good luminance contrast accommodates this by requiring contrast that is independent of color perception"). The claim restates the normative text accurately — no overreach. Adversarial search (APCA / WCAG-3 criticism) REINFORCES rather than contradicts: the standard critique is that WCAG 2.x measures luminance contrast, not perceptual color difference (e.g., saturated red vs. green of similar luminance scores low) — which presupposes, not disputes, that the calculation ignores hue. Source quality: W3C is the normative standards body (top-tier primary). Currency: formula unchanged through WCAG 2.2 (2023), still current. Minor nuance (not a refutation): the luminance coefficients themselves derive from human brightness perception (green ~71%), so luminance is not perception-free in origin — but hue genuinely is not a factor, which is exactly what the claim asserts. Consistent with the companion data-viz finding that contrast/eye-strain is luminance-driven, not hue-driven — a synergy, not a conflict.
*Counter-source:* No credible source refutes the claim; APCA/WCAG-3 critiques (accessibilitychecker.org, apcacontrast.com, colorarchive.org) confirm WCAG 2.x is luminance-based and hue-agnostic — they argue it is TOO luminance-focused, which supports the claim.

#### V74 `af922d50` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Hick's law states choice reaction time is a logarithmic function of the number of equally likely alternatives: RT = a + b·log2(n), where a is the intercept and b the slope; it can also be written RT = a + bH where H is the average information/uncertainty in bits."

**Source:** https://web.ics.purdue.edu/~dws/pubs/ProctorSchneider_2018_QJEP.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The more widely accepted version of Hick's law for n equally likely alternatives is RT = a + b log 2n, in which a is the intercept and b the slope of the function relating RT to information. Log 2 has come to be used because of its link to binary digits (bits) of information... the equation for Hick's law is sometimes expressed in terms of the average information or uncertainty (H) in a series of trials, in the form RT = a + bH."

*Evidence:* The claim faithfully paraphrases the cited primary source. Proctor & Schneider (2018, Quarterly Journal of Experimental Psychology, "Hick's law for choice reaction time: A review") states verbatim: "The more widely accepted version of Hick's law for n equally likely alternatives is RT = a + b log2n, in which a is the intercept and b the slope... the equation for Hick's law is sometimes expressed in terms of the average information or uncertainty (H)... in the form RT = a + bH." The claim reproduces exactly this: log2(n) form for equally likely alternatives (a=intercept, b=slope) plus the RT = a + bH information-theoretic form. Convergent independent sources confirm: Hyman (1953) defined H = -Sum p(i)log2 p(i) (bits), and for equally likely alternatives H = log2(n), so the two forms the claim gives are mathematically consistent. The one historical nuance (Hick's original 1952 equation used log2(n+1), the +1 accounting for the no-response option) does NOT refute the claim, because the cited source explicitly endorses log2(n) as "the more widely accepted version" — the claim is faithful to its source, not cherry-picking. Source quality is high: a peer-reviewed review article on this exact topic; the claim is standard textbook material, not an extraordinary claim. Not outdated (stable 1952/1953 law, 2018 review confirms). Not marketing. Claim is accurate, current, and source strength matches.
*Counter-source:* Hick's original 1952 formulation used RT = b·log2(n+1) (the +1 for the no-response option), and some textbooks state Hick's law that way — but the cited Proctor & Schneider 2018 review explicitly frames log2(n) as "the more widely accepted version," so this is a non-refuting nuance, not a contradiction.

#### V75 `af9e4696` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Tractinsky (1997) replicated Kurosu & Kashimura's finding of a strong relationship between an interface's perceived aesthetics and its perceived usability, running three controlled experiments in a different culture (Israel) and finding very high correlations between perceived aesthetics and a priori perceived ease of use. This is a foundational primary source for the aesthetic-usability effect."

**Source:** https://www.semanticscholar.org/paper/Aesthetics-and-apparent-usability:-empirically-and-Tractinsky/536a5136eaf69bea96ba015d0a36b327c16909af (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Three experiments were conducted to validate and replicate, in a different cultural setting, the results of a study by Kurosu and Kashimura concerning the relationships between users' perceptions of interface aesthetics and usability."

*Evidence:* Claim is fully supported by the primary source (Tractinsky, N. 1997, "Aesthetics and apparent usability: empirically assessing cultural and methodological issues," CHI '97 Proceedings, ACM doi:10.1145/258549.258626). The paper's own abstract states near-verbatim: "Three experiments were conducted to validate and replicate, in a different cultural setting, the results of a study by Kurosu and Kashimura concerning the relationships between users' perceptions of interface aesthetics and usability... Very high correlations were found between perceived aesthetics of the interface and a priori perceived ease of use of the system." Every element of the claim checks out: (1) three experiments — confirmed; (2) different culture/Israel — confirmed (Tractinsky, Ben-Gurion University of the Negev, Beer Sheva, Israel; Israel vs. Japan); (3) replication of Kurosu & Kashimura — confirmed; (4) very high correlations between perceived aesthetics and a priori (pre-use) perceived ease of use — confirmed (r ≈ 0.83; correlation held even when measurement order was reversed in a second experiment with 81 students). The claim is also careful and accurate on the critical nuance: it specifies PERCEIVED/apparent usability (a priori, before use), not actual usability — which is exactly what "apparent usability" means in the paper. Source quality matches claim strength: this is the actual peer-reviewed primary source, not a secondary summary, and it is the canonical foundational citation for the aesthetic-usability effect (cited as such by Wikipedia's "Aesthetic–usability effect" article and the IxDF/Interaction Design Foundation literature). Date appropriateness: 1997 is correct and this is a historical/foundational paper, not a stale claim about a fast-moving field. One honest refinement (does NOT refute the claim): the cross-cultural difference Tractinsky found ran OPPOSITE to his hypothesis — he expected weaker aesthetics-usability coupling in "action-oriented" Israeli culture but found it as strong or stronger than in Japan; the claim does not misstate this, it only says "very high correlations," which is accurate. No credible source disputes the characterization. The companion data-viz consistency note also holds: this is a perceived-aesthetics/perceived-usability (halo/preference) finding, not a controlled comprehension-accuracy result — consistent with our practice of distinguishing preference from performance.

### Aesthetic preference — why people intuitively like a UI

#### V76 `a067c394` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The fluency-driven affect is misattributed to the stimulus 'by default': in Winkielman & Fazendeiro (2001), priming raised liking for new pictures, but telling participants their reactions might come from background music eliminated the effect — showing the positive feeling is normally (mis)attributed to the judged target without awareness of its true source. [STRONG]"

**Source:** https://pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkielman/Processing_Fluency_as_the_Source_of_Experiences_PSYCHE-2002.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "attributing the affective reaction to the music eliminated this effect... This pattern of results is consistent with our theoretical assumption that processing facilitation elicits a positive affective reaction that is 'by default' attributed to the judged target."

*Evidence:* Claim verified verbatim against its own primary source. I extracted the text of the PSYCHE-2002 PDF (zlib-decompressed streams) at pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkielman. It states: "a recent series of studies by Winkielman and Fazendeiro (2001)... some participants were told that their reactions to the stimuli might be influenced by music playing in the background... The 'no-attribution' condition replicated the earlier effect of fluency on liking. That is, participants judged new pictures as more likeable when they were preceded by related rather than by unrelated word primes. However, attributing the affective reaction to the music eliminated this effect." The supporting quote is present verbatim: "processing facilitation elicits a positive affective reaction that is 'by default' attributed to the judged target." Three checks pass: (1) the year 2001 in the claim exactly matches the year used in the cited source (not an error; the later Reber et al. 2004 review cites the same study as 2003 due to manuscript-date drift); (2) priming raised liking for new pictures — confirmed; (3) music-attribution eliminated the effect — confirmed. Independent corroboration: Reber, Schwarz & Winkielman (2004), Pers. Soc. Psychol. Rev. (peer-reviewed, dornsife.usc.edu) describes the same study and ADDS a strengthening dissociation — attributing fluency to music did NOT eliminate the effect, but attributing the affective reaction DID, isolating the fluency→affect→liking path. Not outdated (foundational theory), not marketing, primary academic sources. One caveat, not fatal: the underlying Winkielman & Fazendeiro study is reported within review chapters as (apparently unpublished) primary data rather than a standalone peer-reviewed report, so the [STRONG] label leans on secondhand reporting; but the misattribution paradigm is a gold-standard causal design and the broader fluency-affect-misattribution finding is well-replicated. The claim's only simplification — omitting that only affect-attribution (not fluency-attribution) eliminated the effect — faithfully tracks the PSYCHE source's own framing and does not contradict it.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. The closest skeptical angle — that the cited Winkielman & Fazendeiro study appears to be unpublished primary data reported within review papers (PSYCHE 2002; Reber et al. PSPR 2004) rather than a standalone peer-reviewed article — is a strength caveat, not a contradiction. The finding is corroborated across two review venues and consistent with the broader well-replicated misattribution/mere-exposure literature.

#### V77 `a06e2d11` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Prototypicality of visual stimuli predicts BOTH processing fluency (faster categorization speed) and rated attractiveness — establishing that the same stimuli that are easy to process are also liked more."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1111/j.1467-9280.2006.01785.x (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "prototypicality was a predictor of both fluency (categorization speed) and attractiveness"

*Evidence:* The source is Winkielman, Halberstadt, Fazendeiro & Catty (2006), "Prototypes Are Attractive Because They Are Easy on the Mind," Psychological Science 17(9), 799-806 — a top-tier peer-reviewed primary source reporting controlled experiments. The PubMed abstract (pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/16984298) states verbatim: "In both experiments, prototypicality was a predictor of both fluency (categorization speed) and attractiveness" and "fluency mediated the effect of prototypicality on attractiveness, although some effect of prototypicality remained when fluency was controlled." The claim is therefore directly supported by the quote and the abstract — not a misread. Stimuli used were random-dot patterns and geometric patterns varying in prototypicality; participants both categorized (speed = fluency) and rated attractiveness. The claim's framing ("same stimuli easy to process are liked more") is justified by the explicit mediation result, and it does NOT overclaim full fluency mediation (the paper notes residual prototypicality effect). Source quality matches claim strength. Not marketing, not cherry-picked. CAVEAT (qualification, not refutation): the broader beauty-in-averageness effect has documented boundary conditions/reversals — "Ugliness-in-Averageness" when blends are made from recognizable faces or when a categorization task precedes judgment (biorxiv.org/content/10.1101/360651; NeurIPS 2018 Bayesian account). These refine the generality of the fluency→liking link but do not contradict this specific 2006 finding that, within these experiments, prototypicality predicted both fluency and attractiveness. The fluency-aesthetics framework remains active (Royal Society Phil Trans B, 379:20230326, 2024), so the finding is not outdated. Recommended label: [STRONG] (controlled studies).
*Counter-source:* Boundary-condition qualifications only (not refutations): "Beauty-in-averageness and its contextual modulations: A Bayesian statistical account" (biorxiv.org/content/10.1101/360651; NeurIPS 2018) documents Ugliness-in-Averageness reversals under task-driven categorization — refines generality but does not dispute the 2006 dual-prediction result.

#### V78 `a079d16d` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "High processing fluency carries a positive hedonic mark: fluent processing produces a positively-valenced subjective experience that participants interpret as a reaction to the stimulus, yielding more positive evaluations (the fluency -> affect -> liking mechanism). [STRONG]"

**Source:** https://pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkielman/Processing_Fluency_as_the_Source_of_Experiences_PSYCHE-2002.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "we assume that high fluency leads to a positively marked subjective experience. Participants interpret this positive response as their reaction to the stimulus, resulting in more positive evaluations. This assumption is consistent with a more general model of how experiences influence judgments, the so-called 'feelings-as-information model.'"

*Evidence:* Claim is a faithful paraphrase of the cited Winkielman PSYCHE 2002 quote, and the [STRONG] label is justified by controlled studies beyond the source paper. Direct physiological evidence: Winkielman & Cacioppo (2001, J. Personality & Social Psychology) used facial EMG and found experimentally-induced fluency raised zygomaticus (smiling-muscle) activity but not corrugator (frowning-muscle) activity — showing the positive affect is genuine and online, not merely a post-hoc judgment artifact. Reinforced by Reber, Winkielman & Schwarz (1998, Psychological Science) manipulating perceptual fluency to increase liking, and the Reber, Schwarz & Winkielman (2004, Personality & Social Psychology Review) review on fluency and aesthetic pleasure. Adversarial check found qualifications, not refutations: Unkelbach (2006/2007) shows fluency interpretation is learned and context-dependent (can be reattributed to truth/brightness/recognition and even reversed by retraining), and a competing "amplification" account exists (the 2019 JESP amplification-vs-hedonic-marking paper). But these bound the mechanism's scope rather than negate the default positive hedonic mark — and a 2021 study (Consciousness and Cognition) found fluent processing produces positive evaluations even when base rates suggest negative ones, strengthening the default-positive claim. Current, primary-sourced, no conflict with companion luminance/blue-light/dark-mode data-viz findings (orthogonal domain; synergy with clarity/contrast holds). Honest caveat: the hedonic-marking-vs-amplification mechanism debate is still active, so the mechanism's universality is qualified, but the core claim that high fluency carries a positive hedonic mark interpreted as liking is well-supported.
*Counter-source:* Unkelbach (2006/2007) learned-interpretation account and the 2019 JESP "amplification versus hedonic marking" paper qualify the mechanism's scope but do not refute the default positive hedonic mark; the 2021 Consciousness and Cognition base-rate study supports it.

The one detail not named in the abstract is the specific "zygomaticus/smile muscle," but that is accurate to the full paper and is the standard EMG index of positive valence — a true specification, not an overreach. No credible contradicting evidence found; this is a foundational, heavily cited finding and the fluency→positive-affect link is robust. Strength label [STRONG] (controlled physiological study) matches source quality. #### V80 `a083a8c1` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Visual appeal of web homepages can be assessed within 50 ms: mean appeal ratings at 50 ms exposure correlated with ratings at 500 ms exposure at r = .947 (r2 = .897) on the first phase and r = .953 (r2 = .908) on the second, both p < .001 — so a first impression of appeal forms essentially as fast at 50 ms as at 500 ms."

**Source:** https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/01449290500330448 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "It revealed that r = .947, p < .001 (r2 = .897). This result was slightly lower, but comparable to that obtained in Study 2 for the 500 ms condition alone... the correlation for the second phase of both conditions yielded a value of r = .953, p < .001, (r2 = .908)... Findings would thus appear to be as robust with an exposure time of 50 ms as with an exposure time of 500 ms."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED AGAINST PRIMARY SOURCE. Independently confirmed the exact statistics from a full-text copy (fliphtml5.com/hhwj/gqex) of Lindgaard, Fernandes, Dudek & Brown (2006), "Attention web designers: You have 50 milliseconds to make a good first impression!", Behaviour & Information Technology 25(2), 115-126. Verbatim text: "A Pearson Product Moment correlation comparing the mean visual appeal ratings on the first phase for the two conditions (50 and 500 ms) was calculated. It revealed that r = .947, p < .001 (r² = .897)" and "the correlation for the second phase of both conditions yielded a value of r = .953, p < .001, (r² = .908)."

(1) SUPPORTED BY QUOTE — yes. The most plausible misread was whether .947/.953 are cross-condition (50ms vs 500ms) or within-condition test-retest reliability. The source explicitly resolves this: the correlation compares "the two conditions (50 and 500 ms)" and "both conditions" = between-condition. The claim reads them correctly. The paper separately reports high phase-to-phase within-condition reliability too, but the cited numbers are the between-condition values the claim states.

(2) Internal consistency check passes: .947² = .8968 ≈ .897 and .953² = .9082 ≈ .908. The reported r² exactly match the r values, confirming accurate transcription (not fabricated).

(3) Interpretation faithful: the paper's own abstract concludes "visual appeal can be assessed within 50 ms," matching the claim's "forms essentially as fast at 50 ms as at 500 ms." The claim correctly says "mean appeal ratings" (these are aggregate means across web-page stimuli — rank-order of appeal preserved across exposure durations).

(4) Source/recency: primary peer-reviewed source, 1000+ citations; corroborated, not contradicted, by later replication (Tuch et al. 2012, which pushed the threshold to ~17-50ms). No contradicting credible source found.

(5) No conflict with companion data-viz findings (luminance/dark-mode/data-ink) — unrelated domain.

Minor non-refuting caveat: the correlation is of aggregate means across stimuli (rank-order consistency), and Study 3 was explicitly designed to test the "mere exposure" alternative; but the claim does not overstate beyond the paper's own conclusion. Appropriate [STRONG] labeling.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. Searched for methodological critiques; the only nuance is that these are aggregate-mean (rank-order) correlations and the study itself probed a "mere exposure" interpretation — neither refutes the quoted statistics or the conclusion. Later work (Tuch et al. 2012) replicates rather than contradicts.

#### V83 `a1154a8f` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The fluency-driven affect is misattributed to the stimulus 'by default': in Winkielman & Fazendeiro (2001), priming raised liking for new pictures, but telling participants their reactions might come from background music eliminated the effect — showing the positive feeling is normally (mis)attributed to the judged target without awareness of its true source. [STRONG]"

**Source:** https://pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkielman/Processing_Fluency_as_the_Source_of_Experiences_PSYCHE-2002.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "attributing the affective reaction to the music eliminated this effect... This pattern of results is consistent with our theoretical assumption that processing facilitation elicits a positive affective reaction that is 'by default' attributed to the judged target."

*Evidence:* Decompressed the cited primary PDF (Reber, Fazendeiro & Winkielman, PSYCHE 8(10), Oct 2002) and confirmed the supporting quote verbatim. Source text: "Winkielman and Fazendeiro (2001)... added a misattribution manipulation. Specifically, some participants were told that their reactions to the stimuli might be influenced by music playing in the background... The 'no-attribution' condition replicated the earlier effect of fluency on liking. That is, participants judged new pictures as more likeable when they were preceded by related rather than by unrelated word primes. However, attributing the affective reaction to the music eliminated this effect... This pattern of results is consistent with our theoretical assumption that processing facilitation elicits a positive affective reaction that is 'by default' attributed to the judged target." Every element of the claim matches: the 2001 citation (the source itself uses 2001), priming raised liking for new pictures, music misattribution eliminated the effect, and default-attribution to the judged target without awareness of the true source. The misattribution design is the standard causal test for the feelings-as-information account (Schwarz & Clore 1983), and no credible source contradicts this specific finding. Caveats (not refutations): the underlying Winkielman & Fazendeiro study was an unpublished/conference manuscript reported in peer-reviewed reviews rather than a standalone empirical paper, and some later reviews cite it as "2003" — but it is a genuine controlled misattribution experiment and the claim is internally consistent with its own cited 2001 source, so [STRONG] is defensible.

#### V84 `a11e2c5e` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "A single fluency mechanism parsimoniously unifies multiple aesthetic-preference phenomena — preference for familiarity, symmetry, prototypicality, and high figure-ground contrast all arise because each variable eases (facilitates) processing and thereby triggers positive affect. [STRONG for the mechanism; the listed perceptual cues themselves are CONVENTION-to-STRONG depending on cue]"

**Source:** https://pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkielman/Processing_Fluency_as_the_Source_of_Experiences_PSYCHE-2002.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "the notion of fluency as an indicator of cognitive progress may provide a parsimonious account for a variety of preference phenomena, such as preference for familiarity, symmetry, prototypicality, high figure-ground contrast, and many others. In this account, all these phenomena occur because the respective variable facilitates processing and thereby triggers positive affect."

*Evidence:* Claim survives 3 adversarial refutation attempts. (1) QUOTE FIDELITY: supporting quote is near-verbatim from Winkielman, Schwarz, Fazendeiro & Reber (PSYCHE 2002), explicitly naming familiarity, symmetry, prototypicality, and high figure-ground contrast as facilitated-processing→positive-affect phenomena. Claim hardens "may provide a parsimonious account" to "parsimoniously unifies" but its own labels ([STRONG mechanism; cues CONVENTION-to-STRONG]) flag this honestly — not an overreach. (2) MECHANISM = STRONG holds: not resting on the proponents' theory paper alone — Reber, Winkielman & Schwarz (1998, Psychological Science 9:45-48) manipulated figure-ground CONTRAST, priming, and duration in controlled experiments (higher fluency = judged prettier/less ugly); Winkielman & Cacioppo (2001, JPSP) showed fluency elicits zygomaticus/smiling EMG activity; Winkielman, Halberstadt, Fazendeiro & Catty (2006, Psychological Science 17:799-806, "Prototypes are attractive because they are easy on the mind") directly demonstrated fluency MEDIATES prototypicality→attractiveness, with EMG confirmation. (3) STRONGEST QUALIFIER does not contradict: Graf & Landwehr (2015, PSPR, "A Dual-Process Perspective on Fluency-Based Aesthetics," PIA model) EXTENDS fluency theory — adds a second deliberative route for complex/novel (disfluent) stimuli — and itself describes its fluency framework as "parsimonious." That is a SCOPE caveat (fluency is not a total theory of aesthetics; it does not explain novelty/interest-driven liking), not a refutation of the four ease-of-processing cues the claim actually unifies. Minor caveat already covered by claim's hedging: Winkielman 2006 found PARTIAL mediation ("some effect of prototypicality remained when fluency was controlled"), consistent with "CONVENTION-to-STRONG depending on cue." Source quality (primary, peer-reviewed originator + independent controlled replications), currency (2002-2024 literature still builds on it), and strength labels all match. Consistent with companion findings (figure-ground contrast↔processing ease is synergistic with luminance-contrast comfort).
*Counter-source:* Graf & Landwehr (2015), "A Dual-Process Perspective on Fluency-Based Aesthetics," Personality and Social Psychology Review — qualifies SCOPE (adds disfluency/interest route) but does not refute the four-cue unification; https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/1088868315574978

#### V85 `a12acfa0` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Visual-appeal judgments are highly reliable/stable: when participants rated the same homepages twice, mean appeal ratings across the two phases correlated at r = .97 (p < .001) in Study 1, indicating appeal is consistent rather than idiosyncratic across repeated exposure."

**Source:** https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/01449290500330448 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "correlation coefficient (r) was .97 (p < .001), indicating that... participants' ratings were highly reliable."

*Evidence:* The claim is accurately sourced to Lindgaard, Fernandes, Dudek & Brown (2006), "Attention web designers: You have 50 milliseconds to make a good first impression!", Behaviour & Information Technology 25(2):115-126 — DOI 10.1080/01449290500330448 (a primary, peer-reviewed source, appropriate for the claim's strength). Study 1 had participants rate the visual appeal of the same web homepages TWICE, each shown for 500 ms. Multiple corroborating descriptions of the paper confirm the exact figure: "the correlation between the mean evaluation of the visual attractiveness of web pages in the first phase and the mean evaluations in the second phase was 0.97." Search summaries independently confirm Study 1's design (rated twice at 500ms) and that "visual appeal ratings were highly correlated from one phase to the next." The supporting quote ("r was .97 (p < .001)... highly reliable") matches the paper's own conclusion, so the claim is not an overreach of the quote. CAVEATS worth flagging (qualify, not refute): (1) The r=.97 is between MEAN ratings averaged across participants, which the claim correctly states ("mean appeal ratings"); aggregate means correlate far higher than individual-level test-retest would, so .97 reflects stability of the per-page average, not that any given person reproduces their own rating that tightly. (2) The gloss "consistent rather than idiosyncratic" is slightly loose — a mean-rating test-retest measures aggregate temporal stability, not inter-individual agreement, so it doesn't by itself rule out idiosyncratic individual tastes that wash out on averaging. (3) This is a stable historical statistic, not a fast-moving-field claim, so age (2006) is not a defect. Net: the load-bearing fact (r=.97 test-retest of mean appeal ratings, Study 1, p<.001) is correct and primary-sourced.
*Counter-source:* No credible source disputes the r=.97 figure. The closest adjacent critique (Tractinsky/Tuch-line work and "Evaluating the consistency of immediate aesthetic perceptions of web pages," J. Human-Computer Studies) questions the 50ms-impression generalization, not the Study 1 test-retest reliability value itself. Only nuance: the .97 is an aggregated mean-rating correlation, which the claim states accurately.

#### V89 `a22bb91c` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The fluency-to-liking effect is a misattribution: it is mediated by the affective reaction the ease produces, and it is eliminated when people are led to attribute that feeling to an external source (e.g., background music) — direct evidence that fluency is experienced as 'I like this object' rather than 'this was easy to process.'"

**Source:** https://dornsife.usc.edu/norbert-schwarz/wp-content/uploads/sites/231/2023/11/04_pspr_reber_et_al_beauty.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The impact of fluency on evaluative judgments is apparently mediated by the elicited affective reactions and hence eliminated when the informational value of the affective reaction is called into question through misattribution manipulations (Winkielman & Fazendeiro, 2003)."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by a high-quality primary source and is accurate down to the cited example. The supporting quote is verbatim from Reber, Schwarz & Winkielman (2004, PSPR 8(4):364-382, "Processing Fluency and Aesthetic Pleasure"): "The impact of fluency on evaluative judgments is apparently mediated by the elicited affective reactions and hence eliminated when the informational value of the affective reaction is called into question through misattribution manipulations (Winkielman & Fazendeiro, 2003)." (1) Affect-mediation: directly stated by the quote. (2) Elimination via external attribution with the SPECIFIC "background music" example: independently confirmed — Winkielman & Fazendeiro (2003) replicated a conceptual-priming liking study and told participants their reactions "might be influenced by background music"; discounting the affective reaction by attributing it to the music eliminated the fluency-liking effect. The example is precisely accurate, not fabricated. (3) "Fluency experienced as 'I like this object'" is the standard feelings-as-information interpretation (Schwarz & Clore 1983 lineage), not an overreach. Source quality matches claim strength (peer-reviewed PSPR review by the field's leading researchers; corroborated by Reber, Winkielman & Schwarz 1998, Psychological Science, "Effects of Perceptual Fluency on Affective Judgments," and Winkielman, Schwarz, Fazendeiro & Reber 2003, "The hedonic marking of processing fluency"). Not outdated — foundational, still-current psychology theory. Minor caveats that REFINE rather than contradict: the specific 2003 cite was an unpublished manuscript at the time, and the literature notes the fluency-elicited affect is hedonically positive (not merely nonspecific arousal), which strengthens the affect-mediation account. No credible source disputes the core mechanism. [STRONG].
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. The only "criticism" in the literature (whether fluency affect is nonspecific arousal vs. specifically positive valence) refines the misattribution-mediation account in the claim's favor rather than refuting it. Caveat: the named Winkielman & Fazendeiro (2003) study was an unpublished manuscript, but the effect is corroborated by published work (Reber, Winkielman & Schwarz 1998, Psychological Science 9(1):45-48).

#### V90 `a2445bb9` — **upheld** · confidence: high

This is foundational support for the fluency-->positive-affect-->liking chain underpinning intuitive aesthetic preference."

**Source:** https://pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkiel/reber-winkielman-schwarz-Fluency-PS-1998.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "We conclude (a) that perceptual fluency increases liking and (b) that the experience of fluency is affectively positive, and hence attributed to positive but not to negative features, as reflected in a differential impact on positive and negative judgments."

None is repetition/mere-exposure — dissociating fluency from the repetition paradigm is the paper's central contribution, so the claim's emphasis ("means OTHER than repetition") is accurate, not overreach. The supporting quote is the paper's verbatim conclusion. Mechanism independently corroborated: (a) misattribution test — Winkielman & Fazendeiro (2003) eliminated the fluency->liking effect when reactions were attributed to background music, confirming the affect-as-information chain; (b) facial-EMG zygomaticus ("smile") response to fluent stimuli (Winkielman & Cacioppo 2001). Extended, not retracted, by Reber, Schwarz & Winkielman (2004) PSPR review. No credible refutation found. Source quality matches the strong claim. Only nuance: the BROADER universal "fluency=beauty" law has downstream qualifications (disfluency can raise engagement; Berlyne arousal/inverted-U), but the claim is narrowly scoped to this paper and labels it "foundational support" — a correct, modest characterization. No conflict with companion data-viz findings; fluency<->clarity/contrast is a synergy.
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source found. Strongest corroborating (not contradicting) test: Winkielman & Fazendeiro (2003) misattribution replication, summarized in Reber, Schwarz & Winkielman (2004), "Processing Fluency and Aesthetic Pleasure," Pers Soc Psychol Rev 8(4):364-382. Downstream qualifications of the universal generalization (disfluency-raises-engagement; Berlyne inverted-U) exist but do not contradict this paper's specific findings.

Not marketing, not outdated for this stable finding, no conflict with companion fluency/restraint findings. Minor: "closer to monotonic decline" slightly simplifies a plateau-then-drop shape (low≈medium, then drop), but the "closer to" hedge keeps it faithful.
*Counter-source:* Geissler, Zinkhan & Watson (2006) and the 2021 "Revisiting Berlyne's inverted U-shape" product-design paper still report inverted-U, but for purchase-intent/product aesthetics rather than website visual-appeal first impressions — outside the claim's explicit scope and already covered by its "naive inverted-U" qualifier.

Source quality is high: primary, peer-reviewed, three controlled experiments. Not outdated (foundational, stable field, replicated e.g. 2024 Scientific Reports expertise study; cited by Cila/Hekkert 2014). Not marketing. But the supporting quote itself says "jointly and equally effective," so the claim faithfully mirrors the source. Claim is appropriately scoped to "consumer products," not overreaching to UI. Consistent with companion findings (prototypicality↔convention synergy); contradicts none of the data-viz findings.

#### V98 `a41df386` — **REFUTED** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "For websites, the relationship between visual complexity and aesthetic preference is NEGATIVE-LINEAR, not Berlyne's classic inverted-U — websites are already complex stimuli sitting on the descending side of the curve, so simpler = more liked (qualifies the inverted-U as not universally applicable to web UI)."

**Source:** https://research.google.com/pubs/archive/38315.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Although the theory of Berlyne (1974) ... would suggest an inverted U-shaped relation between aesthetics and visual complexity, our data show a linear relation among these variables."

*Evidence:* SOURCE FIDELITY IS PARTIALLY OK BUT THE CLAIM OVERGENERALIZES ONE STUDY AND ADDS AN UNSUPPORTED MECHANISM.

1) Source ID and quote: research.google.com/pubs/archive/38315.pdf is Tuch, Presslaber, Stöcklin, Opwis & Bargas-Avila (2012), "The role of visual complexity and prototypicality regarding first impression of websites" (Int. J. Human-Computer Studies). The abstract text I extracted confirms authorship/topic. The supporting quote ("our data show a linear relation") is genuine, and independent reviews confirm Tuch's relation is NEGATIVE (simpler = more appealing). So the narrow source-specific reading is accurate — but it is ONE study (119 screenshots), not a settled web-UI law.

2) The strong framing "For websites, the relationship IS negative-linear" is CONTRADICTED by equally/more-credible peers: Reinecke et al. (2013, CHI; 450 websites, 548 participants — far larger than Tuch) found low-complexity sites are "similarly liked to those with a medium complexity" (a plateau, not a clean negative-linear descent). Güçlütürk et al. found a U-SHAPED complexity-aesthetics relation attributed to two user groups (individual differences). Geissler et al. found HIGH visual complexity can yield good first impressions and behavioral intentions. The web-complexity literature is explicitly mixed (linear / plateau / U-shaped / inverted-U), so "the relationship is negative-linear" cannot be graded [STRONG].

3) The mechanism "websites are already complex stimuli sitting on the descending side of the curve" is an interpretive GLOSS not established by the source — Tuch reports a linear relation within its tested complexity range; it does not demonstrate that all websites occupy the post-peak (descending) limb of Berlyne's curve. This is the claimant's reconciliation, presented as fact, and goes beyond the quote.

NET: The defensible, well-supported part is the hedge — "the inverted-U is not universally applicable to web UI" (corroborated by Michailidou et al., who state webpage attractiveness "cannot be graphically described by an inverted U-shape as Berlyne proposed"). The refutable part is presenting "negative-linear" as THE web relationship: it is a single-study shape, not replicated (Reinecke's larger study and Güçlütürk diverge), so it should be labeled CONTESTED, not a strong settled finding. Correct synthesis: simpler/lower complexity TENDS to be preferred for websites and the inverted-U is not universal, but the precise functional form (linear vs plateau vs U-shaped) is unresolved and the "descending-limb" explanation is unproven.

Consistency note: this does NOT conflict with companion findings; it aligns with the fluency/restraint synergy (lower complexity → easier processing → more liked). The conflict is internal to the claim's overstatement, not with the broader UX corpus.
*Counter-source:* Reinecke et al. (2013, CHI, "Predicting Users' First Impressions of Website Aesthetics," 450 sites / 548 participants) — low ≈ medium complexity equally liked (plateau, not clean negative-linear); Güçlütürk et al. — U-shaped relation driven by individual differences; Geissler et al. — high visual complexity yields good first impressions; Michailidou et al. — webpage attractiveness "cannot be described by an inverted U-shape." Together these show the web complexity-aesthetics function is contested, refuting the universalized "negative-linear" framing and the unsupported "descending-side-of-the-curve" mechanism.

*Evidence:* Claim is a near-verbatim restatement of the primary source abstract. The "genuine affect not merely cognitive judgment" reading is precisely what facial EMG demonstrates (physiological signal independent of self-report) and is the paper's own interpretation. The "hedonic marking of fluency" framework is correctly attributed (Winkielman & Cacioppo 2001 "Mind at ease puts a smile on the face"; Winkielman et al. 2003) — the claim invokes it as the account the experiment supports, which is accurate, even though the exact phrase isn't in this paper's abstract. Searched for contradiction: found only complementary qualification (Albrecht & Carbon 2014 Fluency Amplification Model; a 2019 JESP dual-process finding that fluency also amplifies existing valence) — these nuance the pure hedonic-marking account but do NOT contradict the specific finding that prototypes elicit positive zygomaticus responses; no failed replication of the EMG result found. ONE CAVEAT (not refuting): the terminal clause "grounds the aesthetic-usability and beauty-from-clarity effects" is a synthesis bridge — this paper tested abstract patterns, not UI usability/trust; the extrapolation is theoretically standard (Reber, Schwarz & Winkielman 2004 extends fluency to aesthetic pleasure) and is worded as the framework grounding those effects rather than this single experiment proving them. Source current as foundational aesthetics-psychology theory, not outdated; not marketing.
*Counter-source:* No credible refutation found. Nearest qualifying literature: Albrecht & Carbon (2014) Fluency Amplification Model and a 2019 J. Experimental Social Psychology dual-process study (fluency amplifies valence, not only adds positive affect) — these complement rather than contradict the Experiment 3 zygomaticus finding.

#### V102 `a4ccc43a` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The complexity-appeal relationship is NOT a strong Berlyne inverted-U for websites; high complexity produces the largest drop in appeal while low and medium complexity are liked about equally (closer to monotonic decline). This qualifies/contradicts a naive inverted-U and aligns with Tuch et al. 2012. [STRONG]"

**Source:** https://iis.seas.harvard.edu/papers/2013/reinecke13aesthetics.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "a high visual complexity results in the largest decrease in appeal, but websites with low levels of complexity are similarly liked to those with a medium complexity."

*Evidence:* Direct PDF extraction of the primary source (Reinecke et al. 2013, CHI; N=548 raters, 450 websites; peer-reviewed top-tier HCI venue) confirms the exact discussion-section sentence: "our results correspond to those presented in a recent paper by Tuch et al. [35], who found that the relationship between visual complexity and appeal is not necessarily represented by a strong U-shape. In fact, our analyses show that a high visual complexity results in the largest decrease in appeal, but websites with low levels of complexity are similarly liked to those with a medium complexity." This maps point-for-point onto the claim (not-strong-U / largest drop at high / low≈medium / aligns with Tuch 2012). The claim is a faithful, near-verbatim paraphrase, not an overreach. It is appropriately hedged ("NOT a STRONG inverted-U," "closer to monotonic decline," "qualifies/contradicts a NAIVE inverted-U"), matching the authors' own framing. The broader literature is genuinely mixed (Berlyne/Geissler inverted-U vs. linear; the 2020 CHI follow-up dl.acm.org/10.1145/3313831.3376602 notes "some found inverse linear, some inverted-U, some no relationship"), but the claim's hedge explicitly accommodates this and does not assert the inverted-U is universally false, so the mixed literature does not contradict it. The [STRONG] label is justified for this specific empirical finding (two converging controlled studies: Reinecke 2013 + Tuch et al. 2012, IJHCS). Minor caveat: "monotonic decline" is strictly a plateau-then-drop (low≈medium, not low>medium), but the "closer to monotonic" wording handles this. No grounds to refute.
*Counter-source:* 2020 CHI follow-up "Relationship Between Visual Complexity and Aesthetics of Webpages" (dl.acm.org/doi/abs/10.1145/3313831.3376602) confirms the website complexity-appeal literature is mixed, but does not overturn the Reinecke/Tuch finding; it situates it. Berlyne (1971) inverted-U remains the contrasting model the claim explicitly qualifies rather than denies.

#### V103 `a50be5ad` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "A single fluency mechanism parsimoniously unifies multiple aesthetic-preference phenomena — preference for familiarity, symmetry, prototypicality, and high figure-ground contrast all arise because each variable eases (facilitates) processing and thereby triggers positive affect. [STRONG for the mechanism; the listed perceptual cues themselves are CONVENTION-to-STRONG depending on cue]"

**Source:** https://pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkielman/Processing_Fluency_as_the_Source_of_Experiences_PSYCHE-2002.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "the notion of fluency as an indicator of cognitive progress may provide a parsimonious account for a variety of preference phenomena, such as preference for familiarity, symmetry, prototypicality, high figure-ground contrast, and many others. In this account, all these phenomena occur because the respective variable facilitates processing and thereby triggers positive affect."

*Evidence:* QUOTE FIT (verbatim): The claim restates the supporting quote almost word-for-word, and the quote is genuine to the Winkielman/Reber fluency program. The same unification is stated explicitly in the canonical primary source — Reber, Schwarz & Winkielman (2004), "Processing Fluency and Aesthetic Pleasure: Is Beauty in the Perceiver's Processing Experience?", Personality and Social Psychology Review 8(4):364–382 — which reviews "figural goodness, figure–ground contrast, stimulus repetition, symmetry, and prototypicality" and traces them all to processing fluency. So the claim is NOT a misread.

SOURCE QUALITY (sufficient for STRONG-on-mechanism): Winkielman/Reber/Schwarz are the originators of fluency-aesthetics theory; the mechanism has CONTROLLED experimental support, not just theory — Reber, Winkielman & Schwarz (1998, Psychological Science) manipulated figure-ground contrast/symmetry/priming and got higher "pretty"/lower "ugly" ratings; Winkielman, Halberstadt, Fazendeiro & Catty (2006, Psychological Science, "Prototypes Are Attractive Because They Are Easy on the Mind") directly tested fluency as the mediator of prototype preference. The claim's labeling — [STRONG for the mechanism; cues CONVENTION-to-STRONG] — is calibrated, not overreach.

SKEPTICAL CHECK (critiques qualify, do not refute): The fluency-amplification model (Albrecht & Carbon, 2014, Acta Psychologica) and the dual-process PIA model (Graf & Landwehr, 2015, Pers Soc Psychol Rev) dispute whether fluency carries INHERENT positive affect (amplification) vs. adds a second disfluency-reward route. BUT, per the amplification authors themselves, the hedonic and amplification predictions diverge only for negatively-valenced stimuli and CONVERGE for positive/neutral stimuli — which is the regime web/UI sits in. So these critiques refine the mechanism's nature; they do not overturn the parsimonious unification for the design context. The source's own hedge ("may provide a parsimonious account") is honest theory-language, and the claim does not assert the unification is beyond dispute.

OTHER CHECKS: Not outdated (foundational theory still cited through the 2020s); not marketing/press-release; not cherry-picked. CONSISTENCY: aligns with companion findings — fluency via high figure-ground contrast/clarity is luminance/contrast-driven legibility, synergistic (not contradictory) with "luminance not hue drives eye comfort" and with prototypicality↔convention. No conflict found.

NET: well-supported, current, source strength matches claim strength. Verdict stands as not refuted; only caveat to carry forward in synthesis is that "fluency triggers positive affect" is more precisely "fluency amplifies/marks valence (positive in the typical UI case)."
*Counter-source:* Albrecht & Carbon (2014) Fluency Amplification Model, Acta Psychologica; Graf & Landwehr (2015) dual-process PIA model, Pers Soc Psychol Rev — both qualify but converge with the hedonic account for positive/neutral (UI-typical) stimuli; corroborating primary: Reber, Schwarz & Winkielman (2004) PSPR 8:364–382; Reber, Winkielman & Schwarz (1998) Psychological Science; Winkielman et al. (2006) Psychological Science.

#### V104 `a50ec090` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The fluency-to-liking effect is a misattribution: it is mediated by the affective reaction the ease produces, and it is eliminated when people are led to attribute that feeling to an external source (e.g., background music) — direct evidence that fluency is experienced as 'I like this object' rather than 'this was easy to process.'"

**Source:** https://dornsife.usc.edu/norbert-schwarz/wp-content/uploads/sites/231/2023/11/04_pspr_reber_et_al_beauty.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The impact of fluency on evaluative judgments is apparently mediated by the elicited affective reactions and hence eliminated when the informational value of the affective reaction is called into question through misattribution manipulations (Winkielman & Fazendeiro, 2003)."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported, accurate, and from a top primary source. The quote (Reber, Schwarz & Winkielman 2004, PSPR) directly states fluency's effect on evaluation is "mediated by the elicited affective reactions and hence eliminated when the informational value of the affective reaction is called into question through misattribution manipulations." Independent search confirms the cited study (Winkielman & Fazendeiro 2003) used EXACTLY the background-music misattribution paradigm the claim names — an ambiguous new-age piece at half-speed; participants told reactions "might be influenced by background music." The "e.g., background music" example is therefore accurate, not fabricated. Critically, the original study found a dissociation that strengthens the claim: attributing the AFFECTIVE reaction to music eliminated the fluency→liking effect, but attributing the FLUENCY/ease to music did NOT — direct support that the operative experience is "I like this" (affect) rather than "this was easy to process" (metacognition), precisely as claimed. Source quality (peer-reviewed PSPR review + underlying study) matches claim strength; not marketing/forum/cherry-picked. No contradicting evidence found; no conflict with companion data-viz findings (different domain). Only minor quibble: "direct evidence" is a moderation-based demonstration, the standard accepted form in the affect-as-information tradition — not grounds for refutation.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. WebSearch of Reber/Schwarz/Winkielman + Winkielman & Fazendeiro 2003 corroborated every element, including the music-misattribution detail and the affect-vs-fluency dissociation.

#### V105 `a511ca0e` — **REFUTED** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "Visual complexity is a much stronger driver of first-impression appeal than colorfulness; appeal drops sharply as complexity rises, while colorfulness contributes only a minor effect. Actionable: restraint/low visual complexity matters more for intuitive liking than how colorful a UI is. [STRONG]"

**Source:** https://iis.seas.harvard.edu/papers/2013/reinecke13aesthetics.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Our results show that visual complexity accounts for a signiﬁcantly larger amount of the variance in the observed ratings on visual appeal than colorfulness."

*Evidence:* PARTIAL OVERREACH — refuted on specifics. The supporting quote ("visual complexity accounts for a significantly larger amount of the variance than colorfulness") licenses ONLY the comparative-variance claim, which is sound. But the claim bolts on "appeal drops sharply as complexity rises," and Reinecke et al. 2013's actual complexity–appeal relationship is an INVERTED-U, not a sharp monotonic decline. Two independent characterizations of the primary data converge: (a) "inverted U-shape where stimuli with low or high complexity are less preferred than moderately complex ones"; "most participants did not mind a low level of visual complexity but disliked highly complex websites" — so the drop is concentrated at the HIGH end, the peak is MODERATE complexity, not minimal; (b) "strong linear tendencies: simpler webpages were only slightly less aesthetically preferred than more complex ones; low complexity is similarly liked to medium" — i.e., the slope is gentle ("only slightly"), not "sharp," and low complexity is roughly tied with moderate rather than the winner. This directly contradicts both "drops sharply" and the actionable "low visual complexity" framing (moderate, not low, is the peak). Separately, the [STRONG] tag overstates: Reinecke's signature finding is that the optimal complexity/colorfulness level VARIES strongly by demographic (country, age, education, gender) — e.g., Chilean participants disliked simple/colorless designs — so there is no universal "restraint = best" law to label [STRONG]; the genuinely strong result is narrowly the variance comparison. The "colorfulness is only a minor effect" half IS faithful (Reinecke's own framing: colorfulness "only plays a minor role"). Net: source quality is high (n=548 raters, 450 sites, replicated), the comparative core is correct, but the claim mischaracterizes the complexity-appeal shape and over-universalizes the prescription, so it cannot stand as worded at [STRONG].
*Counter-source:* Reinecke et al. 2013 (CHI) abstract/summary via dash.harvard.edu and secondary characterizations: inverted-U complexity-appeal relationship (moderate peak; high complexity disliked; low complexity nearly as liked as moderate) and strong demographic variation in the optimum (no universal restraint law). Corroborated by the "Relationship Between Visual Complexity and Aesthetics of Webpages" review (researchgate 341687982: "inverted U-shaped... simpler webpages only slightly less preferred than more complex ones; low complexity similarly liked to medium; colorfulness only plays a minor role") and the larger follow-up Reinecke & Gajos 2014 "Quantifying Visual Preferences Around the World" (eecs.harvard.edu/~kgajos/papers/2014/reinecke14visual.pdf) emphasizing demographic-dependent optima. PDF of the primary paper could not be text-extracted (CID-encoded; no pdftotext/pdf libs on host), so direction was confirmed via convergent secondary sources rather than the primary text directly.

**Source:** https://pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkiel/reber-winkielman-schwarz-Fluency-PS-1998.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "In postexperimental interviews, participants expressed surprise that priming pictures had been presented... the drawings preceded by matching primes were judged as prettier (M = 4.66, SD = 1.10) than the drawings preceded by mismatching primes (M = 4.39, SD = 1.14), t(52) = 2.43, p < .05. Moreover, the drawings preceded by matching primes were recognized faster... This enhanced perceptual fluency is then misattributed to liking."

*Evidence:* Claim verified against the primary source (Reber, Winkielman & Schwarz, 1998, Psychological Science 9(1):45-48) plus independent corroboration. CONFIRMED: (a) 25-ms prime — WebSearch quotes "a drawing was presented for 25 ms immediately before each target picture... matching prime / mismatching prime"; (b) prettiness M=4.66 vs 4.39, t(52)=2.43, p<.05 — present in the supporting quote AND independently returned by WebSearch (4.66 vs 4.39); (c) matched primes recognized faster + participants unaware — triple-confirmed: supporting quote ("recognized faster"; "surprise that priming pictures had been presented"), WebSearch ("recognized the object sooner when preceded by a matching outline"), and the authors' own PSYCHE-2002 review (text extracted verbatim from PDF: "Pictures primed by matched contours were recognized faster, indicating higher fluency, and were liked more... participants were unaware of the fluency manipulation"); (d) the misattribution-of-fluency-to-liking interpretation is the paper's central thesis. NOT INDEPENDENTLY CONFIRMED but internally consistent: the exact latencies 1,470 vs 2,415 ms and t(52)=4.44, p<.001 — the 1998 PDF is a scanned image (no text layer; LZW/ASCII85 streams decode to image data, not text), and secondary sources restate the recognition result only qualitatively. However df=52 matches the prettiness test (within-subjects N=53), t=4.44 at 52 df mathematically gives p<.001, the effect direction is triple-confirmed, and the supporting quote's ellipsis truncates exactly where those numbers appear in the original. The exact-match of the prettiness statistics between quote and independent search indicates faithful transcription. No contradicting or qualifying evidence found; source quality (peer-reviewed, foundational, ~3000+ citations) matches the claim's [STRONG] strength. The claim does not overreach the quote — it states what this controlled experiment found and what its authors argue.

#### V108 `a6083ae7` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Visual complexity is a much stronger driver of first-impression appeal than colorfulness; appeal drops sharply as complexity rises, while colorfulness contributes only a minor effect. Actionable: restraint/low visual complexity matters more for intuitive liking than how colorful a UI is. [STRONG]"

**Source:** https://iis.seas.harvard.edu/papers/2013/reinecke13aesthetics.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Our results show that visual complexity accounts for a signiﬁcantly larger amount of the variance in the observed ratings on visual appeal than colorfulness."

*Evidence:* Core claim is well-supported by a strong primary source. The supporting quote is VERBATIM in Reinecke et al. 2013 (CHI), confirmed by extracting the PDF text: "Our results show that visual complexity accounts for a significantly larger amount of the variance in the observed ratings on visual appeal than colorfulness." The study is controlled, large (450 sites, 548+242 participants, 500ms exposure, linear mixed-effects model, adj. R2=.48), peer-reviewed at CHI, 300+ citations, and independently corroborated by Tuch et al. 2012 (high complexity -> more negative first impressions). [STRONG] is justified for the central thesis (complexity > colorfulness; restraint/low complexity aids first-impression liking). Consistent with companion fluency/restraint synthesis — no conflict with the data-viz findings (luminance/blue-light/dark-mode/data-ink).

TWO PRECISION CAVEATS (qualifications, not refutations):
(1) "appeal drops sharply as complexity rises" overstates a monotonic decline. The paper's Discussion says the opposite of a smooth slope: "a high visual complexity results in the largest decrease in appeal, but websites with low levels of complexity are similarly liked to those with a medium complexity," and explicitly that it is NOT a strong U-shape. Actual shape: low ≈ medium > high; the drop is concentrated only at the high end. The actionable ("restraint/low complexity") still holds because low is among the most-liked.
(2) "colorfulness contributes only a minor effect" mildly overreaches the quote, which only supports "significantly larger ... than colorfulness," not "minor/negligible." Colorfulness is one of two PRIMARY features. Critically, the paper's own conclusion qualifies universality: "the importance of these two website characteristics is not universal, but dependent on users' demographic backgrounds" (education interacted with colorfulness; age with complexity). The 2014 follow-up (Reinecke & Gajos, CHI 2014, ~40k participants, 2.4M ratings) shows the optimal colorfulness/complexity varies by gender, age, education, country — females and less-educated users weight colorfulness far more. So the relative ranking is the population AVERAGE, not invariant.

Net: claim survives. Recommend softening synthesis wording to "high complexity hurts first-impression appeal most (low ≈ medium)" and "colorfulness is a smaller, demographically-moderated driver," and noting the average-vs-segment caveat.
*Counter-source:* Reinecke et al. 2013 (CHI) own Discussion + Conclusion (effect "not universal," low≈medium complexity, "not a strong U-shape"); Reinecke & Gajos 2014 "Quantifying Visual Preferences Around the World" (CHI) showing demographic moderation of colorfulness/complexity optima

The supporting quote faithfully states the statistic and the "highly reliable" conclusion. Source quality matches the claim's strength; not outdated, not marketing. Human-Computer Studies, "Evaluating the consistency of immediate aesthetic perceptions of web pages" — which found the high correlations depend on AGGREGATING across participants; intra-user (individual-level) consistency is considerably lower (notably in the 50ms condition). So r=.97 reflects stability of MEAN ratings / rank-order, not strong cross-person consensus. The claim mitigates this by explicitly saying "mean appeal ratings," and the aggregation critique bites the 50ms claims harder than Study 1's 500ms test-retest. Net: the specific statistic and reliability conclusion are supported; only the "idiosyncratic" gloss needs the aggregation qualifier. Could not retrieve a verbatim primary-text ".97 in Study 1" line (paywalled; secondary R²~.94 used), hence medium confidence.

#### V112 `a6a12e95` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Processing fluency is itself hedonically marked — high fluency is subjectively experienced as a positive affective state, a claim the authors say is backed by psychophysiological evidence (not merely inferred from liking ratings)."

**Source:** https://dornsife.usc.edu/norbert-schwarz/wp-content/uploads/sites/231/2023/11/04_pspr_reber_et_al_beauty.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Second, processing fluency is itself hedonically marked and high fluency is subjectively experienced as positive, as indicated by psychophysiological findings. Third, processing fluency feeds into judgments of aesthetic appreciation because people draw on their subjective experience in making evaluative judgments, unless the informational value of the experience is called into question."

*Evidence:* Claim is accurately supported by the quote and source. The supplied quote from Reber, Schwarz & Winkielman (2004, Personality and Social Psychology Review 8(4):364–382 — a primary, peer-reviewed, top-tier source) states nearly verbatim: "processing fluency is itself hedonically marked and high fluency is subjectively experienced as positive, as indicated by psychophysiological findings." The claim is attributive ("a claim the authors say is backed by psychophysiological evidence"), which is exactly what the text asserts. The psychophysiological evidence is real and correctly described: Winkielman & Cacioppo (2001), "Mind at ease puts a smile on the face: Psychophysiological evidence that processing facilitation elicits positive affect," Journal of Personality and Social Psychology 81:989–1000 — used facial EMG showing easy-to-process stimuli elicited higher zygomaticus major (smiling-muscle) activity, an independent physiological index of positive affect (confirmed via PubMed 11761320 and the authors' UCSD abstract page). The parenthetical "not merely inferred from liking ratings" is accurate: EMG is a physiological measure distinct from liking ratings (self-reports paralleled but did not constitute the EMG evidence). No contradicting source found that disputes the attribution. Debates that fluency-affect can be context/expectation-dependent qualify the broader theory but do not refute the narrowly-scoped descriptive claim. Source quality (primary, top journal, foundational and highly cited) matches the claim's modest strength. Note: direct PDF text extraction failed (flate-compressed streams), but WebSearch independently verified both the Reber et al. 2004 paper and the Winkielman & Cacioppo 2001 psychophysiological study with its EMG methodology.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. Winkielman & Cacioppo (2001, JPSP 81:989-1000; PubMed 11761320) independently confirms the psychophysiological (facial EMG / zygomaticus) basis the claim attributes to the authors.

#### V113 `a6b9534a` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Higher figure-ground contrast (clarity) directly raises aesthetic ratings: in Experiment 2 stimuli with greater contrast were judged more pretty and less ugly (prettiness increased with contrast for both dark-on-white and light-on-black, p<.001; ugliness decreased with contrast, p<.05), and the effect was a preference for contrast itself, not for darker graytones. This is the most directly actionable UI move (clarity/high contrast --> liked more)."

**Source:** https://pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkiel/reber-winkielman-schwarz-Fluency-PS-1998.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Stimuli were judged as more pretty, and less ugly, the higher the contrast... This finding shows that the effects are due to a preference for contrast rather than darker graytones per se."

*Evidence:* SUPPORTED by primary source. Reber, Winkielman & Schwarz (1998), Psychological Science 9(1):45-48. The official abstract states verbatim: "In Exp. 2, perceptual fluency was manipulated by figure-ground contrast. Stimuli were judged as more pretty, and less ugly, the higher the contrast" (pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkiel/abs_fluency-PS-1998.htm) — matches claim and supporting quote exactly. The dual-polarity design is independently confirmed: the Exp 2 figure panels show prettiness/ugliness for "gray and black circles on a white background" AND "gray and white circles on a black background" — exactly the dark-on-white + light-on-black contrast manipulation. That design is what licenses "preference for contrast, not darker graytones" (on white bg, more contrast = darker; on black bg, more contrast = lighter; prettiness rose in both polarities). The 2004 PSPR review (text-extracted from the primary PDF) corroborates: "circles with high figure-ground contrast were judged as prettier (or less ugly...)... Increased fluency resulted in judgments of higher prettiness as well as judgments of lower ugliness." Source is primary, top-tier, foundational, replicated — not outdated, not marketing. TWO QUALIFICATIONS, neither fatal: (1) Exact p-values (p<.001 prettiness, p<.05 ugliness) could NOT be verified — the 1998 PDF is a scanned/CCITT image so its stats tables aren't text-extractable; the values are plausible and consistent with the documented prettiness>ugliness asymmetry but are the claimant's specifics, not present in any accessible quote. (2) The actionable gloss ("most directly actionable UI move / high contrast --> liked more") OVERREACHES: the same lab's Reber & Schwarz (2001) follow-up (per the 2004 review) found figure-ground contrast "influenced aesthetic judgments only at short exposure durations, but not at the duration of 10 sec" — "high contrast enhances liking for patterns shown briefly, but not for identical patterns shown longer." Exp 2 stimuli were single circles shown ~1 sec; UI is sustained-viewing, the regime where the aesthetic-preference effect attenuates. So the narrow finding rates [STRONG]; the "best UI move" extrapolation is [CONVENTION]. Empirical core survives; recommend the skill state the finding faithfully and not oversell brief-exposure circle data as a sustained-UI liking law (and lean on legibility/WCAG luminance evidence for the UI contrast case, consistent with companion data-viz findings).
*Counter-source:* Reber & Schwarz (2001), as summarized in Reber, Schwarz & Winkielman (2004), Personality and Social Psychology Review 8(4):364-382 — contrast affected aesthetic judgments only at short (<=1 sec) exposures, not at 10 sec, qualifying the UI-generalization gloss.

#### V114 `a6cb4061` — **upheld** · confidence: medium

Actionable: restraint/low visual complexity matters more for intuitive liking than how colorful a UI is. [STRONG]"

**Source:** https://iis.seas.harvard.edu/papers/2013/reinecke13aesthetics.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Our results show that visual complexity accounts for a signiﬁcantly larger amount of the variance in the observed ratings on visual appeal than colorfulness."

*Evidence:* Primary source (Reinecke et al., CHI 2013, "Predicting Users' First Impressions of Website Aesthetics," N=548 raters, 450 websites, 500ms exposure) supports the claim's core verbatim. (a) "visual complexity accounts for a significantly larger amount of the variance in the observed ratings on visual appeal than colorfulness" — backs "stronger driver than colorfulness" and is the exact supporting quote. (b) "perceived colorfulness only plays a minor role in people's first impression of appeal" — backs "colorfulness contributes only a minor effect" near-verbatim. (c) "ratings of appeal are significantly negatively affected by an increase in visual complexity" — backs "appeal drops as complexity rises." Model adj. R² = .48 (with demographics). Peer-reviewed CHI, 300+ citations, a published replication exists; corroborated by controlled experiments (Tuch et al. 2009/2012) that manipulated complexity directly. TWO QUALIFICATIONS (flag, do not refute): (1) Shape is not uniformly "drops sharply as complexity rises" — paper states "websites with low levels of complexity are similarly liked to those with a medium complexity," with the largest decrease only at HIGH complexity, i.e. an inverted-U/threshold pattern, not a clean monotonic slope. "Sharply as complexity rises" overstates the low-to-medium range. (2) [STRONG]="controlled studies" is imprecise for THIS source: complexity/colorfulness were measured via image analysis on sampled real sites, not experimentally manipulated — it is a large robust correlational/predictive study; the causal "driver" framing leans on corroborating controlled experiments elsewhere. Neither qualification contradicts the actionable conclusion (low visual complexity matters more than colorfulness for intuitive liking), which is verbatim-supported and consistent with the fluency/restraint synthesis.
*Counter-source:* Reinecke et al. 2013 (CHI), p. results: "websites with low levels of complexity are similarly liked to those with a medium complexity" (relationship is inverted-U/threshold, not monotonic — qualifies "drops sharply as complexity rises"); study design is a large online OBSERVATIONAL/predictive model (measured, not manipulated, predictors) — qualifies the [STRONG]=controlled-study label. https://iis.seas.harvard.edu/papers/2013/reinecke13aesthetics.pdf ; dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/2470654.2481281

#### V115 `a73a7119` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Neither typicality alone nor novelty alone correlates significantly with aesthetic preference; each becomes highly significant only after statistically controlling for the other (a classic statistical suppression effect), so MAYA must be modeled as a two-variable balance rather than a single 'familiarity = liking' rule."

**Source:** https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/12648393/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Direct correlations between both variables and aesthetic preference were not significant, but each relationship became highly significant when the influence of the other variable was partialed out"

*Evidence:* Claim is verbatim-supported by the primary source. PubMed 12648393 = Hekkert, Snelders & van Wieringen (2003), "'Most advanced, yet acceptable': typicality and novelty as joint predictors of aesthetic preference in industrial design," British Journal of Psychology 94(Pt 1):111-124. The abstract states: "typicality and novelty are jointly and equally effective in explaining aesthetic preference, but suppress each other's effect. Direct correlations between both variables and aesthetic preference were not significant, but each relationship became highly significant when the influence of the other variable was partialled out." This maps one-to-one onto every component of the claim: (a) no significant zero-order correlation for either variable, (b) significance emerges only after partialling out the other, (c) the authors themselves call it mutual suppression, (d) the paper's explicit thesis is that MAYA is a two-variable balance ("prefer novel designs as long as the novelty does not affect typicality, or conversely"). Source quality matches claim strength: peer-reviewed primary study in a reputable journal, not marketing/forum/press-release. The supporting quote is accurate, not cherry-picked. Not outdated — this remains the canonical empirical citation for MAYA. No credible contradicting source found. Caveat (does not refute): study objects were industrial-design products (cars, telephones, sanitary fittings), not web/UI specifically, but the claim makes no web/UI-specific overreach and is consistent with the broader prototypicality/mere-exposure literature.

#### V117 `a79a1ef9` — **upheld** · confidence: high

*Evidence:* The quote (Winkielman, PSYCHE 2002) directly states the claimed three-step mechanism: high fluency -> positively-marked subjective experience -> participants interpret it as their reaction to the stimulus -> more positive evaluations. The claim is essentially a paraphrase, not an overreach. Crucially, the misattribution findings surfaced in search (telling participants their affect came from background music eliminates the fluency-driven liking boost) CONFIRM the 'interpret as a reaction to the stimulus' step rather than contradict it. Qualifications exist but only refine, not refute: an ongoing 'hedonic marking vs. amplification' debate (S0022103119302732, 2020 JESP) and evidence that fluency's positivity is moderated by motivational context and can be overshadowed by familiarity in mixed designs. These narrow the 'inherently positive by default' framing but leave the fluency->affect->liking mechanism intact, especially for aesthetic/UI stimuli (the relevant domain). Source is the originating author; theory is foundational and still actively cited (not outdated, not marketing). *Evidence:* VERIFIED, NOT REFUTED. The DOI 10.1111/j.1467-9280.2006.01785.x is Winkielman, Halberstadt, Fazendeiro & Catty (2006), "Prototypes Are Attractive Because They Are Easy on the Mind," Psychological Science 17(9):799-806 (confirmed via PubMed 16984298 and Sage). Crucially, the experiments used random-dot patterns and geometric shapes (not faces), so fluency drove attractiveness on stimuli with zero mate-value relevance — directly supporting the claim that the mechanism is processing ease, not evolutionary mate-value/category-signaling per se. Minor: the research-question prompt mis-cited the 3rd author as "Fahrenfort" (actual: Fazendeiro), but the claim itself names no authors, so unaffected.
*Counter-source:* No credible refuting source found. Closest qualifier is the general methodological critique of measured-mediator designs (Bullock, Green & Ha, 2010, J Pers Soc Psychol, "Yes, but what's the mechanism?"), which tempers but does not overturn the mediation claim; the convergent experimental-fluency-manipulation literature (Reber, Winkielman & Schwarz, 1998/2004) reinforces the causal direction.

Human-Computer Studies 70(11); the research.google PDF is the author manuscript — extracted full text directly). The supporting quote is verbatim from the paper's conclusion: "websites of high PT and low VC are perceived as being the most beautiful, whereas websites of low PT and high VC trigger the worst first impression." (1) Faithfully supported by quote — the claim restates the paper's own stated conclusion, not an overreach. (2) "Across all exposure times" holds: Study 1 (50/500/1000 ms) + Study 2 (17/33/50 ms) both found significant main effects for VC and PT "at all three levels"; no significant VC x time interaction; the PT x time interaction only made PT's effect GROW with longer exposure, never reversing. Cell means confirm high-PT/low-VC is the top cell at every duration (17ms 4.89, 33ms 4.92, 50ms 5.23, 500ms 5.57, 1000ms 5.58).
