(5) Not marketing/cherry-picked — academic with full stats. So "keep layouts simple" mainly pays off when the design is already prototypical; PT also dominated VC (eta-p-sq .81 vs .58). Also a minor simplification carried over from the paper's own abstract: in Study 1 the literal worst cell was often low-PT/MEDIUM-VC (3.76), not low-PT/high-VC. These nuances should be footnoted but they qualify rather than overturn the claim.
*Counter-source:* No external source refutes the claim. Related but non-contradicting: 2023 CHI "Statistically Controlling for Processing Fluency Reduces the Aesthetic-Usability Effect" targets a different downstream link (aesthetics->usability), not VC/PT->beauty.

No conflict with companion luminance/dark-mode/data-ink findings; reinforces fluency↔clarity/contrast/restraint synergy.
*Counter-source:* Albrecht & Carbon, "The nature of processing fluency: Amplification versus hedonic marking" (ScienceDirect S0022103119302732) — refines but does not refute

#### V125 `a8453d03` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Processing fluency statistically MEDIATES the prototypicality-attractiveness link: prototypes are attractive because they are easier to process, not (only) because of mate-value or category-signaling per se. This is the paper's core causal claim and the mechanism underlying the [STRONG] fluency-liking effect."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1111/j.1467-9280.2006.01785.x (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "fluency mediated the effect of prototypicality on attractiveness"

*Evidence:* Source confirmed as Winkielman, Halberstadt, Fazendeiro & Catty (2006), "Prototypes Are Attractive Because They Are Easy on the Mind," Psychological Science 17(9):799-806 — top-tier peer-reviewed primary source. The supporting quote is verbatim from the abstract; the paper's title itself is the causal claim. Two experiments showed prototypicality predicted both fluency (categorization speed) and attractiveness, with fluency statistically mediating the prototypicality-attractiveness link; Exp 3 (facial EMG) confirmed prototypes evoke fast positive affect. The authors deliberately used abstract dot/geometric patterns (not just faces) to demonstrate generality and argue fluency — not a face-specific mate-value account — is the parsimonious mechanism, so the claim's "not (only) mate-value/category-signaling" framing is faithful. One precision point the claim handles correctly: the abstract states it was PARTIAL mediation ("some effect of prototypicality remained when fluency was controlled"), and the claim's hedge "not (only)" accommodates this rather than overclaiming full mediation. No credible contradicting source found; follow-up work (Halberstadt 2006; PLOS One 2024 neural prototypicality study) extends/supports it. Only standard caveat: statistical mediation is suggestive not definitive of causal mechanism, but the claim accurately represents the paper's own conclusion. Source quality matches claim strength; finding is foundational and not outdated.

#### V126 `a8b98858` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Visual appeal of web homepages can be assessed within 50 ms: mean appeal ratings at 50 ms exposure correlated with ratings at 500 ms exposure at r = .947 (r2 = .897) on the first phase and r = .953 (r2 = .908) on the second, both p < .001 — so a first impression of appeal forms essentially as fast at 50 ms as at 500 ms."

**Source:** https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/01449290500330448 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "It revealed that r = .947, p < .001 (r2 = .897). This result was slightly lower, but comparable to that obtained in Study 2 for the 500 ms condition alone... the correlation for the second phase of both conditions yielded a value of r = .953, p < .001, (r2 = .908)... Findings would thus appear to be as robust with an exposure time of 50 ms as with an exposure time of 500 ms."

*Evidence:* The claim faithfully reports Lindgaard, Fernandes, Dudek & Brown (2006), "Attention web designers: You have 50 milliseconds to make a good first impression!", Behaviour & Information Technology 25(2):115-126 (the exact cited primary source, DOI 10.1080/01449290500330448; peer-reviewed, ~1,000+ citations). The figures r=.947 (r2=.897) and r=.953 (r2=.908), both p<.001, are confirmed verbatim by the supporting quote and corroborated by multiple independent secondary sources. The interpretation is correct: the paper's own wording "the correlation for the second phase of BOTH CONDITIONS yielded r=.953" establishes these are correlations between mean appeal ratings at 50ms and at 500ms across the website stimuli (not test-retest within one condition), matching the claim's "50ms correlated with 500ms" framing and the first-phase/second-phase labeling. The summary clause is the paper's own stated conclusion: "Findings would thus appear to be as robust with an exposure time of 50 ms as with an exposure time of 500 ms." The finding has been replicated/extended (Tuch et al. 2012; exposures down to ~17ms), not contradicted. Only qualification: the correlations are at the AGGREGATE site-mean level (a later replication found lower intra-individual consistency at 50ms while aggregate consistency held) — but the claim correctly says "mean appeal ratings," so it stays within what the data support and does not overclaim a deliberative individual judgment. This qualifies depth, not truth. Label: [STRONG] for aggregate appeal-consistency at brief exposure.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. The only nuance (intra-individual consistency at 50ms < 500ms; correlations are aggregate site-means rather than individual judgments) qualifies but does not refute the claim, which is explicitly about mean ratings. Replications (Tuch et al. 2012, Behaviour & Information Technology; ultra-rapid evaluations down to ~17ms) support rather than dispute it.

#### V127 `a902f2fb` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "A single fluency mechanism parsimoniously unifies multiple aesthetic-preference phenomena — preference for familiarity, symmetry, prototypicality, and high figure-ground contrast all arise because each variable eases (facilitates) processing and thereby triggers positive affect. [STRONG for the mechanism; the listed perceptual cues themselves are CONVENTION-to-STRONG depending on cue]"

**Source:** https://pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkielman/Processing_Fluency_as_the_Source_of_Experiences_PSYCHE-2002.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "the notion of fluency as an indicator of cognitive progress may provide a parsimonious account for a variety of preference phenomena, such as preference for familiarity, symmetry, prototypicality, high figure-ground contrast, and many others. In this account, all these phenomena occur because the respective variable facilitates processing and thereby triggers positive affect."

*Evidence:* Claim is a faithful near-verbatim paraphrase of the cited Winkielman/Schwarz/Fazendeiro/Reber (2002, PSYCHE) quote, and is backed even more assertively by the peer-reviewed companion: Reber, Schwarz & Winkielman (2004, Personality and Social Psychology Review 8(4):364-382) — "Variables known to influence aesthetic judgments—such as figural goodness, figure-ground contrast, stimulus repetition, symmetry, and prototypicality—trace their effects to changes in processing fluency." The mechanism (fluency to positive affect, misattributed as liking) has CONTROLLED-study support, not just theoretical parsimony: Reber, Winkielman & Schwarz (1998, Psychological Science) manipulated fluency independently of stimulus content (contrast/duration/priming) and produced liking effects; Winkielman & Cacioppo (2001, JPSP) measured facial-EMG zygomaticus activity showing fluency elicits positive affect physiologically. This justifies the "[STRONG for the mechanism]" label. Recent literature (Graf & Landwehr 2015 PIA model; Van de Cruys et al. 2024, Phil Trans R Soc B 379:20230326) adds a second "interest/arousal" route from DISfluency and shows expectation/attribution moderate fluency's hedonic sign — but these refine rather than refute the unification of the listed ease-driven cues, and the only live debate (symmetry/prototypicality may also have non-fluency evolutionary drivers) is precisely covered by the claim's own hedge "cues themselves CONVENTION-to-STRONG depending on cue." Source quality matches claim strength (field's primary authorities, primary venues), the work is foundational and still actively extended in 2024, and it is consistent with the companion luminance/contrast and data-ink findings (fluency synergizes with clarity/contrast/restraint).
*Counter-source:* Van de Cruys, Bervoets & Moors (2024), Phil Trans R Soc B 379:20230326, and Graf & Landwehr (2015) add a disfluency-driven interest route and expectation/attribution moderators — qualifications, not refutations of the core unification.

#### V128 `a9371fc5` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "For websites, the relationship between visual complexity and aesthetic preference is NEGATIVE-LINEAR, not Berlyne's classic inverted-U — websites are already complex stimuli sitting on the descending side of the curve, so simpler = more liked (qualifies the inverted-U as not universally applicable to web UI)."

**Source:** https://research.google.com/pubs/archive/38315.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Although the theory of Berlyne (1974) ... would suggest an inverted U-shaped relation between aesthetics and visual complexity, our data show a linear relation among these variables."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary source (extracted full text via PyMuPDF). research.google.com/pubs/archive/38315.pdf = Tuch, Presslaber, Stöcklin, Opwis & Bargas-Avila, "The role of visual complexity and prototypicality regarding first impression of websites," Int. J. Human-Computer Studies, 2012 (Bargas-Avila = Google/YouTube UX Research).

(1) QUOTE IS VERBATIM AND IN CONTEXT — not cherry-picked or misread. Full passage: "Although the theory of Berlyne (1974) and results from experimental studies (Geissler et al., 2006) would suggest an inverted U-shaped relation between aesthetics and visual complexity, our data show a linear relation among these variables. This is not surprising as other studies dealing with websites as stimuli (Michailidou et al., 2008; Pandir and Knight, 2006; Tuch et al., 2009, 2011) also found a negative linear relationship between aesthetics and VC. In the context of Berlyne's theory, it seems that websites are in general rather complex stimuli and therefore lying on the right side of the inverted U-shaped curve. This would explain why there is a negative linear relation instead of a quadratic one."

(2) EVERY ELEMENT OF THE CLAIM IS THE PAPER'S OWN WORDS: "negative-linear, not inverted-U" → "negative linear relationship... instead of a quadratic one"; "websites sit on the descending side of the curve" → "websites are... rather complex stimuli and therefore lying on the right side of the inverted U-shaped curve"; "simpler = more liked" → negative slope. The author did NOT invent the mechanism — it is the paper's stated explanation.

(3) CORROBORATED BY MULTIPLE INDEPENDENT STUDIES, not a lone result: the paper cites four other website studies (Michailidou 2008, Pandir & Knight 2006, Tuch 2009, Tuch 2011) finding the same negative-linear pattern. The companion Reinecke, Yeh, Miratrix, Gajos et al., CHI 2013 (iis.seas.harvard.edu/papers/2013/reinecke13aesthetics.pdf) independently concludes "a high visual complexity results in the largest decrease in appeal," and that complexity-appeal "is not necessarily represented by a strong U-shape."

(4) SOURCE QUALITY MATCHES CLAIM STRENGTH: peer-reviewed IJHCS + peer-reviewed ACM CHI — primary empirical HCI sources, not marketing or forum speculation.

(5) APPROPRIATELY HEDGED: the claim says inverted-U is "not universally applicable to web UI" — it does NOT overclaim that Berlyne is wrong generally, matching the paper's own scoping.

MINOR NUANCE (refines, does not refute): Reinecke 2013 found low and medium complexity were SIMILARLY liked, with the drop concentrated at high complexity — so the relation is monotonic-decreasing-with-a-flattening-low-end rather than a perfectly straight line. The claim's own "descending side of the curve" framing already absorbs this, so it stands. Also consistent with our companion UX findings (simplicity/restraint, prototypicality favors convention) — no conflict; a synergy.
*Counter-source:* Reinecke et al., CHI 2013 (corroborating, mild refinement): low and medium complexity rated similarly, drop concentrated at high complexity — monotonic-decreasing rather than perfectly straight-line; does not refute. Geissler et al. 2006, cited within the source itself, found inverted-U — but for non-website stimuli, which is exactly why the claim is scoped to web UI.

#### V129 `a9a8b822` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Processing/perceptual fluency causally increases liking even when fluency is manipulated by means OTHER than repetition (visual priming, figure-ground contrast, exposure duration) — across three experiments fluency raised preference and the experience of fluency was affectively positive. This is foundational support for the fluency-->positive-affect-->liking chain underpinning intuitive aesthetic preference."

**Source:** https://pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkiel/reber-winkielman-schwarz-Fluency-PS-1998.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "We conclude (a) that perceptual fluency increases liking and (b) that the experience of fluency is affectively positive, and hence attributed to positive but not to negative features, as reflected in a differential impact on positive and negative judgments."

*Evidence:* Claim verified against primary source. The Winkielman-hosted abstract (pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkielman/abs_fluency-PS-1998.htm) confirms verbatim: "Three experiments examined the effects of perceptual fluency on affective judgments. Exp 1: matching vs non-matching PRIME; Exp 2: figure-ground CONTRAST; Exp 3: presentation DURATION." All three are non-repetition manipulations, exactly as the claim states ("means OTHER than repetition: visual priming, figure-ground contrast, exposure duration"). The supporting quote is the paper's verbatim conclusion: fluency increases liking, and the experience of fluency is affectively positive (attributed to positive not negative features, differential impact). Source is the actual peer-reviewed paper, Psychological Science 1998, vol 9, pp 45-48 (DOI 10.1111/1467-9280.00008) — top-tier journal, primary, heavily cited; it directly grounds the Reber/Schwarz/Winkielman 2004 PSPR fluency-aesthetics framework. Adversarial search surfaced only QUALIFICATIONS, not contradictions: (a) boundary conditions where fluency amplification fails for very negative stimuli — itself consistent with the 1998 paper's positive/negative asymmetry; (b) a small/possibly-absent color-contrast effect with publication-bias concern, but for TRUTH judgments (different domain, not liking); (c) a hedonic-marking-vs-amplification mechanism debate (2019) that refines why fluency feels positive without denying it does. The claim correctly frames the paper as "foundational support" rather than settled universal law. Verdict: well-supported, source quality matches claim strength, properly an aesthetic-preference [STRONG] (controlled experiments) finding. Caveat for the skill's synthesis: cite boundary conditions (effect strongest for positively-valenced/neutral stimuli; do not overgeneralize to truth judgments) so it is not overclaimed as universal. Consistent with companion findings: fluency<->clarity/contrast/restraint synergy holds; no conflict with the luminance/blue-light/dark-mode data-viz results.
*Counter-source:* No credible source refutes the core claim. Adjacent qualifications only: Schwarz et al. 2025 revisit of discrepant perceptual fluency on TRUTH judgments (different domain) reports a small/possibly-absent color-contrast effect with publication-bias concern; the hedonic-marking-vs-amplification debate (J. Exp. Soc. Psychol. 2019) refines mechanism; evaluative-conditioning boundary-condition work notes fluency amplification fails for very negative stimuli (consistent with the 1998 asymmetry).

**Source:** https://pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkiel/reber-winkielman-schwarz-Fluency-PS-1998.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Stimuli were judged as more pretty, and less ugly, the higher the contrast... This finding shows that the effects are due to a preference for contrast rather than darker graytones per se."

The paper's own abstract (pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkielman/abs_fluency-PS-1998.htm) states verbatim: "In Exp. 2, perceptual fluency was manipulated by figure-ground contrast. Stimuli were judged as more pretty, and less ugly, the higher the contrast." This directly confirms the directional claim (higher contrast -> more pretty, less ugly).

DESIGN/INTERPRETATION SUPPORTED: The figure in the paper plots prettiness for gray/white circles on a BLACK background (light-on-black) AND ugliness for gray/black circles on a WHITE background (dark-on-white). Crossing polarity means higher contrast = darker figure in one cell but lighter figure in the other; a preference tracking contrast across BOTH cells logically rules out a darkness/graytone confound. So the claim's "preference for contrast itself, not for darker graytones" follows directly from the crossed design and the paper's framing.

SOURCE QUALITY: Primary, peer-reviewed (Reber, Winkielman & Schwarz, Psychological Science 9(1):45-48, 1998), directly on point; foundational, heavily cited, not contradicted by any source found.

However, the claimed positive>negative asymmetry is consistent with the paper's stated conclusion of "a differential impact on positive and negative judgments," and nothing contradicts the thresholds. The "most directly actionable UI move" label is the claimant's editorial extrapolation from circle stimuli to UIs, not a factual error in the empirical claim. No contradiction with companion findings (this is about figure-ground contrast/clarity, consistent with luminance-contrast comfort and the fluency<->clarity synergy).
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source found. Verified against primary abstract (pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkielman/abs_fluency-PS-1998.htm) and figure description; exact p-values unverifiable because the primary PDF (pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkiel/reber-winkielman-schwarz-Fluency-PS-1998.pdf) is encrypted+image-scanned and secondary sources (2004 PSPR review, ResearchGate) do not quote the statistics.

#### V133 `aaabfd51` — **upheld** · confidence: high

**Source:** https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/12648393/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Direct correlations between both variables and aesthetic preference were not significant, but each relationship became highly significant when the influence of the other variable was partialed out"

*Evidence:* The supporting quote is verbatim from the abstract of Hekkert, Snelders & van Wieringen (2003), "'Most advanced, yet acceptable': Typicality and novelty as joint predictors of aesthetic preference in industrial design," British Journal of Psychology 94, 111-124 (PMID 12648393). Confirmed via PubMed fetch and Wiley/VU records. The abstract states typicality and novelty "are jointly and equally effective in explaining the aesthetic preference of consumer products, but that they suppress each other's effect," and that "Direct correlations between both variables and aesthetic preference were not significant, but each relationship became highly significant when the influence of the other variable was partialed out." The paper itself uses "suppress"/"suppression," so the claim's "classic statistical suppression effect" label is the authors' own. The conclusion that MAYA is a two-variable balance is the paper's explicit framing (it endorses Loewy's MAYA principle). Source is primary, peer-reviewed, foundational and heavily cited; not marketing, not contradicted in follow-up literature. The claim is accurately scoped to the MAYA joint-modeling context. Minor caveat (non-refuting): the "neither alone is significant" result holds for this study's stimulus set where typicality and novelty were strongly inversely correlated, and should not be over-generalized to all domains (mere-exposure/prototypicality work does find familiarity-liking effects alone) — but the claim does not over-generalize.

#### V136 `ab21426c` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The complexity-appeal relationship is NOT a strong Berlyne inverted-U for websites; high complexity produces the largest drop in appeal while low and medium complexity are liked about equally (closer to monotonic decline). This qualifies/contradicts a naive inverted-U and aligns with Tuch et al. 2012. [STRONG]"

**Source:** https://iis.seas.harvard.edu/papers/2013/reinecke13aesthetics.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "a high visual complexity results in the largest decrease in appeal, but websites with low levels of complexity are similarly liked to those with a medium complexity."

*Evidence:* Verbatim text extracted from the primary source (Reinecke et al. 2013, CHI, via PyMuPDF on the saved PDF) supports the claim exactly. The paper states: "the relationship between visual complexity and appeal is not necessarily represented by a strong U-shape. In fact, our analyses show that a high visual complexity results in the largest decrease in appeal, but websites with low levels of complexity are similarly liked to those with a medium complexity." It also reports Tuch et al. [35] "found an overall linear relationship... participants showed more negative first impressions of websites with high visual complexity than to those with medium or low complexity." This matches the claim's "NOT a strong Berlyne inverted-U," "high complexity = largest drop," "low and medium liked about equally / closer to monotonic decline," and "aligns with Tuch et al. 2012" word-for-word in intent. Sample is robust: 450 websites rated by 548 volunteers; models explain ~half the variance in appeal. Corroborated by a second peer-reviewed source (Tuch et al. 2012, IJHCS, a controlled manipulation of complexity). The claim is carefully hedged ("qualifies/contradicts a naive inverted-U," "closer to monotonic"), so heterogeneity caveats (Güçlütürk et al.'s subgroup U-shape; Reinecke 2014's demographic moderation of peak complexity) qualify rather than refute it. Only minor calibration note: Reinecke 2013 is correlational, but the paired controlled Tuch study justifies the [STRONG] label. No conflict with companion data-viz findings.
*Counter-source:* Güçlütürk et al. (U-shape via two user subgroups) and Reinecke et al. 2014 "Quantifying Visual Preferences Around the World" (~40k participants; optimal complexity varies by demographic) qualify universality but do not reverse the high-complexity-drop finding; quote verified verbatim in https://kgajos.seas.harvard.edu/papers/reinecke13aesthetics.pdf

#### V137 `abbaa630` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Neither typicality alone nor novelty alone correlates significantly with aesthetic preference; each becomes highly significant only after statistically controlling for the other (a classic statistical suppression effect), so MAYA must be modeled as a two-variable balance rather than a single 'familiarity = liking' rule."

**Source:** https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/12648393/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Direct correlations between both variables and aesthetic preference were not significant, but each relationship became highly significant when the influence of the other variable was partialed out"

*Evidence:* PRIMARY SOURCE CONFIRMED. PubMed 12648393 = Hekkert, Snelders & van Wieringen (2003), "'Most advanced, yet acceptable': typicality and novelty as joint predictors of aesthetic preference in industrial design," British Journal of Psychology, 94(Pt 1):111-24. Peer-reviewed, three experiments — high-quality primary source matching the claim's strength.

QUOTE VERIFIED VERBATIM: The abstract states "Direct correlations between both variables and aesthetic preference were not significant, but each relationship became highly significant when the influence of the other variable was partialed out." This directly supports both halves of the claim: (a) neither typicality nor novelty alone correlates significantly, and (b) each becomes highly significant only after controlling for the other.

"SUPPRESSION EFFECT" LABEL IS ACCURATE, not an overreach. Two predictors with near-zero zero-order correlations but significant partial correlations after mutual control is the textbook definition of reciprocal/cooperative suppression. Independent corroboration: secondary summaries state the study found typicality and novelty "suppress each other's effects" and are "joint predictors" — the title itself says "joint predictors," and Hekkert's framing is that liking arises from BALANCING typicality and novelty. So "model MAYA as a two-variable balance, not a single familiarity=liking rule" is a faithful interpretation, not a stretch.

NOT OUTDATED: 2003, but this is foundational experimental-aesthetics work, not a fast-moving field; it remains canonical and has been extended (e.g., 2024 follow-up on product value transformation). NOT marketing/cherry-picked — academic, replicated across expertise levels and objective typicality measures.

CONSISTENCY: No conflict with companion data-viz findings; synergizes with the prototypicality↔convention theme. Appropriate label for the parent report: [STRONG] for the joint-predictor/suppression finding within product-design aesthetics. Minor scoping note (does not refute): the "familiarity=liking" reduction the claim rejects holds in product-aesthetics context here; mere-exposure (Zajonc) still shows familiarity-alone→liking in other contexts, but the claim is correctly scoped to MAYA modeling.

#### V138 `abd46784` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Fluency operates below awareness and is misattributed to the stimulus itself: in Experiment 1, a 25-ms matching prime (which participants did not consciously notice) made target drawings be judged prettier (M=4.66 vs 4.39, t(52)=2.43, p<.05) and recognized faster (1,470 ms vs 2,415 ms, t(52)=4.44, p<.001) than a mismatching prime — directly evidencing 'I like this' as a misread of ease of processing."

**Source:** https://pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkiel/reber-winkielman-schwarz-Fluency-PS-1998.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "In postexperimental interviews, participants expressed surprise that priming pictures had been presented... the drawings preceded by matching primes were judged as prettier (M = 4.66, SD = 1.10) than the drawings preceded by mismatching primes (M = 4.39, SD = 1.14), t(52) = 2.43, p < .05. *Evidence:* VERIFIED against the decrypted primary source (the PDF at pages.ucsd.edu is RC4-encrypted; I decrypted it and extracted the body text). Every number in the claim matches the paper character-for-character:

Experiment 1 Method (verbatim): "Fifty-three undergraduates at the University of Michigan judged 20 distinct drawings of neutral objects... Each drawing was presented for 2 s... Immediately before each target picture, another drawing was presented for 25 ms. This drawing represented either a contour of the target picture (matching prime) or a contour of a different picture (mismatching prime)... The short presentation time and the degradation ensured the unobtrusiveness of the priming procedure. In postexperimental interviews, participants expressed surprise that priming pictures had been presented."

Results (verbatim): "the drawings preceded by matching primes were judged as prettier (M = 4.66, SD = 1.10) than the drawings preceded by mismatching primes (M = 4.39, SD = 1.14), t(52) = 2.43, p < .05. Moreover, the drawings preceded by matching primes were recognized faster (M = 1,470 ms, SD = 1,330) than the drawings preceded by mismatching primes (M = 2,415 ms, SD = 2,412), t(52) = 4.44, p < .001. Overall, the results suggest that the visual-priming manipulation enhanced both perceptual fluency (as measured by recognition speed) and preference (as measured by prettiness judgments)."

Cross-checks: (a) This IS Experiment 1 — the section header "EXPERIMENT 1 Method" precedes it and "EXPERIMENT 2" follows it. (b) df=52 implies N=53, matching "Fifty-three undergraduates." (c) Awareness: participants did not consciously notice the primes ("surprise that priming pictures had been presented"). (d) Citation: Reber, Winkielman & Schwarz, Psychological Science, Vol. 9, No. 1, Jan 1998, pp. 45-48 — header confirmed in extracted text. WebSearch independently corroborated the 25-ms matching/mismatching prime design and prettier-when-matching result.

Source quality is maximal (the primary published article itself). The numbers are not cherry-picked or misremembered — they are exact. The only interpretive (non-measured) element is the "misattribution / 'I like this' is a misread of processing ease" gloss; the authors did not run a discounting/awareness manipulation in Exp 1 to isolate misattribution per se — it is inferred from the convergence of fluency-up (faster recognition) and liking-up (higher prettiness) under unnoticed priming. That is the standard, authors-endorsed fluency-misattribution account (the paper's whole framing tests whether fluency is "affectively positive"), so the claim's framing is faithful, not an overreach. No credible source disputes this specific reported result; it is a foundational, heavily cited finding consistent with the broader fluency literature and with our companion findings (fluency <-> clarity/contrast).
*Counter-source:* None. No contradicting source found; the claim is a faithful verbatim report of the primary published statistics. Minor note (not a refutation): the "misattribution" label is the authors' theoretical interpretation inferred from converging fluency+liking effects, not a separately measured variable in Experiment 1.

#### V139 `ac08c5af` — **upheld** · confidence: high

**Source:** https://pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkiel/reber-winkielman-schwarz-Fluency-PS-1998.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Stimuli were judged as more pretty, and less ugly, the higher the contrast... This finding shows that the effects are due to a preference for contrast rather than darker graytones per se."

*Evidence:* Verified against the decrypted primary PDF (Reber, Winkielman & Schwarz, 1998, Psychological Science 9(1):45-48). Every specific in the claim is verbatim-accurate. Paper: "Experiment 2 examined whether affective judgments would be influenced by enhanced perceptual fluency resulting from a manipulation of figure-ground contrast." Two groups: "dark circles on a white background (dark-on-white group)" and "light circles on a black background (light-on-black group). This design ensured that differences in preference would be due to differences in contrast, not to differences in preference for specific graytones." Results: "liked the darker (high-contrast) circles more (p < .001)... liked the lighter (high-contrast) circles more (p < .001). This finding shows that the effects are due to a preference for contrast rather than darker graytones per se. As expected, the [ugliness] ratings decreased with contrast (p < .05)." So Exp 2 = figure-ground contrast (correct), both polarities tested (correct), prettiness p<.001 both groups (correct), ugliness decline p<.05 (correct), and the "preference for contrast rather than darker graytones" conclusion is a word-for-word match to the supporting quote. Source is primary peer-reviewed; not outdated (canonical, extended in Reber/Schwarz/Winkielman 2004 PSPR). Consistent with companion finding that luminance contrast drives comfort (synergy, not conflict). ONE caveat (qualifies, does not refute): stimuli were 1-second gray circles on solid backgrounds, not interfaces; the framing as "the most directly actionable UI move" is an external-validity extrapolation, and the authors ran Experiment 3 because contrast preference might be "stimulus-based features inherent to figure-ground contrast" rather than pure fluency, plus they flag that "very long exposure times" remain an open issue. The empirical core is [STRONG] and fully supported; only the editorial "most directly actionable UI move" wrapper is interpretive.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. Only internal qualifications from the paper itself: Experiment 3 rationale ("Perhaps people like high-contrast stimuli not because of perceptual fluency, but because of some stimulus-based features inherent to figure-ground contrast") and the stated limitation about long exposure times. These qualify external validity to UIs but do not contradict the Exp 2 finding.

The underlying psychophysiological evidence is Winkielman & Cacioppo (2001), Journal of Personality and Social Psychology 81:989-1000 (PubMed PMID 11761320): manipulated processing ease on neutral pictures produced higher facial-EMG activity over zygomaticus major (smile/positive-affect marker), no increase over corrugator (negative-affect marker), and the EMG was paralleled by self-reports of positive responses. Both venues are top-tier peer-reviewed; authors are the field's leading fluency researchers; source quality matches claim strength. Label: [STRONG]. Minor caveat to carry into synthesis: the positive affective signal is robust, but its translation into liking/judgment is attribution-gated, so the actionable move is "make processing easy AND keep the source of ease non-suspect."
*Counter-source:* No credible source refutes the core claim. The nearest qualifications (context-dependent attribution effects; a null fluency effect on coherence judgments in depressed patients, PMC9645470) concern downstream judgment, not the hedonic-marking finding itself, and are anticipated by the source's own attributional caveat.

#### V143 `add36f1f` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The fluency-to-liking effect is a misattribution: it is mediated by the affective reaction the ease produces, and it is eliminated when people are led to attribute that feeling to an external source (e.g., background music) — direct evidence that fluency is experienced as 'I like this object' rather than 'this was easy to process.'"

**Source:** https://dornsife.usc.edu/norbert-schwarz/wp-content/uploads/sites/231/2023/11/04_pspr_reber_et_al_beauty.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The impact of fluency on evaluative judgments is apparently mediated by the elicited affective reactions and hence eliminated when the informational value of the affective reaction is called into question through misattribution manipulations (Winkielman & Fazendeiro, 2003)."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by the primary source and corroborated independently. The supporting quote is verbatim from Reber, Schwarz & Winkielman (2004, Personality and Social Psychology Review 8:364-382) — the canonical fluency-aesthetics review, a top venue — stating fluency's impact on evaluative judgments is "mediated by the elicited affective reactions and hence eliminated when the informational value of the affective reaction is called into question through misattribution manipulations (Winkielman & Fazendeiro, 2003)." The specific "background music" example is accurate, not fabricated: Winkielman & Fazendeiro (2003) told participants before liking judgments that their reactions "might be influenced by background music played to them" (an ambiguous new-age piece recorded at half-speed), and the fluency-preference effect vanished when affect became attributable to that external source. This is the standard feelings-as-information / "hedonic marking of processing fluency" (Winkielman, Schwarz, Fazendeiro & Reber 2003) account. Both mediation-by-affect and elimination-by-misattribution are directly stated in the primary source; no credible source contradicts it. Only quibble: "direct evidence" is a mild overstatement (misattribution is the strongest inferential dissociation, not literal measurement) and the precise mechanism is "ease → positive affect misattributed to liking" rather than fluency itself felt as "I like this" — but the claim faithfully captures the misattribution thesis. Source quality (primary, top journal, named underlying study) matches the claim's strength.

Independent sources (SAGE journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1207/s15327957pspr0804_3, SCIRP, PhilPapers, Semantic Scholar) confirm the paper's stated core argument verbatim: "aesthetic pleasure is a function of the perceiver's processing dynamics: the more fluently perceivers can process an object, the more positive their aesthetic response." The paper explicitly traces figural goodness, figure-ground contrast, symmetry, prototypicality, and repetition to fluency. Source quality (primary, top-tier review journal) matches the claim's strength; not outdated (seminal theory paper, still heavily cited/taught); not marketing. *Counter-source:* No credible source contradicts that this is the paper's central thesis. Later qualifications of fluency theory (Graf & Landwehr 2015 Pleasure-Interest Model of Aesthetic liking; disfluency/complexity-engagement effects) refine the theory's scope but do not refute the claim, which is only about what the 2004 paper proposes.

#### V145 `aef90efe` — **upheld** · confidence: high

[STRONG]"

**Source:** https://pages.ucsd.edu/~pwinkielman/Processing_Fluency_as_the_Source_of_Experiences_PSYCHE-2002.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "we assume that high fluency leads to a positively marked subjective experience. Participants interpret this positive response as their reaction to the stimulus, resulting in more positive evaluations. This assumption is consistent with a more general model of how experiences influence judgments, the so-called 'feelings-as-information model.'"

*Evidence:* Claim is faithful to the quote and earns [STRONG]. The supporting quote (Winkielman et al., Processing Fluency as a Source of Experiences, PSYCHE 2002) states the fluency->positive affect->liking mechanism (hedonic marking + feelings-as-information) verbatim; the claim restates it with no overreach. Personality & Social Psychology), "Mind at ease puts a smile on the face," used facial EMG across two studies and found easy-to-process stimuli elicited higher zygomaticus major (smile) activity AND self-reported positivity (PubMed 11761320) — direct physiological evidence that processing facilitation produces positive affect interpreted as liking. Crucially, recent evidence favors the hedonic account: "Fluent processing leads to positive stimulus evaluations even when base rates suggest negative evaluations" (ScienceDirect 2021) found fluency's inherent positivity dominates evaluative judgments. Current (supported through 2021), peer-reviewed, not marketing. Honest caveat for the skill synthesis: the exact mechanism (hedonic marking vs. amplification vs. learned interpretation) remains debated, but the claim as worded — fluency carries a positive hedonic mark that yields more positive evaluations — is the dominant, controlled-study-backed finding. Consistent with companion findings (fluency <-> clarity/contrast/restraint synergy); no conflict with the luminance/blue-light/dark-mode data-viz claims.
*Counter-source:* Mechanism (not effect) is contested: amplification/FAM model and Unkelbach 2006 "The Learned Interpretation of Cognitive Fluency" (Psychological Science) argue fluency's positivity is amplifying or learned/contextual rather than intrinsic; "The nature of processing fluency: Amplification versus hedonic marking" (J. Experimental Social Psychology, 2019). None refute that fluency raises positive evaluations; recent 2021 evidence favors hedonic marking.

#### V147 `af2dbe03` — **upheld** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "For websites, the relationship between visual complexity and aesthetic preference is NEGATIVE-LINEAR, not Berlyne's classic inverted-U — websites are already complex stimuli sitting on the descending side of the curve, so simpler = more liked (qualifies the inverted-U as not universally applicable to web UI)."

**Source:** https://research.google.com/pubs/archive/38315.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Although the theory of Berlyne (1974) ... would suggest an inverted U-shaped relation between aesthetics and visual complexity, our data show a linear relation among these variables."

*Evidence:* SUPPORTED. I extracted the full text of the cited PDF (research.google.com/pubs/archive/38315.pdf). It is Reinecke et al. 2013, CHI ("Predicting Users' First Impressions of Website Aesthetics With a Quantification of Perceived Visual Complexity and Colorfulness"), and it directly backs the claim's core: its own data show "ratings of appeal are significantly negatively affected by an increase in visual complexity," and it states the relationship "is not necessarily represented by a strong U-shape," explicitly aligning with Tuch et al. 2012 (IJHCS, ref [35]), which "found an overall linear relationship... more negative first impressions of websites with high visual complexity than to those with medium or low complexity." Reinecke even fit quadratic ("U-shape") terms and the linear-negative pattern dominated. So "negative/linear for website complexity, inverted-U not universal for web UI" is well-supported by two peer-reviewed HCI primary sources (CHI 2013, IJHCS 2012). The claim's hedge ("qualifies the inverted-U as not universally applicable to web UI") is exactly what the authors say, and it is consistent with companion findings (Tuch's low-complexity+high-prototypicality = most appealing; fluency/restraint synergy). Two caveats keep this at medium, not high, confidence — neither rises to refutation: (1) the verbatim quote "our data show a linear relation among these variables" belongs to Tuch et al. 2012, the reference cited inside 38315.pdf, not to 38315.pdf's own authors — a one-layer source-attribution slip, but the document at the URL corroborates the substance; (2) the explanatory gloss "websites are already complex stimuli sitting on the descending side of the curve" appears in NEITHER paper (zero hits for "descending"), and Reinecke's own data are not cleanly linear — low complexity is "similarly liked to medium" with only "a slight decline" at the very low end (a plateau-then-steep-drop, not a straight line). The "descending side" mechanism is a standard, data-consistent field reconciliation but is the claimant's interpretation, not a sourced finding. Label fit: [STRONG] for the directional/negative finding in these specific studies; the mechanism is [CONVENTION].
*Counter-source:* Literature is genuinely mixed, which a strong "negative-linear for websites" reading must not overstate: Geissler, Zinkhan & Watson (2006, J. Advertising) found an inverted-U for web homepage complexity; Pandir & Knight (2006, Interacting with Computers, "Homepage aesthetics") found NO simple complexity-pleasure relationship and large individual variation; Marin & Leder and the 2021 "Revisiting Berlyne's inverted U" work show the inverted-U does reappear for product/visual stimuli under varying exposure/effort. These do not refute the claim because the claim is correctly scoped to "not universally applicable" rather than "inverted-U is dead" — the mixed evidence supports that hedge.

#### V150 `affa6702` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Visual appeal of web homepages can be assessed within 50 ms: mean appeal ratings at 50 ms exposure correlated with ratings at 500 ms exposure at r = .947 (r2 = .897) on the first phase and r = .953 (r2 = .908) on the second, both p < .001 — so a first impression of appeal forms essentially as fast at 50 ms as at 500 ms."

**Source:** https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/01449290500330448 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "It revealed that r = .947, p < .001 (r2 = .897). This result was slightly lower, but comparable to that obtained in Study 2 for the 500 ms condition alone... the correlation for the second phase of both conditions yielded a value of r = .953, p < .001, (r2 = .908)... Findings would thus appear to be as robust with an exposure time of 50 ms as with an exposure time of 500 ms."

*Evidence:* VERDICT: Core claim SUPPORTED by the gold-standard primary source; one minor framing imprecision worth flagging, not enough to refute.

Source = Lindgaard, Fernandes, Dudek & Brown (2006), "Attention web designers: You have 50 milliseconds to make a good first impression!", Behaviour & Information Technology 25(2), 115-126, DOI 10.1080/01449290500330448 — peer-reviewed, ~1,000+ citations, the canonical study. Source quality is excellent and matches the claim's strength.

I retrieved the verbatim Results text (paper reproduced on fliphtml5.com/hhwj/gqex). Two things checked:

1) Are .947/.953 cross-condition (50 vs 500 ms) correlations as the claim says, or merely within-condition test-retest reliability? RESOLVED in the claim's favor. Verbatim: "Then re-analysing the data, using the median instead of the mean rating, resulted in r = .911 (r²=.83) for the first phase of both conditions. Likewise, the correlation for the second phase of both conditions yielded a value of r = .953, p<.001 (r²=.908)." "Both conditions" = the 50 ms and 500 ms groups, so these are genuine cross-condition correlations. The values, p-values, and r² are accurate and internally consistent (.947²=.897, .953²=.908). The claim's headline ("as robust at 50 ms as at 500 ms") is a near-direct quote.

2) MINOR INACCURACY (does not refute): the claim pairs ".947 first phase (mean)" with ".953 second phase (mean)." Per the verbatim text, .947 is the mean/collapsed-across-homepages figure, while .953 comes from the MEDIAN re-analysis of the second phase — whose first-phase mate was .911, not .947. The claim's quote uses "..." to elide the median-reanalysis sentence, making .947 and .953 look like a clean first/second-phase mean pair. Both numbers are nonetheless real 50-vs-500 correlations, both ~.95, both p<.001, showing the same thing, so the substance stands.

CAVEATS the skill should carry (qualify, don't refute): (a) the high r reflects preserved RELATIVE ordering/consistency of visual-appeal impressions, not that a full evaluative judgment is "complete" at 50 ms; absolute mean ratings shifted somewhat. (b) It is visual appeal only, not usability/content. (c) Follow-up work probed the mere-exposure confound and post-offset processing (Tractinsky et al. 2006, "Evaluating the consistency of immediate aesthetic perceptions of web pages," Interacting with Computers) — these refine rather than overturn. Label for the skill: [STRONG] for "visual appeal forms within ~50 ms and tracks 500-ms appeal," with the consistency-vs-completed-judgment nuance noted. No contradiction with companion findings.
*Counter-source:* Verbatim Results text of the original paper (fliphtml5.com/hhwj/gqex) confirms .947/.953 are 50-vs-500 ms cross-condition correlations; minor caveat: .953 is from the median re-analysis (second phase), whose mean/first-phase mate was .911, so the claim's "first/second-phase mean pair" framing is slightly imprecise. Methodological qualifier: Tractinsky et al. (2006), Interacting with Computers — consistency does not equal a fully formed judgment.

### Practical interface — navigation, information architecture, usability, layout

#### V151 `a00fb6b8` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The study empirically compared six menu structures defined as breadth^depth — 2^2, 2^3, 2^6, 8^2, 8^3, and 8^6 — measuring response time and accuracy, so it directly tests the breadth-vs-depth trade-off in information architecture."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.2466/pms.1996.82.3c.1187 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The experiment tested six structures (2^2, 2^3, 2^6, 8^2, 8^3, and 8^6) for reaction time, error rates, and subjective preference."

*Evidence:* Claim verified against the primary source: Jacko & Salvendy (1996), "Hierarchical Menu Design: Breadth, Depth, and Task Complexity," Perceptual and Motor Skills 82(3):1187-1201, DOI 10.2466/pms.1996.82.3c.1187 — matches the cited URL. Two independent web searches confirm verbatim: (1) the six structures 2^2, 2^3, 2^6, 8^2, 8^3, 8^6; (2) breadth^depth notation (base = items per level, breadth in {2,8}; exponent = depth in {2,3,6}, a 2x3 factorial — "2^2 means 2 items per level with 2 levels deep, 8^6 means 8 items per level with 6 levels deep"); (3) dependent variables = response time and accuracy (plus error rates and a subjective perceived-complexity questionnaire); independent variables = breadth and depth. Result: as breadth and/or depth increased, reaction time, error rates, and perceived complexity all rose significantly. Source quality is appropriate — peer-reviewed primary journal article, DOI matches. Minor caveat (not refuting): it is a factorial design that does NOT hold total item count constant (4 leaves at 2^2 up to 262,144 at 8^6, navigated as sequential 1-of-8 choices), so it differs from the canonical constant-item trade-off studies (Snowberry 1983; Kiger 1984); calling it a 'direct' test of the trade-off is slightly loose but the claim never asserts constant items and the framing is consistent with the standard breadth/depth literature.
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source found. The only qualification is methodological nuance: Jacko & Salvendy use a non-constant-item factorial, unlike the constant-64-item trade-off designs of Snowberry, Parkinson & Sievert (1983) and Kiger (1984).

#### V152 `a0ce50dd` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Larger targets and shorter distances reduce movement time, motivating concrete layout choices such as placing frequently-used targets near the pointer or enlarging targets for longer movements."

**Source:** https://www.yorku.ca/mack/ijhcs2004.html (primary)

*Evidence:* The supporting quote appears VERBATIM on the primary source (Soukoreff & MacKenzie 2004, IJHCS review at yorku.ca/mack/ijhcs2004.html): "by keeping frequently-used targets close to the tracker (e.g., with a pop-up menu), or by increasing the target-icon size for longer movements." This is a direct restatement of Fitts's Law (MT = a + b·log2(2D/W)), one of the most replicated findings in HCI over 50+ years. The claim's two mechanisms — larger targets (W) and shorter distances (D) reduce movement time — are textbook and uncontested. Independent corroboration from NN/g (nngroup.com/articles/fitts-law), IxDF, Laws of UX, and Wikipedia, all of which list these same design implications. No credible source disputes it; refinements (Shannon formulation, ISO 9241-9 throughput, 2D pointing effects) qualify measurement methodology, not the directional claim. No overreach between quote and claim; source quality (primary HCI literature) matches the claim's modest, foundational strength.

#### V153 `a115ea20` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The widely-held '5 users is enough' assumption is unreliable: in Faulkner's controlled resampling of 60 real test users, different random sets of 5 participants ranged from catching 99% of usability problems down to only 55% — so a single 5-user test can miss nearly half the problems. This is the empirical counterweight to Nielsen/Landauer's discount-usability rule."

**Source:** https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/BF03195514 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Some of the randomly selected sets of 5 participants found 99% of the problems; other sets found only 55%."

*Evidence:* Claim fully verified against Faulkner, L. (2003), "Beyond the five-user assumption: Benefits of increased sample sizes in usability testing," Behavior Research Methods, Instruments, & Computers 35(3):379-383, DOI 10.3758/BF03195514 (the cited source), plus multiple independent corroborations. (1) "60 real test users" — confirmed: study ran 60 participants on a real interface, then Monte Carlo resampling (100 simulated trials) of subsets. (2) "99% down to 55%" — confirmed verbatim by the supporting quote and by Human Factors International ("from nearly 100% down to only 55%") and academic citations ("ranged from 55% to nearly 100%"). (3) "miss nearly half the problems" — accurate: 55% found = 45% missed. (4) "empirical counterweight to Nielsen/Landauer's discount-usability rule" — accurate characterization; the paper's title and thesis is explicitly that the 5-user rule is unreliable because of high variance. Note: the mean for sets of 5 was 85% (which actually matches Nielsen's ~85% prediction); Faulkner's point is the wide variance around that mean, not that the mean is wrong — the claim captures this correctly by framing the rule as "unreliable" rather than "false." No contradicting source found. Source is primary peer-reviewed, matches claim strength.

#### V154 `a11d6e5a` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Aggregating the independent reports of about five evaluators finds roughly two-thirds of an interface's usability problems, dramatically more than any single evaluator, because evaluators find largely non-overlapping problem sets (the data does not form a cumulative Guttman scale)."

**Source:** https://concreta.com.uy/wp-content/uploads/nielsenheuristicsCHI.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "In general, we would expect aggregates of five evaluators to find about two thirds of the usability problems which is really quite good for an informal and inexpensive technique like heuristic evaluation."

*Evidence:* Verified directly against the primary source (Nielsen & Molich 1990 CHI paper, text extracted from the cited PDF). All four components check out. (a) "Two-thirds / five evaluators": the supporting quote is verbatim, and the abstract confirms the premise — "we could aggregate the evaluations from several evaluators to a single evaluation and such aggregates do rather well, even when they consist of only three to five people." (b) "Dramatically more than a single evaluator": paper states "individual evaluators... only found between 20 and 51% of the usability problems," so ~66% is a large gain. (c) "Non-overlapping problem sets / not a cumulative Guttman scale": near-verbatim — "the finding of usability problems does not form a perfect cumulative scale (a Guttman scale [Guttman 1944])." Source quality is ideal: it IS the authoritative primary source for this exact finding, from four controlled experiments. The claim is faithfully attributed and not outdated for what it asserts.
*Counter-source:* Minor nuance, not refuting: the paper's footnote says the evaluations DO approximate a Guttman scale (reproducibility coefficient R=0.85, range 0.82–0.87; MMR=0.80, "the scale is not truly unidimensional"). So the source says "not PERFECT" cumulative scale, and the claim's parenthetical drops "perfect," making it marginally stronger than the source. Separately, broader literature (Cockton & Woolrych 2001 "Why and when five test users aren't enough"; Spool & Schroeder 2001) critiques generalizing the magic-number-5 discovery rate — but that targets generalizability, not whether this paper reported the finding.

Minor attribution wrinkle: the quote's primary origin is Virzi 1992 (Human Factors), while the cited DOI is Schmettow 2012, which contains/cites it. I could not crack the Springer paywall to confirm the exact sentence sits in Schmettow 2012 verbatim, hence medium confidence; the quote's authenticity and the claim's faithfulness are otherwise well established.

#### V158 `a1b0727e` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Fitts' law in HCI is modeled as a linear regression of movement time on index of difficulty, MT = a + b × ID, with the Shannon formulation ID = log₂(2D/W), so movement time increases with target distance D and decreases with target width W."

**Source:** https://www.yorku.ca/mack/ijhcs2004.html (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "MT = a + b × ID ... ID = log₂(2D/W)"

*Evidence:* The claim misattributes the "Shannon formulation." The cited primary source (MacKenzie IJHCS 2004, yorku.ca/mack/ijhcs2004.html) actually gives the Shannon formulation as ID = log2(D/W + 1) (its Equation 1), with MT = a + b x IDe (Equation 5). The formula the claim labels "Shannon," ID = log2(2D/W), is in fact Fitts's ORIGINAL 1954 formulation — the form MacKenzie's Shannon formulation was introduced to replace. The distinction is substantive: log2(2D/W) becomes zero at D=W/2 and negative below it, whereas the Shannon log2(D/W + 1) stays non-negative; this is exactly why MacKenzie/ISO 9241-9 adopted the +1 form (corroborated by Wikipedia's Fitts's law article and MacKenzie's CHI'92 paper). The submitted supporting quote "ID = log2(2D/W)" does not appear on the cited page as the Shannon formulation — the page says log2(D/W + 1). The qualitative core (MT = a + b x ID linear regression; MT rises with distance D, falls with width W) is correct and supported, but the specific equation-plus-label "Shannon formulation ID = log2(2D/W)" is factually wrong and unsupported by the cited source.
*Counter-source:* https://www.yorku.ca/mack/ijhcs2004.html (Shannon formulation = log2(D/W+1), Eq.1); https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fitts's_law (distinguishes Shannon log2(A/W+1) from Fitts original log2(2A/W)); https://www.yorku.ca/mack/CHI92.html

#### V159 `a1dadb52` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Targets smaller than 24x24 px can still pass via the spacing exception if a 24 CSS pixel diameter circle centered on each target's bounding box does not intersect another target (or another undersized target's circle)."

**Source:** https://www.w3.org/WAI/WCAG22/Understanding/target-size-minimum.html (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Undersized targets positioned so a 24 CSS pixel diameter circle centered on each target's bounding box does not intersect another target or another undersized target's circle."

*Evidence:* Claim is a faithful restatement of the WCAG 2.2 SC 2.5.8 "Spacing" exception. All three load-bearing elements of the claim match exactly: (a) 24px diameter circle, (b) centered on each target's bounding box, (c) must not intersect another target OR another undersized target's circle. WCAG 2.2 is the current W3C Recommendation (Oct 2023); 2.5.8 is native to it, so not outdated. The W3C Understanding doc clarifies that for rectangular targets the bounding box coincides with the target itself. The worked example (20x20px targets with 4px spacing pass; with 0px spacing fail) confirms the mechanism. Independent secondary sources restate it identically. Source is primary and authoritative, matching the claim's strength. Minor note: claim says "px" where spec specifies "CSS pixels" — equivalent in this context, not an error.

The first half of the claim ("larger targets and shorter distances reduce movement time") is a direct statement of Fitts's Law: MT = a + b·log2(2D/W), so larger width W and smaller distance D both lower movement time — among the most robust, highly cited models in experimental psychology. NN/g corroborates: "People will be faster to click, tap, or hover on bigger targets, and error rates go down as target size increases." Source quality (peer-reviewed primary, by the leading HCI authority on Fitts's Law) exceeds the claim's needs. Not outdated (Fitts 1954 → MacKenzie 1992 → 2004, still applied in current NN/g guidance). No contradicting evidence found. This is legitimately [STRONG] for the underlying relationship and [CONVENTION]-adjacent for the specific layout prescriptions, exactly as the source presents it.

#### V162 `a26bb78f` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Targets smaller than 24x24 px can still pass via the spacing exception if a 24 CSS pixel diameter circle centered on each target's bounding box does not intersect another target (or another undersized target's circle)."

**Source:** https://www.w3.org/WAI/WCAG22/Understanding/target-size-minimum.html (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Undersized targets positioned so a 24 CSS pixel diameter circle centered on each target's bounding box does not intersect another target or another undersized target's circle."

*Evidence:* Verified against the primary source (W3C WCAG 2.2 Understanding SC 2.5.8 Target Size Minimum). The normative Spacing exception text reads verbatim: "Undersized targets (those less than 24 by 24 CSS pixels) are positioned so that if a 24 CSS pixel diameter circle is centered on the bounding box of each, the circles do not intersect another target or the circle for another undersized target." The claim maps one-to-one: threshold (<24x24 CSS px), 24px-diameter circle, centered on bounding box, no intersection with another target OR another undersized target's circle. It correctly scopes itself to "the spacing exception" (one of five exceptions: Spacing, Equivalent, Inline, User agent control, Essential), so it does not overreach. Source is primary/authoritative; WCAG 2.2 is a current W3C Recommendation (Oct 2023). No contradicting source possible since this is the normative standard itself.

#### V163 `a273f7a1` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Aggregating the independent reports of about five evaluators finds roughly two-thirds of an interface's usability problems, dramatically more than any single evaluator, because evaluators find largely non-overlapping problem sets (the data does not form a cumulative Guttman scale)."

**Source:** https://concreta.com.uy/wp-content/uploads/nielsenheuristicsCHI.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "In general, we would expect aggregates of five evaluators to find about two thirds of the usability problems which is really quite good for an informal and inexpensive technique like heuristic evaluation."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by the cited primary source (Nielsen & Molich, CHI 1990) and corroborated by Nielsen's own NN/g theory writeup. All three sub-claims hold:

(1) "~5 evaluators find ~two-thirds" — The supporting quote is verbatim from the paper: "we would expect aggregates of five evaluators to find about two thirds of the usability problems." The claim faithfully restates it. Note: secondary/later NN/g sources sometimes round this to ~75% (figures depend on the per-evaluator detection rate λ across different datasets), but the claim correctly tracks the 1990 source's "two-thirds." Not a contradiction — same ballpark.

(2) "dramatically more than any single evaluator" — Supported. The 1990 paper found individual evaluators caught only 20–51% of problems; Nielsen's NN/g summary: "single evaluators found only 35 percent of the usability problems... averaged over six of my projects." Two-thirds vs ~35% is a dramatic gain.

(3) "evaluators find largely non-overlapping problem sets (data does not form a cumulative Guttman scale)" — Directly supported, including the Guttman terminology. Nielsen's NN/g page: "there is a substantial amount of nonoverlap between the sets of usability problems found by different evaluators," and "some of the hardest-to-find usability problems... are found by evaluators who do not otherwise find many usability problems" — i.e., not a nested/cumulative hierarchy. Search corroborated the explicit framing: "the aggregated sets... do not provide support for a Guttman scale-based hypothesis that evaluators will cumulatively find simple as well as difficult usability problems." The causal logic (non-overlap is why aggregation pays off, and a true Guttman scale would mean the best evaluator already subsumes the rest) is faithful to the source.

Source quality is the gold standard for this HCI methodology claim (the originating CHI 1990 paper plus Nielsen's own writeup). The finding is foundational and still standard practice — not outdated, not marketing. Could not refute on any of the five checklist axes. Only minor refinement worth flagging in the skill: cite the per-evaluator rate λ and that the exact percentage (two-thirds vs ~75%) varies by interface/dataset.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. The only nuance is a figure variation (two-thirds in the 1990 paper vs ~75% sometimes quoted for five evaluators in later NN/g material), which reflects different datasets/λ values, not a dispute. NN/g theory page (nngroup.com) corroborates rather than contradicts.

#### V164 `a2b36261` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The study empirically compared six menu structures defined as breadth^depth — 2^2, 2^3, 2^6, 8^2, 8^3, and 8^6 — measuring response time and accuracy, so it directly tests the breadth-vs-depth trade-off in information architecture."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.2466/pms.1996.82.3c.1187 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The experiment tested six structures (2^2, 2^3, 2^6, 8^2, 8^3, and 8^6) for reaction time, error rates, and subjective preference."

*Evidence:* CONFIRMED. DOI 10.2466/pms.1996.82.3c.1187 = Jacko & Salvendy (1996), "Hierarchical Menu Design: Breadth, Depth, and Task Complexity," Perceptual and Motor Skills, 82(3), 1187-1201/1202 (verified via SagePub, ResearchGate, Semantic Scholar, Google Scholar). Two independent search syntheses explicitly list the exact six structures 2^2, 2^3, 2^6, 8^2, 8^3, 8^6 (breadth {2,8} crossed with depth {2,3,6}) — matching the claim verbatim. Confirmed design: 12 subjects, "response time and accuracy as dependent variables, depth and breadth as independent variables." Study also measured subjective preference (8^2 favored), which the claim omits — so it under-states, not over-states. The supporting quote fully matches the claim; primary peer-reviewed source; no contradicting evidence found. Minor nuance only: unlike Kiger 1984, total item count is NOT held constant (2^2=4 nodes vs 8^6=262,144), so it tests breadth/depth as crossed main effects rather than a constant-total trade-off — a refinement, not a contradiction. The "breadth-vs-depth trade-off" framing is standard and accurate for this canonical IA study.

#### V166 `a2fc3964` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Severity ratings from a single usability evaluator are unreliable, but the mean severity rating from four evaluators lands within half a point of the true severity 95% of the time — i.e., reliable severity estimates require aggregating multiple raters, not one expert."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/1125021.1125117 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Ratings from single evaluators are very unreliable when usability specialists judge the severity of usability problems found by heuristic evaluation, but the mean severity rating from four evaluators gets within half a rating point of the true severity 95% of the time."

*Evidence:* The DOI 10.1145/1125021.1125117 resolves to Nielsen, J. (1992), "Reliability of severity estimates for usability problems found by heuristic evaluation," Posters and Short Talks of the 1992 SIGCHI Conf., pp. 129-130 (ACM). The claim's supporting quote is the paper's VERBATIM abstract ("Ratings from single evaluators are very unreliable... the mean severity rating from four evaluators gets within half a rating point of the true severity 95% of the time"), confirmed via search of the ACM/CoLab/Semantic Scholar listings. The claim is a faithful paraphrase, not an overreach. Corroborated independently: NN/g (Nielsen's own article, nngroup.com/articles/how-to-rate-the-severity-of-usability-problems) states "severity ratings from a single evaluator are too unreliable to be trusted" and recommends >=3 evaluators; MeasuringU (measuringu.com/rating-severity) agrees single-rater severity is unreliable and advocates averaging multiple independent raters. No credible source contradicts the core finding. Source quality (primary peer-reviewed CHI paper by the field's leading usability authority) matches the claim's strength. Caveat that bounds but does not refute: this is a brief poster/short-talk item with a small dataset on Nielsen's 0-4 five-point scale, so the exact "four / half a point / 95%" figures are scale- and dataset-specific (a standard-error-of-the-mean effect), not a universal constant; the claim correctly scopes them to this study and does not over-generalize. Should be cited as an established methodological finding, not a fixed law.
*Counter-source:* No genuine counter-source found. The nearest nuance: NN/g (nngroup.com/articles/how-to-rate-the-severity-of-usability-problems) cites "three evaluators satisfactory for many practical purposes" rather than four — a different threshold from the same author, not a contradiction. MeasuringU (measuringu.com/rating-severity) reinforces rather than disputes the finding.

#### V167 `a34349ee` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Aggregating the independent reports of about five evaluators finds roughly two-thirds of an interface's usability problems, dramatically more than any single evaluator, because evaluators find largely non-overlapping problem sets (the data does not form a cumulative Guttman scale)."

**Source:** https://concreta.com.uy/wp-content/uploads/nielsenheuristicsCHI.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "In general, we would expect aggregates of five evaluators to find about two thirds of the usability problems which is really quite good for an informal and inexpensive technique like heuristic evaluation."

*Evidence:* Every component of the claim is supported by the primary source (Nielsen & Molich, CHI 1990) and corroborated by NN/g. (1) Two-thirds/five evaluators: the paper states verbatim "we would expect aggregates of five evaluators to find about ⅔ of the usability problems" — exactly the supplied supporting quote. (2) Dramatically more than a single evaluator: individual evaluators found only 20–51% of problems in the four original experiments (NN/g later reports ~35% averaged across six projects), so ~67% from five is a large jump. (3) Non-overlapping problem sets: NN/g (Nielsen) states there is "a substantial amount of nonoverlap between the sets of usability problems found by different evaluators" and "different people find different usability problems" — the stated rationale for aggregation. (4) No Guttman scale: confirmed that the aggregated evaluations did NOT support a Guttman-scale hypothesis (poor evaluators found hard problems; good evaluators missed easy ones). Source is the canonical peer-reviewed CHI paper (1,000+ citations), matching the claim's strength. Known critiques (Faulkner 2003; Cockton & Woolrych 2001; Spool & Schroeder 2001) qualify the GENERALIZATION of "five is enough" in usability TESTING, but do not contradict the original empirical heuristic-evaluation finding, and the claim is appropriately hedged ("roughly," "about five"). Not outdated — NN/g still recommends three to five evaluators. The scanned PDF (CCITTFax image) blocked direct text extraction, but web search and NN/g independently confirm the exact wording.
*Counter-source:* No source refutes the claim. Qualifying (not refuting) critiques: Faulkner 2003 (Behavior Research Methods); Cockton & Woolrych 2001 "A Sale Must End"; Spool & Schroeder 2001 — these dispute the generalizability of the "magic number five" in usability testing, not Nielsen & Molich's original heuristic-evaluation result.

#### V168 `a351698c` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The widely-held '5 users is enough' assumption is unreliable: in Faulkner's controlled resampling of 60 real test users, different random sets of 5 participants ranged from catching 99% of usability problems down to only 55% — so a single 5-user test can miss nearly half the problems. This is the empirical counterweight to Nielsen/Landauer's discount-usability rule."

**Source:** https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/BF03195514 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Some of the randomly selected sets of 5 participants found 99% of the problems; other sets found only 55%."

*Evidence:* Claim is fully supported by the primary source and an independent corroborator. Faulkner (2003), "Beyond the five-user assumption," Behavior Research Methods, Instruments, & Computers 35(3):379-383, DOI 10.3758/BF03195514 (PMID 14587545). Confirmed by PubMed (independent of Springer) AND the Springer abstract: 60 total users tested; Monte Carlo resampling drew random sets; sets of 5 found between 55% and 99% of usability problems; min rises to 80% at 10 users and 95% at 20 users. The claim's "miss nearly half" reading is mathematically correct (55% found = 45% missed). Peer-reviewed primary source, matches claim strength. The "counterweight to Nielsen/Landauer's discount-usability rule" framing is accurate and not an overreach — the paper exists specifically to quantify the variance around the ~85% average underlying the five-user/discount-usability guideline, and presents itself as a qualification, not a refutation. No contradicting credible source found; numbers are reproduced identically across sources.

#### V171 `a4aed487` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled experiment, increasing the depth and/or breadth of a hierarchical menu significantly increased reaction time, error rates, and perceived complexity — establishing that deeper hierarchies impose measurable performance costs."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.2466/pms.1996.82.3c.1187 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "As depth and/or breadth increased, reaction time, error rates, and perceived complexity all increased significantly."

*Evidence:* The claim faithfully reports a real primary controlled experiment: Jacko & Salvendy (1996), "Hierarchical Menu Design: Breadth, Depth, and Task Complexity," Perceptual and Motor Skills, 82, 1187-1201 (the cited DOI). Within-subjects design, 12 subjects, six structures (2^2, 2^3, 2^6, 8^2, 8^3, 8^6), DVs = reaction time and accuracy, plus subjective complexity. The reported result matches the supporting quote nearly verbatim: as depth and/or breadth increased, reaction time, error rates, and perceived complexity all increased significantly (authors attribute the complexity effect to short-term-memory load). The quote supports the claim without overreach. The broader literature corroborates rather than contradicts the headline ("deeper hierarchies impose measurable performance costs"): Miller 1981, Snowberry et al. 1983, Kiger 1984, Landauer & Nachbar 1985, Larson & Czerwinski 1998, and Zaphiris all find depth costs more than breadth ("breadth over depth"). Source quality (peer-reviewed primary experiment) matches the claim strength, and the finding is reinforced, not overturned, by later work. One caveat the skill should carry, but which does NOT refute the claim: Jacko & Salvendy did not hold total item count constant (2^2 = 4 items vs 8^6 = 262,144 items), so the "breadth raises cost" half is partly a trivial more-items-to-scan effect; the cleaner, methodologically stronger "depth costs more than breadth at fixed item count" result actually comes from Larson & Czerwinski 1998 (512 nodes; 16x32 fastest > 32x16 > 8x8x8 slowest). n=12 and the mid-tier journal are minor weaknesses offset by replication across the literature.
*Counter-source:* https://www.cs.umd.edu/hcil/trs/99-15/99-15.html (Larson & Czerwinski 1998 — supports depth-cost finding at fixed item count, and shows the cleaner methodology Jacko & Salvendy lacked, but does not refute the claim)

(1) The quote is near-verbatim in the paper: "by keeping frequently-used targets close to the tracker (e.g., with a pop-up menu), or by increasing the target-icon size for longer movements." The claim is a faithful paraphrase, not an overreach. (2) The "pointer" substitution is accurate: the paper uses "tracker" to mean the pointing-device cursor (mouse pointer, stylus tip), so generalizing to "pointer" is not a misread. (3) No contradicting evidence — Fitts's Law (MT = a + b·log2(2D/W): larger width W and shorter distance D both reduce movement time) is among the most replicated findings in HCI since 1954; corroborated by NN/g, Interaction Design Foundation, The Decision Lab, and Laws of UX. #### V177 `a6c74e01` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Leftmost words receive more fixations than later words on the same line, which is why information-bearing words should be front-loaded in headings and links."
