DESCRIPTIVE half: The NN/g article (f-shaped-pattern-reading-web-content) states verbatim that "First few words on the left of each line of text receive more fixations than subsequent words on the same line" — matching the supporting quote exactly, not an overreach. This is corroborated independently by NN/g's separate "Horizontal Attention Leans Left" study and by the well-documented leftward attentional bias / optimal-viewing-position findings in reading research. PRESCRIPTIVE half ("front-load information-bearing words"): this is NOT the verifier's own inference — it is NN/g's explicit recommendation in the SAME article: "Start headings and subheadings with the words carrying most information: if users see only the first 2 words, they should still get the gist," and links should "include information-bearing words (instead of generic 'go', 'click here' or 'more')." SOURCE QUALITY: NN/g eye-tracking research (original 2006 study, reaffirmed in the 2017 update titled "Misunderstood, But Still Relevant"); current and primary-grade for a UX best-practice claim. No credible source disputing or heavily qualifying the front-loading guidance was found in search; the common "criticism" of the F-pattern is that it signals poor scanning of bad content (so use headings/bullets to break it) — which REINFORCES, not refutes, front-loading important words. Only scope caveat: applies to left-to-right reading languages (RTL mirrors); this qualifies, it does not refute. Appropriate tag for a skill: [STRONG] for the fixation-distribution finding, [CONVENTION/best-practice — NN/g] for the front-loading prescription.
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source found. Closest "criticism" (NN/g 2017 "Misunderstood, But Still Relevant") reinforces rather than refutes: F-pattern is a fallback to avoid via headings/bullets, but front-loading information-bearing words remains NN/g's standing recommendation.

#### V178 `a6e4f26e` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled experiment, increasing the depth and/or breadth of a hierarchical menu significantly increased reaction time, error rates, and perceived complexity — establishing that deeper hierarchies impose measurable performance costs."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.2466/pms.1996.82.3c.1187 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "As depth and/or breadth increased, reaction time, error rates, and perceived complexity all increased significantly."

*Evidence:* Claim is faithful to the source and corroborated by the menu-design literature. Source identified: Jacko & Salvendy (1996), "Hierarchical Menu Design: Breadth, Depth, and Task Complexity," Perceptual and Motor Skills 82(3c):1187-1201. Controlled experiment, 12 subjects, six structures (2², 2³, 2⁶, 8², 8³, 8⁶ = breadth^depth); IVs = depth and breadth; DVs = response time, accuracy/errors, perceived complexity. Multiple independent summaries confirm the verbatim finding: "as depth and/or breadth increased, reaction time, error rates, and perceived complexity all increased significantly," with short-term memory load as the cognitive driver. The claim does not overreach the quote. No contradicting evidence found — the broader corpus corroborates depth being costly: Miller (1981, 2-level/8-choice menu fastest, fewest errors), Snowberry et al. (1983, broad-shallow superiority replicated), Landauer & Nachbar (1985, increased breadth reduces response time). The famous "breadth favored over depth" consensus is NOT a contradiction: it is a fixed-total-items tradeoff, whereas Jacko & Salvendy's factorial design adds total items as either dimension grows (8⁶ ≈ 262k leaf nodes), so both dimensions raising cost is expected. Source quality is sufficient: Salvendy is a leading human-factors researcher (editor, Handbook of Human Factors), peer-reviewed, well-cited; not marketing. Caveat for downstream use (not a refutation): must not be misread as "narrow menus are better" — the actionable lesson is to favor breadth over depth for a fixed item count, since cost rises more steeply with depth. Minor: n=12 makes "establishing" slightly strong for one study, but it is robust across the corroborating literature.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found; Miller 1981, Snowberry et al. 1983, and Landauer & Nachbar 1985 all corroborate that depth imposes performance costs (breadth-favored consensus is a fixed-total tradeoff, not a contradiction of the factorial finding).

#### V179 `a6fb688c` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Fitts' law in HCI is modeled as a linear regression of movement time on index of difficulty, MT = a + b × ID, with the Shannon formulation ID = log₂(2D/W), so movement time increases with target distance D and decreases with target width W."

**Source:** https://www.yorku.ca/mack/ijhcs2004.html (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "MT = a + b × ID ... ID = log₂(2D/W)"

*Evidence:* The claim mislabels the formula. The cited source (Soukoreff & MacKenzie 2004, "Towards a Standard for Pointing Device Evaluation: Perspectives on 27 Years of Fitts' Law Research in HCI," IJHCS) explicitly defines the SHANNON formulation as ID = log₂(D/W + 1) — its Equation 1, the recommended/ISO-9241-9 standard form. The claim's ID = log₂(2D/W) is the ORIGINAL Fitts (1954) formulation, NOT the Shannon formulation; the claim swaps the labels. This is contradicted by the cited paper itself and corroborated by MacKenzie's canonical work (MacKenzie 1989/1992) and his note "On the Validity of the Shannon Formulation" (yorku.ca/mack/ojas2013). The distinction is substantive, not cosmetic: log₂(2D/W) produces negative ID when D/W < 0.5, and removing exactly that defect (always non-negative ID, better empirical fit) is the entire reason the Shannon form log₂(D/W+1) was introduced. CORRECT parts of the claim: the linear-regression form MT = a + b × ID is right, and MT increasing with distance D / decreasing with width W is right. But the load-bearing named-formula assertion ("the Shannon formulation ID = log₂(2D/W)") is factually wrong per its own source. The right actionable statement for a skill: Fitts' law MT = a + b·log₂(D/W + 1) (Shannon form), where log₂(2D/W) is the older original Fitts form.
*Counter-source:* Soukoreff & MacKenzie (2004), IJHCS, the cited paper itself: Shannon formulation = ID = log₂(D/W + 1) (Eq. 1); original Fitts = log₂(2D/W). Corroborated by MacKenzie (1992) CHI and yorku.ca/mack/ojas2013.html.

Web search returned the study's findings verbatim matching the supporting quote: it tested six menu structures (2^2, 2^3, 2^6, 8^2, 8^3, 8^6) and found "as depth and/or breadth increased, reaction time, error rates, and perceived complexity all increased significantly." The claim faithfully restates this — no overreach or misread. Source tier (controlled experiment) matches the STRONG claim. But the claim's actual stated conclusion ("deeper hierarchies impose measurable performance costs") is the well-supported part and is accurately reported. Not outdated (stable cognition finding), not marketing.
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source for the depth-cost conclusion. Only refinement: the broad-shallow-vs-narrow-deep literature (Kiger 1984; Landauer & Nachbar 1985) holds total items fixed, whereas this study confounds breadth/depth with total item count — so the "breadth" portion of the finding is a complexity effect, not a verdict that breadth per se is harmful.

#### V186 `a77074cd` — **upheld** · confidence: high

**Source:** https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/BF03195514 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "With 10 users, the lowest percentage of problems revealed by any one set was increased to 80%, and with 20 users, to 95%."

*Evidence:* Source verified as Faulkner, L. (2003), "Beyond the five-user assumption: Benefits of increased sample sizes in usability testing," Behavior Research Methods, Instruments, & Computers 35(3):379-383 (DOI 10.3758/BF03195514; PubMed 14587545) — a primary peer-reviewed article, matching the claim's strength. The supporting quote is verbatim from the paper: "With 10 users, the lowest percentage of problems revealed by any one set was increased to 80%, and with 20 users, to 95%." The claim's restatement (minimum/worst-case detection floor rises: 10 users -> 80%, 20 -> 95%) faithfully maps "lowest percentage revealed by any one set" to "worst-case floor" — no overreach. Method backs the framing: Faulkner tested 60 users on one interface, then drew random sets; sets of 5 ranged 55%-99% (mean ~85%), and larger samples raise the floor — exactly her thesis about the marginal value of testing beyond 5 users. No credible contradiction: the contested literature (Spool & Schroeder 2001, Woolrych & Cockton 2001) argues against 5-user sufficiency, which reinforces this claim. Not outdated (sampling-variance result, still widely cited). Not marketing. Sole caveat (non-refuting): numbers are dataset-specific (one interface, 60-user pool) and the "minimum" is lowest-observed across random samples, not a guaranteed theoretical floor — but the claim scopes them as concrete study numbers, which is accurate.

The supporting quote is reproduced verbatim across multiple independent sources. The "miss nearly half" framing is accurate (55% found = 45% missed at the worst-case set). Source is a peer-reviewed, widely-cited journal article — quality matches the empirical claim's strength. Not refutation-worthy.

"Default" verbatim: "The F-pattern is the default pattern when there are no strong cues to attract the eyes toward meaningful information." Conditions verbatim: occurs when text has "little or no formatting," user "trying to be most efficient," and "not so committed... willing to read every word." The claim is properly conditioned and does NOT overclaim universality or make a prescriptive "design for the F" assertion (the actual myth NN/g's update corrects). The strongest contradicting source (EyeQuant, 99 sites/46 users, found no F-bias) targets the UNIVERSAL/unconditioned version on formatted production sites — consistent with, not contradictory to, the conditioned claim, since NN/g holds that formatting disrupts the F-pattern. Caveat: NN/g eye-tracking is practitioner research, not peer-reviewed controls, so "confirmed" is mildly strong; but the brief explicitly sanctions NN/g for descriptive eye-tracking findings, and this is descriptive not prescriptive.
*Counter-source:* EyeQuant quantitative analysis (99 websites, 46 participants each) found no evidence of a universal F-shape bias — https://www.eyequant.com/resources/eye-tracking-studies-does-the-famous-f-shape-pattern-really-exist — but this disputes the unconditioned/universal version, not the carefully-conditioned claim under review.

#### V191 `a8ee35ff` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Increasing sample size raises the worst-case (minimum) problem-detection floor: with 10 users the lowest any set detected rose to 80%, and with 20 users to 95%. This gives concrete numbers for the marginal value of testing beyond 5 users."

**Source:** https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/BF03195514 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "With 10 users, the lowest percentage of problems revealed by any one set was increased to 80%, and with 20 users, to 95%."

*Evidence:* VERBATIM MATCH to the primary peer-reviewed source. The claim cites Laura Faulkner (2003), "Beyond the five-user assumption: Benefits of increased sample sizes in usability testing," Behavior Research Methods, Instruments, & Computers 35(3):379-383, DOI 10.3758/BF03195514. The abstract (confirmed via PubMed PMID 14587545 and Springer) states word-for-word: "With 10 users, the lowest percentage of problems revealed by any one set was increased to 80%, and with 20 users, to 95%." METHOD: 60 users tested on one interface; random subsets repeatedly drawn; sets of 5 ranged from 55% to 99% of problems found. The claim's paraphrase — "worst-case (minimum) problem-detection floor" — accurately mirrors the source's own phrase "lowest percentage of problems revealed by any one set." CHECKS: (1) Quote support — exact, no overreach. (2) Contradicting evidence — none; the finding is a widely-cited landmark, frequently used to qualify the Nielsen-Landauer "5 users" rule. (3) Source quality — peer-reviewed primary study, matches claim strength. (4) Currency — 2003, but it is a stated historical empirical finding about testing methodology (not a fast-moving fact); still cited as-is in 2020s NN/g and UX literature. (5) Not marketing/cherry-picked — peer-reviewed journal. ONE FAIR NUANCE (does not refute): the 80%/95% figures are empirical minima from random draws on ONE interface's 60-user pool against a 100%-baseline defined as what those 60 found — they are study-specific minima, not universal guaranteed floors. The claim does not overstate this; it explicitly frames the numbers as study results illustrating the marginal value of testing beyond 5 users, matching how the source presents them.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. The only adjacent caveat is that the figures are single-study, single-interface minima (the standard limitation of subset-resampling studies like Nielsen-Landauer 1993), but no source disputes the reported numbers themselves.

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by the primary source (Nielsen & Molich, "Heuristic Evaluation of User Interfaces," Proc. CHI 1990, pp. 249-256). (1) Quote support: the cited sentence "they only found between 20 and 51% of the usability problems" is verbatim from that paper and directly establishes the 20-51% range. (2) Per-experiment values (51%, 38%, 26%, 20%) are corroborated independently — a WebSearch returned "the proportion of usability problems found in four experiments was 51%, 38%, 26%, and 20% with single evaluators," matching the four studies. (3) Math: finding 20-51% means missing 49-80%, accurately restated as "roughly half to four-fifths." (4) Source strength: peer-reviewed CHI primary source, 3,500+ citations, appropriate for the claim. A later Nielsen (1992) figure (single evaluators ~35% averaged over six studies; ~42% major / ~32% minor problems) is complementary, not contradictory. Known critiques (Spool/Schroeder, Cockton/Woolrych) target the multi-evaluator aggregation/sample-size claims, not these single-evaluator detection rates. Caveat: could not text-extract the PDF locally (no pdftotext/PDF libraries installed), so the four mid-values rest on independent secondary corroboration rather than direct re-reading; the 20-51% range is confirmed verbatim.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. Nielsen (1992) ~35% average over six studies is a complementary dataset, not a contradiction; HE critiques (Spool & Schroeder; Cockton & Woolrych) dispute the 5-evaluator aggregation math, not single-evaluator detection rates.

#### V193 `a9e4d9de` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The authors recommend using between three and five evaluators for a heuristic evaluation; the number of problems found rises steeply from one to five evaluators and hits diminishing returns around ten, so resources beyond five are better spent on other evaluation methods."

**Source:** https://concreta.com.uy/wp-content/uploads/nielsenheuristicsCHI.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "We recommend that heuristic evaluation is done with between three and five evaluators and that any additional resources are spent on alternative methods of evaluation."

*Evidence:* Claim is faithful to the primary source (Nielsen & Molich 1990, CHI '90, "Heuristic Evaluation of User Interfaces"). The two load-bearing parts are DIRECTLY in the supplied verbatim quote: "We recommend that heuristic evaluation is done with between three and five evaluators and that any additional resources are spent on alternative methods of evaluation." NN/g's authoritative restatement of the paper confirms "three to five evaluators... one does not gain that much additional information by using larger numbers." The empirical-curve part ("rises steeply 1->5, diminishing returns around ten") is an interpretive paraphrase not in the quote but is accurate to the paper's data: individual evaluators found 20-51% of problems (NN/g cites ~35% single, ~85% at five evaluators); the paper's detection-vs-evaluators curve (Fig. 2) flattens, with optimal benefit-cost at ~4 evaluators. "Steep rise 1-5, flat by ~10" matches the actual curve shape. Minor looseness: "around ten" as the diminishing-returns point is imprecise (returns decline from the start; recommended stop is ~4-5) but not misleading. Known critiques (evaluator effect; Woolrych & Cockton 2001 'Why and when five test users aren't enough'; Spool & Schroeder 2001) attack the generalizability of a fixed number / the binomial model's equal-detection assumption — they QUALIFY but do not refute a claim explicitly attributed to what "the authors recommend." Primary academic source matches the claim's strength; recommendation still cited as standard in current (2026) NN/g and IxDF sources. Not marketing, not outdated for an attribution claim.
*Counter-source:* Woolrych & Cockton (2001) "Why and when five test users aren't enough" and the evaluator-effect literature (Hertzum & Jacobsen) qualify the fixed-number recommendation but do not contradict the attribution; the supplied verbatim quote and NN/g's restatement directly support the core claim.

#### V194 `a9ffe91e` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The authors recommend using between three and five evaluators for a heuristic evaluation; the number of problems found rises steeply from one to five evaluators and hits diminishing returns around ten, so resources beyond five are better spent on other evaluation methods."

**Source:** https://concreta.com.uy/wp-content/uploads/nielsenheuristicsCHI.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "We recommend that heuristic evaluation is done with between three and five evaluators and that any additional resources are spent on alternative methods of evaluation."

*Evidence:* Claim is verbatim-supported by the cited primary source. I extracted the actual PDF text of Nielsen & Molich, "Heuristic Evaluation of User Interfaces," CHI '90. The paper's conclusion reads: "[problems] found by aggregates of evaluators grows rapidly in the interval from one to five evaluators but reaches the point of diminishing returns around the point of ten evaluators. We recommend that heuristic evaluation is done with between three and five evaluators and that any additional resources are spent on alternative methods of evaluation." The body restates it: "Each curve rises drastically in the interval from one evaluator to about five evaluators... the point of diminishing returns seems to have been reached at aggregates of about ten evaluators... we would expect aggregates of five evaluators to find about two thirds of the usability problems." Every clause of the claim (3-5 evaluators, steep rise 1-to-5, diminishing returns ~10, spend extra resources on alternative methods) maps directly onto the source text. The supporting quote is exact. Source is the foundational peer-reviewed primary (CHI '90); the 3-5 figure remains standard guidance and is not contradicted (general critiques of heuristic evaluation target false-positives/complementarity with user testing, not this recommendation). Note: an initial WebFetch returned a cache-collision PDF (a Delta-E color survey), but re-fetching the actual URL yielded the genuine Nielsen & Molich paper, which I parsed with PyMuPDF.
*Counter-source:* No credible refutation found. Searches (IxDF, NN/g, Wikipedia, MIT 6.813) all corroborate the 3-5 evaluator recommendation and the diminishing-returns-around-ten curve. The only adjacent "qualifications" in the literature (Cockton & Woolrych; comparisons of heuristic evaluation vs. user testing) critique false positives and what kinds of problems the method finds, not the evaluator-count recommendation itself.

#### V195 `aa17372e` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Position on the page biases attention: earlier lines and the leftmost words of each line get disproportionately more fixations, so content placed lower or to the right is more likely to be skipped."

**Supporting quote:** "First lines of text on a page receive more gazes than subsequent lines of text"

WebFetch confirmed verbatim: (1) top bias — "First lines of text on a page receive more gazes than subsequent lines of text on the same page"; (2) left bias — "First few words on the left of each line of text receive more fixations than subsequent words on the same line"; (3) lower/right skipped — users "skip important content simply because it appears on the right side of the page" and "miss big chunks of content based merely on how text flows in a column." This is a faithful descriptive summary, appropriately hedged ("biases," "more likely to be skipped"), not an overreach.

Source quality matches the claim's strength: NN/g's original finding rests on eye-tracking of 232 users; the claim is descriptive eye-tracking, exactly what NN/g is authoritative for. Not outdated — NN/g re-affirmed it in 2017 ("Misunderstood, But Still Relevant"). Not marketing.

I searched specifically for refutations. The strongest is EyeQuant's analysis ("no evidence that bias towards an F-Shape exists at all"). But that critique attacks a STRONGER claim — the F-SHAPE as a universal/rigid layout template — not the narrower position-bias tendency under review. Even the critics concede users "spend most fixations near where they start reading (usually the top left)," which AFFIRMS this claim's core. The valid qualification is only that the bias is a default that good design (headings, bullets, bold → "layer-cake" pattern) can counteract; the claim's hedged wording already accommodates this. So the contradiction refutes the universal-layout-law version, not this descriptive position-bias statement.
*Counter-source:* https://www.eyequant.com/resources/eye-tracking-studies-does-the-famous-f-shape-pattern-really-exist (disputes F-shape as a universal layout rule, but not the top-left position-bias tendency this claim makes)

#### V197 `aa2c6d08` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "F-pattern scanning is a symptom of weak visual hierarchy rather than a target to design for; adding strong cues (prominent headings, bold key phrases, bullets, front-loaded keywords, trimmed content) redirects the eye away from the inefficient F."

**Source:** https://www.nngroup.com/articles/f-shaped-pattern-reading-web-content/ (primary)

*Evidence:* Claim is faithful to its primary source (NN/g, the firm that discovered the F-pattern via eye-tracking). The article explicitly states each component: F-pattern emerges from poor formatting ("If your pages have big chunks of unformatted text, people will scan it in an F-shape"), it is undesirable ("The F-shaped scanning pattern is bad for users and businesses"; "Good design can prevent F-shape scanning"; "Good web formatting reduces the impact of F-scanning"), and the cure is exactly the cue list in the claim — prominent headings, front-loaded keywords ("Start headings and subheadings with the words carrying most information"), bold key phrases, bulleted lists, grouping, and trimming superfluous content. The supporting quote about users skipping right-side content is verbatim. Source quality (NN/g) is more than sufficient for this CONVENTION/best-practice-level claim; not extraordinary, not outdated (2006 original, 2017 update, reaffirmed by Smashing Magazine 2024), not marketing. Three refutation attempts (paraphrase overreach, strength mismatch, contradicting sources) all failed. One honest caveat: the evidence is observational eye-tracking + expert guidance, not a controlled experiment causally proving the redirect — but the claim is pitched as best practice, not proven science, so the strength matches.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found; recent secondary coverage (Smashing Magazine 2024, https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2024/04/f-shape-pattern-how-users-read/) echoes rather than disputes NN/g. Only nuance: formatting changes the scan shape (to layer-cake/spotted) rather than producing full reading — consistent with, not contrary to, the claim.

#### V198 `ab6dc595` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The F-shaped scanning pattern (two horizontal sweeps across the top, then a vertical scan down the left edge) is confirmed by eye-tracking on both desktop and mobile; it is the default scan when text is unformatted and users are scanning efficiently with low commitment."

**Source:** https://www.nngroup.com/articles/f-shaped-pattern-reading-web-content/ (primary)

(1) Description: article describes a horizontal top movement, a shorter second horizontal movement, and a vertical scan down the left = the F. (2) Mobile+desktop: "the F-shaped scanning pattern is alive and well... both on desktop and on mobile." (3) Default with unformatted text: "The F-pattern is the default pattern when there are no strong cues to attract the eyes toward meaningful information." (4) Efficiency/low commitment: F-scan occurs when "text has little or no formatting," the user "is trying to be most efficient," and "is not so committed or interested that he is willing to read every word." The claim is a faithful, appropriately-hedged paraphrase, not an overreach. The claim under review does not assert universality; it is explicitly conditional (default WHEN unformatted AND low-commitment), which matches NN/g's own caveat that scanning "does not always take the shape of an F" and that other patterns (layer-cake, spotted, inverted-L/E variants) exist. So EyeQuant does not refute this conditional claim. Source quality (NN/g eye-tracking, 232 users in the 2006 original + 2017 mobile follow-up) is appropriate for this UX convention-grade descriptive claim and is current. The claim is also correctly descriptive, not the prescriptive "design for the F" error NN/g explicitly warns against. One caveat for synthesis: tag as descriptive eye-tracking (NN/g), note it is conditional/non-universal and contested by EyeQuant, and pair it with NN/g's actual prescription (format content so the eye is NOT left to default into an F).

#### V199 `ab8ca7c4` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Error rate stops improving above a threshold: there was no statistically significant reduction in error rate for target sizes >= 9.6 mm in discrete tasks and >= 7.7 mm in serial tasks (n=20, RM-ANOVA, Bonferroni-corrected post-hoc)."

**Source:** https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/research/wp-content/uploads/2006/01/parhi-mobileHCI06.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "there were no significant differences in error rate between target sizes ≥ 9.6 mm in discrete tasks and targets ≥ 7.7 mm in serial tasks"

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary source (Parhi, Karlson & Bederson, "Target Size Study for One-Handed Thumb Use on Small Touchscreen Devices," MobileHCI 2006) and its extended HCIL tech report (UMD 2006-11). Every element of the claim checks out: (1) Supporting quote is verbatim from the paper's abstract — I decompressed the PDF content streams directly and stream 1 (abstract) contains the exact lead-in "...there were no significant differences in error rate between target sizes..." (numeric thresholds were truncated in extraction only because the body uses CID-encoded subset fonts that don't decode to ASCII). (2) Thresholds confirmed by the detailed Bonferroni post-hoc results: SERIAL — smallest keys (5.8 mm) caused significantly more errors than keys >= 9.6 mm "with no differences between error rates for the other key sizes," i.e. 7.7 mm and up are statistically indistinguishable -> threshold >= 7.7 mm; DISCRETE — 7.7 mm still differed significantly from the largest target but 9.6 mm and up did not differ -> threshold >= 9.6 mm. The counterintuitive asymmetry (discrete 9.6 > serial 7.7) is exactly what the abstract states. (3) Method confirmed: a 5 (target size) x 4 (location) repeated-measures ANOVA on percent error with Bonferroni-corrected post-hoc comparisons. (4) n=20 confirmed: HCIL tech report states "Twenty participants (17 male, 3 female)," the same group for both phases. Source is a peer-reviewed, award-winning (Best Historical Paper, MobileHCI 2016), heavily cited primary paper — quality matches claim strength. No contradicting source found; secondary breakdowns corroborate. Only trivial non-issue: tested-size lists differ slightly between the conference paper (5.8-13.4 mm) and tech report (discrete 3.8-11.5 mm), but 7.7 and 9.6 mm were tested in both, so the thresholds are unaffected.

#### V203 `ac55e1bc` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "For one-handed thumb use on small touchscreen handhelds, controlled study recommends minimum target size of 9.2 mm for single-target (discrete) tasks like buttons/checkboxes and 9.6 mm for multi-target (serial) tasks like numeric/text entry."

**Supporting quote:** "we recommend that target sizes should be at least 9.2 mm for single-target tasks and 9.6 mm for multi-target tasks in order to keep the dimensions of the targets as small as possible without decreasing performance and user preference."

"Minimum" framing matches the supporting quote ("at least 9.2 mm... as small as possible without decreasing performance and user preference"). Not outdated — finger/thumb ergonomics are a stable property and the numbers remain cited by NN/g and UXmatters. Known limitations (target/interference conflation, restricted constant thumb-travel distance, small task area) are methodological caveats, not contradictions of the recommended sizes. Source is a primary peer-reviewed ACM conference paper, sufficient for the modest claim strength.
*Counter-source:* No credible source contradicts the figures. Caveats only (UXmatters "Common Misconceptions About Touch", uxmatters.com/mt/archives/2013/03): the study often placed test items adjacent (conflating target size and inter-target interference) and constrained thumb-travel distance — limitations on generalizability, not refutations of the 9.2/9.6 mm recommendations. Different bodies (Apple 44pt, Material 48dp) prescribe other minimums but do not dispute this study's findings.

#### V205 `ac6d8065` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Severity ratings from a single usability evaluator are unreliable, but the mean severity rating from four evaluators lands within half a point of the true severity 95% of the time — i.e., reliable severity estimates require aggregating multiple raters, not one expert."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/1125021.1125117 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Ratings from single evaluators are very unreliable when usability specialists judge the severity of usability problems found by heuristic evaluation, but the mean severity rating from four evaluators gets within half a rating point of the true severity 95% of the time."

*Evidence:* The cited DOI 10.1145/1125021.1125117 resolves precisely to Jakob Nielsen (1992), "Reliability of severity estimates for usability problems found by heuristic evaluation," Posters and Short Talks of the 1992 SIGCHI Conference. The supporting quote is that paper's abstract, reproduced essentially verbatim: "Ratings from single evaluators are very unreliable... but the mean severity rating from four evaluators gets within half a rating point of the true severity 95% of the time." The claim is a faithful restatement of this; its gloss ("reliable severity estimates require aggregating multiple raters, not one expert") is an accurate interpretation, not an overreach. Two independent web searches confirmed authorship, year, venue, and the exact numeric wording. The finding is consistent with Nielsen's widely-cited NN/g guidance recommending 3-4 evaluators for reliable severity ratings. The source is the appropriate primary citation, matching the claim's strength. Caveat (does not refute): it is a small 1992 short-talk/poster, and "true severity" is defined as the aggregate of many raters (mildly circular) — but the claim accurately represents what the source says and no contradicting evidence was found.

#### V206 `ace08ec5` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In four controlled experiments, individual evaluators performing heuristic evaluation found only 20-51% of the known usability problems in an interface (per-experiment averages: 51%, 38%, 26%, 20%), so a single evaluator misses roughly half to four-fifths of problems."

**Source:** https://concreta.com.uy/wp-content/uploads/nielsenheuristicsCHI.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Four experiments showed that individual evaluators were mostly quite bad at doing such heuristic evaluations and that they only found between 20 and 51% of the usability problems in the interfaces they evaluated."

*Evidence:* The claim accurately cites Nielsen & Molich, "Heuristic Evaluation of User Interfaces," CHI 1990 (the canonical PRIMARY source — highest possible quality for this claim). VERIFIED elements: (1) The headline range "between 20 and 51%" is a direct quote from the paper, independently corroborated by NN/g, Wikipedia, and multiple academic summaries. (2) The four systems were Savings + Transport (voice-response) and Teledata + Mantel (screen dumps). (3) Two of the four per-experiment single-evaluator averages are independently confirmed via the paper's aggregation tables: WebSearch returned "For Teledata, aggregates of evaluators found 51% (1 evaluator)..." and "For Mantel, the rates were 38% (1)..." — exactly matching the claim's 51% and 38%. The remaining two values (26%, 20%) are consistent: the quoted "20 and 51%" range is precisely the min/max bracket of the four per-experiment means, so 20% is the confirmed floor and 26% sits inside the range. The interpretive gloss ("misses roughly half to four-fifths") is arithmetically correct: 100−51=49% (~half), 100−20=80% (four-fifths). NO contradicting evidence found — the later "evaluator effect" literature (Hertzum & Jacobsen 2001) REINFORCES the low individual hit-rate and high variability, and NN/g's own figure (35% mean across six different projects, λ range 19–51%) is in the same neighborhood. The claim is not outdated (it correctly attributes a specific 1990 study still cited as foundational), not a marketing/benchmark claim, and well-matched to its source strength. Minor caveat: I could not word-for-word reconfirm the specific 26% value in an independent source within this session (pdftotext/pypdf unavailable; the linked PDF could not be text-extracted locally), but the range and the two highest anchor values were confirmed against the primary data, and the claim is faithful to the source. NOTE: the claim attributes these per-experiment averages to the 1990 paper, distinct from the often-confused "35% average over six projects" figure that comes from Nielsen's later work — the claim correctly keeps these separate.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. Searches surfaced only corroborating material (NN/g "Theory Behind Heuristic Evaluations," Wikipedia, Semantic Scholar, the swizec.com summary, and the Hertzum & Jacobsen "Evaluator Effect" paper), all consistent with the claim; none disputes the 20–51% range or the per-experiment values.

The normative Spacing exception reads: "Undersized targets (those less than 24 by 24 CSS pixels) are positioned so that if a 24 CSS pixel diameter circle is centered on the bounding box of each, the circles do not intersect another target or the circle for another undersized target." Checklist: (1) quote fully supports claim, no overreach; (2) no contradicting evidence — this is the official W3C standard text, not disputed; (3) source quality is maximal (W3C, the normative authority that defines the criterion); (4) current — WCAG 2.2 is the live W3C Recommendation as of 2026; (5) not marketing, it is a normative accessibility standard. One nuance worth flagging for the skill author: this is a [CONVENTION/normative-standard] claim (a definitional rule of the WCAG spec), not an [STRONG] empirical-research finding — its truth is that the standard says so, not that controlled studies validated the 24px circle threshold.

#### V216 `ae290ece` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The authors recommend using between three and five evaluators for a heuristic evaluation; the number of problems found rises steeply from one to five evaluators and hits diminishing returns around ten, so resources beyond five are better spent on other evaluation methods."

**Source:** https://concreta.com.uy/wp-content/uploads/nielsenheuristicsCHI.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "We recommend that heuristic evaluation is done with between three and five evaluators and that any additional resources are spent on alternative methods of evaluation."

*Evidence:* The core of the claim is directly supported by the verbatim source quote: "between three and five evaluators" and "additional resources are spent on alternative methods of evaluation" — the canonical Nielsen & Molich (CHI 1990) recommendation. The added curve detail is corroborated by independent sources: a single evaluator finds ~35% of usability problems (Nielsen & Molich reported a per-evaluator range of 20-51%), 5 evaluators find ~75%, with the aggregate detection curve rising steeply 1→5 then flattening — which is precisely why 3-5 is recommended. Source is primary and authoritative (CHI 1990), current (still the standard reference; NN/g still recommends 3-5), and not marketing. One MINOR imprecision: the claim places diminishing returns "around ten," but Nielsen's cost-benefit analysis puts the ONSET of diminishing returns near 5 (the reason the recommendation is 3-5, not 10); by ~10-15 evaluators the curve is essentially saturated. So "around ten" describes the plateau/saturation point rather than the onset — a wording nuance, not a contradiction. It does not undermine the actionable claim. (PDF text extraction failed locally — no pdftotext/PDF lib available — but WebSearch independently confirmed all key figures.)
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. Closest critique — Spool & Schroeder (2001) and Faulkner (2003) — disputes the "5 users" rule for usability TESTING with end users, not the "3-5 evaluators" rule for heuristic evaluation, so it does not refute this claim. Minor caveat only: "diminishing returns around ten" is imprecise; Nielsen's cost-benefit model puts the onset near 5, saturation near 10-15.

#### V217 `ae47df15` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Error rate stops improving above a threshold: there was no statistically significant reduction in error rate for target sizes >= 9.6 mm in discrete tasks and >= 7.7 mm in serial tasks (n=20, RM-ANOVA, Bonferroni-corrected post-hoc)."

**Source:** https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/research/wp-content/uploads/2006/01/parhi-mobileHCI06.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "there were no significant differences in error rate between target sizes ≥ 9.6 mm in discrete tasks and targets ≥ 7.7 mm in serial tasks"

*Evidence:* Every element of the claim is confirmed verbatim in the primary source (Parhi, Karlson & Bederson, "Target Size Study for One-Handed Thumb Use on Small Touchscreen Devices," MobileHCI 2006, Microsoft Research PDF). I extracted the PDF text directly. (1) n=20: "Twenty participants (17 male, 3 female) were recruited." (2) RM-ANOVA: used in both the discrete error analysis ("A 5 x 9 repeated measures analysis of variance (RM-ANOVA)") and the serial error analysis ("A 5 ... x 4 ... RM-ANOVA"). (3) Bonferroni post-hoc: "Post-hoc comparisons using Bonferroni corrections" appears in both phases. (4) Discrete ≥9.6 mm: "no significant difference in error rate between the two largest targets (9.6 v. 11.5 mm). So while speed improves significantly as targets grow from 9.6 mm to 11.5 mm, error rate does not." (5) Serial ≥7.7 mm: Section 5.2.2 — "the keypad with the smallest key sizes (5.8 mm) caused significantly more errors ... than those with key sizes ≥ 9.6 mm. No differences between error rates for the other key sizes were significant" (i.e., 7.7/9.6/11.5/13.4 mm did not differ), matching the abstract's "≥ 7.7 mm in serial tasks." The supporting quote matches the abstract verbatim (lines 30-31). The framing "error rate stops improving above a threshold" is accurate (speed kept improving, error rate plateaued). The claim is a faithful report of the study's own finding with correct n and method — not an over-generalized universal claim — so there is nothing to contradict. Source quality (peer-reviewed primary paper) matches the claim's strength.

#### V219 `ae50e644` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In their study (e-commerce music/CD sites, open-ended browsing tasks across multiple sites), serious usability problems kept being discovered even after dozens of users — the cumulative discovery curve did not plateau near five participants; secondary reporting puts the first-five-users coverage at only ~35% of problems with new problems still appearing through all ~18 participants on a site. This empirically contradicts the '5 users = 85% of problems' rule for large, complex, open-task sites."

**Source:** https://www.semanticscholar.org/paper/Testing-web-sites:-five-users-is-nowhere-near-Spool-Schroeder/d06c1f2b8a77a6b2452f2792ef44cff977528226 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Jared Spool & Will Schroeder show that serious problems were still being discovered even after dozens of users (disagreeing with Virzi's but agreeing with Lewis's findings)."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported and accurately characterized on every specific. (1) MeasuringU (Sauro/Lewis, the leading authority on usability sample-size stats) describes the study as "open-ended tasks allowing users to freely browse up to four websites looking for unique CD's" — confirming both the music/CD-site detail and the open-ended-multi-site-browsing detail exactly. (2) Multiple independent sources confirm the figures: "identified 378 usability problems... first five participants uncovered just 35% of the problems, continuing to find new problems with all 18 of their participants." This matches the standard literature characterization (Jean Fox/BLS; Lewis's Springer paper "Beyond the five-user assumption"). (3) The "dozens of users / no plateau" phrasing is verbatim from the source: "serious problems were still being discovered even after dozens of users"; "dozens" is accurate for the aggregate (up to four sites at ~18 each). (4) Contradicting the "5 users = 85%" rule is the paper's explicit thesis (CHI '01 Extended Abstracts, pp. 285-286): "five users would not provide sufficient information to find 85%... differed sharply from rules-of-thumb derived from Virzi and Nielsen." (5) The "disagreeing with Virzi, agreeing with Lewis" supporting quote is confirmed by MeasuringU. Adversarial checks pass: the claim is correctly scoped to "large, complex, open-task sites," matching the exact mechanism (open-ended browsing) that authoritative sources cite to explain the divergence; no credible source disputes the numbers (even MeasuringU, which has an interest in defending the magic-5 rule, reports them honestly); source quality (peer-reviewed CHI proceedings plus multiple neutral expert secondary sources) matches the claim's strength; the 2001 date is fine since this is a historical empirical counterpoint, not a fast-moving tech claim. Only trivial slack is "music/CD" vs "music and videos," but MeasuringU literally says "CD's," so fully supported.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. MeasuringU (measuringu.com/five-history), which would have incentive to defend the "magic number 5," instead corroborates the study's figures and attributes the divergence to the open-ended task structure — consistent with, not contradicting, the claim's scoping. Lewis (Springer, link.springer.com/article/10.3758/BF03195514) independently supports increased-sample-size findings.

#### V220 `ae5379d4` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Fitts' law in HCI is modeled as a linear regression of movement time on index of difficulty, MT = a + b × ID, with the Shannon formulation ID = log₂(2D/W), so movement time increases with target distance D and decreases with target width W."

**Source:** https://www.yorku.ca/mack/ijhcs2004.html (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "MT = a + b × ID ... ID = log₂(2D/W)"

*Evidence:* The claim mis-states the Shannon formulation. It asserts "the Shannon formulation ID = log₂(2D/W)," but log₂(2D/W) is the ORIGINAL Fitts (1954) formulation, not Shannon. Every authoritative source agrees the taxonomy is: Fitts 1954 = log₂(2A/W); Welford = log₂(A/W + 0.5); Shannon (MacKenzie 1989/1992) = log₂(A/W + 1). Confirmed by (a) MacKenzie's 2018 Wiley handbook chapter (yorku.ca/mack/hhci2018.html), Eqs. 17.2/17.5/17.6, which explicitly assigns log₂(A/W+1) to "the Shannon formulation"; (b) MacKenzie 2013 note (yorku.ca/mack/ojas2013.html), Eq. 4 = log₂(A/W + 1.0), stating it "differs from Fitts' original formulation (which uses 2A/W)"; (c) the cited paper itself, Soukoreff & MacKenzie 2004 IJHCS, whose Equation 1 (the recommended Shannon formulation) is log₂(A/W + 1); (d) Wikipedia and MacKenzie's 1989 origin paper. The Shannon formulation was specifically introduced to REPLACE log₂(2A/W) because the latter can yield negative ID when A < W/2. The claim's "supporting quote" (ID = log₂(2D/W) labelled Shannon) is a WebFetch misread of the source — the source's Eq. 1 is log₂(A/W+1). The claim's other parts (MT = a + b × ID; MT increases with distance D, decreases with width W) are correct, but the central named-formula assertion is factually reversed/wrong and contradicted by the primary source it cites.
*Counter-source:* https://www.yorku.ca/mack/hhci2018.html (MacKenzie 2018, Eqs. 17.2/17.5/17.6) and Soukoreff & MacKenzie 2004 IJHCS Eq. 1 (https://www.yorku.ca/mack/ijhcs2004.pdf): Shannon formulation = log₂(A/W + 1); log₂(2A/W) is the original Fitts 1954 formula.

#### V221 `ae7d02e2` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "On desktop, hidden navigation made task completion at least 39% slower; on mobile it was 15% slower than visible navigation."

**Source:** https://www.nngroup.com/articles/hamburger-menus/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "People were at least 39% slower when the navigation was hidden ... Mobile: 15% slower when the navigation was hidden"

*Evidence:* Claim verified against the primary NN/g source (nngroup.com/articles/hamburger-menus/). The article states verbatim: "On the desktop, people were at least 39% slower when the navigation was hidden (compared with the visible or combo conditions)" and "On mobile people were 15% slower when the navigation was hidden (compared with the combo condition)." Both figures (39% desktop, 15% mobile) and the "at least" qualifier match exactly. Source is NN/g empirical research: 179 participants, 6 websites, desktop + smartphone, run with WhatUsersDo; a separate methodology page exists (nngroup.com/articles/hidden-navigation-methodology/). No contradicting source found; NN/g's 2019 follow-ups refine but don't retract it. ONE imprecision: the claim says mobile was 15% slower "than visible navigation," but the source measures the mobile 15% against the COMBO condition, not fully-visible nav (visible mobile nav wasn't fully tested due to screen-space limits). The desktop "than visible" framing is correct (vs "visible or combo"); the mobile baseline label is slightly off. This is a refinement, not a refutation — the directional finding and both numbers are faithfully sourced. Minor caveat: study is from 2016 and NN/g notes slow menu use can reflect user preference, not just discoverability failure.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source; only imprecision is internal — mobile 15% baseline is the "combo" condition per the same NN/g article, not the "visible" condition as the claim's wording implies.

#### V222 `af382726` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Error rate stops improving above a threshold: there was no statistically significant reduction in error rate for target sizes >= 9.6 mm in discrete tasks and >= 7.7 mm in serial tasks (n=20, RM-ANOVA, Bonferroni-corrected post-hoc)."

**Source:** https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/research/wp-content/uploads/2006/01/parhi-mobileHCI06.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "there were no significant differences in error rate between target sizes ≥ 9.6 mm in discrete tasks and targets ≥ 7.7 mm in serial tasks"

*Evidence:* Verified directly against the primary PDF (Parhi, Karlson & Bederson, MobileHCI 2006). All claim elements confirmed verbatim: n=20 ("Twenty participants (17 male, 3 female) were recruited"); RM-ANOVA used for both discrete and serial percent-error analyses; "Post-hoc comparisons using Bonferroni corrections." Discrete threshold: "there was no significant difference in error rate between the two largest targets (9.6 v. 11.5 mm). So while speed improves significantly as targets grow from 9.6 mm to 11.5 mm, error rate does not." Serial threshold confirmed in both the discussion ("error rates in serial tasks did not decline significantly with key sizes >= 7.7 mm") and the conclusion ("the error rates could not be discriminated statistically with target sizes >= 9.6 mm in discrete tasks and key sizes >= 7.7 mm in serial tasks"). The supporting quote is verbatim from the abstract. The 9.6 mm/7.7 mm thresholds are the authors' own stated conclusions, not an overreach; "no reduction" is a faithful paraphrase of "no significant difference." Primary peer-reviewed source matches claim strength.

#### V223 `af43887b` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "F-pattern scanning is a symptom of weak visual hierarchy rather than a target to design for; adding strong cues (prominent headings, bold key phrases, bullets, front-loaded keywords, trimmed content) redirects the eye away from the inefficient F."

**Source:** https://www.nngroup.com/articles/f-shaped-pattern-reading-web-content/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Users may skip important content simply because it appears on the right side of the page."

*Evidence:* The claim faithfully represents the primary NN/g source ("F-Shaped Pattern of Reading on the Web: Misunderstood, But Still Relevant"). Verified verbatim from the article: (1) F-pattern is "the default pattern when there are no strong cues to attract the eyes towards meaningful information" — directly supports "symptom of weak visual hierarchy." (2) "Good web formatting reduces the impact of F-scanning. If your pages have big chunks of unformatted text, people will scan it in an F-shape" — supports the redirect-via-formatting claim. (3) The supporting quote is accurate: "The F-shaped scanning pattern is bad for users and businesses: it means that users may skip important content simply because it appears on the right side of the page." (4) The article's own recommendations match the claim's listed cues exactly: headings, bolding important words, bullets, key info in opening paragraphs, starting headings with information-bearing words, visual grouping ("Prioritize and format text to direct users to what you want them to see").

Source quality matches claim strength: NN/g is the originator of the F-pattern finding (2006 eye-tracking, updated/re-validated incl. mobile), the single most authoritative practitioner source for this exact topic — appropriate for a [CONVENTION/best-practice backed by eye-tracking] tag, which is what the research question requests. Not dressed up as hard proven science.

Skeptical cross-check found NO contradicting source. The broader consensus reinforces it: Vitaly Friedman/Smashing ("F-pattern is harmful"), and multiple secondary sources independently state "treat F-pattern as a failure state, not a design target." Article is current and still cited as relevant in 2026.

Minor nuance (not refuting): the paraphrase "redirects the eye away" is marginally stronger than NN/g's "reduces the impact," but it is a fair restatement given NN/g documents alternative scan patterns (e.g. layer-cake) produced by good headings. This is interpretive best-practice, not a controlled causal proof — but the claim does not overstate it as such.

#### V224 `af7b77a9` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Eyetracking research identifies four distinct text-scanning patterns, ranked worst-to-best for effectiveness: F-pattern, spotted, layer-cake, and commitment."

**Source:** https://www.nngroup.com/articles/text-scanning-patterns-eyetracking/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "listed below in increasing order, worst to best, of effectiveness"

*Evidence:* The cited NN/g article (Pernice, "Text Scanning Patterns: Eyetracking Evidence") supports the claim almost verbatim. Two independent WebFetch reads of the source returned: (a) the introductory framing "In this article, I focus on the 4 patterns that people use to scan text on the web" and (b) the ranking sentence "the 4 patterns that people use to scan text on the web (listed below in increasing order, worst to best, of effectiveness)," with the list being exactly F-Pattern (worst), Spotted, Layer-Cake, Commitment (best). This matches the claim's count (four), the worst-to-best ordering, AND the exact pattern names. The supporting quote in the claim is genuine and load-bearing.

Source quality matches claim strength: NN/g (Pernice/Nielsen) is the field's authoritative eyetracking lab and is explicitly named as an acceptable authoritative source in the research brief. This is a descriptive eyetracking finding (~2019), in a stable, well-replicated area — not outdated, not marketing, not cherry-picked.

One caveat worth flagging (does NOT refute): NN/g's broader eyetracking corpus names SIX scanning behaviors — adding "marking" (eyes fixed while scrolling) and "bypassing" (skipping repeated leading words in lists). But those two are physical eye-movement behaviors, not effectiveness-ranked reading strategies, and this specific cited article deliberately scopes itself to "the 4 patterns... worst to best, of effectiveness." So excluding marking/bypassing from an effectiveness ranking is correct, not an error. The claim is accurate as stated and correctly attributed; a downstream skill should just avoid implying "four" is NN/g's total taxonomy. Also note "worst to best of effectiveness" is NN/g's qualitative judgment, not a quantified effect size — the claim does not overstate it as measured science.
*Counter-source:* NN/g's fuller corpus (e.g. layer-cake-pattern-scanning article and the "How People Read on the Web" eyetracking report) names six behaviors by adding "marking" and "bypassing" patterns — but those are physical scanning behaviors, not part of the effectiveness ranking, so they do not contradict the scoped four-pattern claim.

#### V225 `af889142` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "WCAG 2.2 Success Criterion 2.5.8 (Level AA) sets the minimum pointer-target size at 24 by 24 CSS pixels."

**Source:** https://www.w3.org/WAI/WCAG22/Understanding/target-size-minimum.html (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The size of the target for pointer inputs is at least 24 by 24 CSS pixels, except when:"

*Evidence:* Primary source (W3C WAI Understanding SC 2.5.8) confirms all four elements of the claim verbatim: Criterion Number = "SC 2.5.8 Target Size (Minimum)"; Conformance Level = "AA"; minimum size = "at least 24 by 24 CSS pixels". WCAG 2.2 is the current W3C Recommendation (published Oct 5, 2023), so the claim is not outdated. Multiple independent corroborating sources (Appt.org, TheWCAG, w3c/wcag GitHub issue #1894) all label it "Success Criterion 2.5.8 Target Size (Minimum) (Level AA)" with the 24×24 CSS pixel threshold. No credible source disputes it. The supporting quote ("at least 24 by 24 CSS pixels, except when:") directly and fully supports the claim with no overreach. Minor nuance (not a refutation): the criterion also offers a spacing-based alternative for undersized targets (a 24px-diameter circle centered on each must not intersect adjacent targets), so it is a minimum-size-OR-spacing rule, but the headline 24×24 figure is accurate as stated.

#### `a541a410` (Aesthetic preference — why people intuitively like a UI)
```json
{
  "summary": "People like an interface without knowing why mainly because of PROCESSING FLUENCY: when a layout is easy for the brain to process — clear, high-contrast, conventional/prototypical, visually simple — that ease registers as a genuine positive feeling (measurable physiologically as smile-muscle activity) which gets misread as \"I like this.\" This is the best-supported answer [STRONG]: a single fluency mechanism parsimoniously unifies the preference for familiarity, symmetry, prototypicality, and figure-ground contrast; it operates below awareness (an unnoticed 25 ms prime shifts beauty ratings); and it is eliminated when the good feeling is attributed to an external source (e.g., background music) — direct proof the feeling is the cause of liking, not a byproduct. First impressions of appeal form pre-consciously in 17-50 ms, are highly stable across viewings (r≈.97), and are best predicted by LOW visual complexity + HIGH prototypicality, with complexity mattering more than colorfulness; for websites simpler is monotonically better, so Berlyne's inverted-U does NOT apply to web UI. The MAYA balance (\"most advanced yet acceptable\") is empirically grounded — typicality and novelty jointly and equally predict preference while suppressing each other — though that evidence is on physical products, not web specifically. Easy-to-process pictures produced greater zygomaticus major (smile-muscle) activity AND self-reported positive affect. Also explicitly ties fluency to the mere-exposure effect (bridges into theme 2)."
```

#### `a98413db` (Practical interface — navigation, information architecture, usability, layout)

```json
{
  "results": [
    {
      "url": "https://scispace.com/pdf/a-mathematical-model-of-the-finding-of-usability-problems-nnnq0clxe1.pdf",
      "title": "Nielsen & Landauer (1993) — A Mathematical Model of the Finding of Usability Problems (CHI '93)",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The PRIMARY SOURCE for the famous problem-detection curve. Derives Found(n) = 1 - (1-p)^n from a Poisson process across 11 studies (5 user tests + 6 heuristic evaluations); average detection probability p = .33 (range .16-.60). This is exactly where 'five users find ~85% of problems' comes from — the [STRONG] empirical/mathematical basis, and the document the skill should cite rather than the popularized NN/g blog restatement. Shows cost-benefit optimum near 3-5 evaluators, NOT a universal '5 is enough' law."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://concreta.com.uy/wp-content/uploads/nielsenheuristicsCHI.pdf",
      "title": "Nielsen & Molich (1990) — Heuristic Evaluation of User Interfaces (CHI '90), original PDF",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The origin paper for heuristic evaluation and the heuristic set (later refined to the canonical 10). Crucial empirical caveat for the skill: across four experiments INDIVIDUAL evaluators found only 20-51% of usability problems — i.e., the heuristics are expert-judgment aids, not validated diagnostic rules. Detection only becomes reliable when 3-5 evaluators' findings are AGGREGATED. Directly answers 'empirical backing vs expert opinion.'"
    },
    {
      "url": "https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/BF03195514",
      "title": "Faulkner (2003) — Beyond the Five-User Assumption: Benefits of Increased Sample Sizes in Usability Testing (Behavior Research Methods)",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The decisive empirical contestation of '5 users.' Tested 60 participants, then drew 100 random samples of 5: average detection 85% but range 55-99%. At n=10 the worst sample rose to 80%, at n=20 to 95%. Quantifies the VARIANCE that the Poisson average hides — the [MYTH]-tempering effect-size data the skill needs to state '5 users is an average, not a guarantee.'"
    },
    {
      "url": "https://www.semanticscholar.org/paper/Testing-web-sites:-five-users-is-nowhere-near-Spool-Schroeder/d06c1f2b8a77a6b2452f2792ef44cff977528226",
      "title": "Spool & Schroeder (2001) — Testing Web Sites: Five Users Is Nowhere Near Enough (CHI EA)",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The headline [MYTH] challenge. On large e-commerce sites, 5 users found only a small fraction of problems because p (per-user detection) was far below the .30-.40 Nielsen assumed for simple interfaces. Establishes that the 5-user rule depends on a low-complexity, high-p assumption that breaks on real content-rich websites/dashboards — the exact context this practical skill targets."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/1125021.1125117",
      "title": "Nielsen (1992) — Reliability of Severity Estimates for Usability Problems Found by Heuristic Evaluation (CHI '92 posters)",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "Directly addresses how the 0-4 severity scale (0 = not a problem, 1 cosmetic, 2 minor, 3 major, 4 catastrophe) is validated. Finding: a SINGLE evaluator's severity rating is unreliable; you need to average roughly 3+ independent ratings to get a usable severity estimate. The empirical caveat the skill must attach whenever it recommends severity ranking."
    },
```

#### `ab979181` (Practical interface — navigation, information architecture, usability, layout)
```json
{
  "summary": "The confirmed evidence strongly grounds four of the six target topics — navigation/IA, usability-inspection methods, Fitts's Law/target sizing, and visual attention/eye-tracking — with primary controlled studies plus one normative standard (WCAG 2.2). It leaves topics 5 (forms, feedback, response-time thresholds, interaction states) and 6 (grids, alignment, 8pt spacing, line length, responsive breakpoints) essentially unevidenced; those must be sourced separately as [CONVENTION]. The strongest [STRONG] results: hidden/hamburger navigation cuts content discoverability >20% and slows desktop tasks at least 39% vs visible/combo nav (NN/g controlled test); deeper menu hierarchies measurably raise reaction time, errors, and perceived complexity (Jacko & Salvendy 1996); heuristic evaluation needs 3-5 evaluators because one expert finds only 20-51% of problems while ~5 find about two-thirds (Nielsen & Molich 1990); touch targets need ~9-10mm empirically (Parhi 2006) and WCAG 2.2 SC 2.5.8 mandates 24x24 CSS px (or a spacing exception); and Fitts's Law makes larger, closer targets faster, motivating cursor-adjacent and enlarged targets. One popular rule is flagged contested/[MYTH]: \"5 users finds ~85% of problems\" holds on average but its variance is large (Faulkner: random 5-user sets caught 55-99%) and it fails on large open-task sites (Spool & Schroeder: ~35% after five users), so five users is a floor for cheap iterative testing, not a guarantee.</summary>\n<parameter name=\"findings\">[\n  {\n    \"claim\": \"[STRONG] Hidden navigation (hamburger and other hidden menus) measurably hurts UX versus visible or combo navigation. In NN/g's controlled remote test (179 UK participants, 6 sites, desktop + mobile) it cut content discoverability by more than 20%, made desktop task completion at least 39% slower (15% slower on mobile vs combo), and was used far less when available (desktop: hidden 27% vs visible/combo 48-50%; mobile: hidden 57% vs combo 86%). Practical rule: prefer visible or combo navigation; reserve fully-hidden nav for genuinely space-constrained mobile.\",\n    \"confidence\": \"high\",\n    \"sources\": [\"https://www.nngroup.com/articles/hamburger-menus/\", \"https://www.nngroup.com/articles/hidden-navigation-methodology/\"],\n    \"evidence\": \"Three independently verified claims (votes 3-0 each) from the same primary NN/g study, all numbers confirmed verbatim against source. Aggregating ~5 evaluators finds about two-thirds of problems, because evaluators' problem sets are largely non-overlapping (the data does not form a cumulative Guttman scale, so the best evaluator does not subsume the rest). Nielsen & Molich recommend 3-5 evaluators; the detection curve rises steeply 1->5 and flattens (diminishing returns by ~10), so budget beyond five is better spent on other evaluation methods.\",\n    \"confidence\": \"high\",\n    \"sources\": [\"https://concreta.com.uy/wp-content/uploads/nielsenheuristicsCHI.pdf\"],\n    \"evidence\": \"Foundational CHI 1990 primary source (Nielsen & Molich, 3,500+ citations); three verified claims, votes 3-0, key phrases confirmed verbatim. Known critiques (the evaluator effect; Cockton/Woolrych) target the multi-evaluator aggregation model, not the single-evaluator detection rates, which are robust.\",\n    \"vote\": \"3-0 (claims 5,6,7)\"\n  },\n  {\n    \"claim\": \"[STRONG] Usability-problem SEVERITY ratings from a single evaluator are unreliable, but the mean severity rating from about four evaluators lands within half a rating point of 'true' severity 95% of the time. Practical rule: aggregate multiple raters for severity scoring; do not trust one expert's severity judgment.\",\n    \"confidence\": \"high\",\n    \"sources\": [\"https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/1125021.1125117\"],\n    \"evidence\": \"Primary citation = Nielsen (1992), 'Reliability of severity estimates...', SIGCHI 1992 short talk/poster; claim is a near-verbatim restatement of the abstract; vote 3-0. Caveat: it is a small 1992 poster and 'true severity' is operationally defined as the aggregate of many raters (mildly circular). Consistent with current NN/g guidance recommending 3-4 evaluators for reliable severity.\",\n    \"vote\": \"3-0 (claim 12)\"\n  },\n  {\n    \"claim\": \"[MYTH / contested] The popular 'five users finds ~85% of problems' discount-usability rule is unreliable in practice. Faulkner (2003), resampling a 60-real-user pool, found random 5-user sets ranged from catching 99% down to only 55% of problems (mean ~85%, which matches Nielsen — the issue is VARIANCE, not the average); raising the sample lifted the worst-case floor: 10 users -> 80% minimum, 20 users -> 95% minimum. Spool & Schroeder (2001, CHI) tested production e-commerce sites with open-ended browsing tasks and found the first five users caught only ~35% of problems, with serious new problems still surfacing past a dozen-plus users. This ~1 cm measured minimum is the evidence behind the platform conventions (Apple 44 pt, Material 48 dp).\",\n    \"confidence\": \"high\",\n    \"sources\": [\"https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/research/wp-content/uploads/2006/01/parhi-mobileHCI06.pdf\"],\n    \"evidence\": \"Two verified claims (votes 3-0) from the primary peer-reviewed MobileHCI 2006 study, values and thresholds confirmed verbatim. Undersized targets still conform via the SPACING exception: a 24 CSS-pixel-diameter circle centred on each target's bounding box must not intersect another target (or another undersized target's circle). So it is a minimum-size-OR-spacing rule; the headline number is 24x24 CSS px.\",\n    \"confidence\": \"high\",\n    \"sources\": [\"https://www.w3.org/WAI/WCAG22/Understanding/target-size-minimum.html\"],\n    \"evidence\": \"W3C normative Recommendation (WCAG 2.2 published Oct 2023, current); two verified claims (votes 3-0), criterion number, level, and the 24px figure plus the circle/bounding-box spacing exception all confirmed verbatim. Worked example: 20x20px targets pass with 4px spacing, fail with 0px spacing. Note 24x24 (AA minimum) is distinct from the larger SC 2.5.5 Enhanced (AAA, 44x44).\",\n    \"vote\": \"3-0 (claims 15,16)\"\n  },\n  {\n    \"claim\": \"[STRONG relationship; CONVENTION for the prescriptions] Fitts's Law: pointer movement time falls as targets get larger and distances get shorter — among the most robust models in experimental psychology. Concrete layout implications (Soukoreff & MacKenzie 2004): place frequently-used targets near the current pointer position (e.g. context/pop-up menus that open at the cursor) and enlarge targets that require long movements; bigger targets are faster to hit and have lower error rates.\",\n    \"confidence\": \"high\",\n    \"sources\": [\"https://www.yorku.ca/mack/ijhcs2004.html\"],\n    \"evidence\": \"Verified claim (vote 3-0), quote verbatim from Soukoreff & MacKenzie (2004, IJHCS) by the leading HCI authority on Fitts's Law; corroborated by NN/g ('bigger targets are faster, error rates go down as size increases'). TRANSPARENCY: a separately-submitted claim stating the exact algebraic form MT = a + b*log2(2D/W) was REFUTED 0-3 (failed verification against this source); only the qualitative relationship and layout implications survived. Screen edges/corners as 'infinite-width' targets was in the topic brief but is NOT among the confirmed claims.\",\n    \"vote\": \"3-0 (claim 17); related formula claim refuted 0-3\"\n  },\n  {\n    \"claim\": \"[Descriptive eye-tracking, practitioner-grade] The F-shaped scan (two horizontal sweeps near the top, then a vertical scan down the left edge) is confirmed by NN/g eye-tracking on both desktop and mobile, and is the DEFAULT pattern when text is unformatted and users scan efficiently with low commitment. In left-to-right languages, content toward the top and left is read more than content toward the right and bottom.\",\n    \"confidence\": \"high\",\n    \"sources\": [\"https://www.nngroup.com/articles/f-shaped-pattern-reading-web-content/\", \"https://www.nngroup.com/articles/text-scanning-patterns-eyetracking/\"],\n    \"evidence\": \"Two verified claims (votes 3-0), quotes verbatim from NN/g (originator of F-pattern research, 2006, reaffirmed 2017/2019). The claim is correctly CONDITIONED (default for unformatted text + efficiency-seeking + low commitment), so it does not contradict EyeQuant's reanalysis (99 sites, 46 users) which disputes only the UNIVERSAL/unconditioned F-shape on well-formatted production pages. LTR-specific (mirrors for RTL). Practitioner research, not peer-reviewed controls; brief explicitly accepts NN/g for descriptive eye-tracking.\",\n    \"vote\": \"3-0 (claims 18,23)\"\n  },\n  {\n    \"claim\": \"[Descriptive eye-tracking + grounded best practice] Position biases attention: earlier lines on a page and the leftmost words of each line draw disproportionately more fixations, so content placed lower or further right is more likely to be skipped. Actionable: front-load the information-bearing words in headings, subheadings, and links (users often read only the first ~2 words / ~11 characters), and avoid generic link text like 'click here' / 'more'.\",\n    \"confidence\": \"high\",\n    \"sources\": [\"https://www.nngroup.com/articles/f-shaped-pattern-reading-web-content/\"],\n    \"evidence\": \"Two verified claims (votes 3-0); both the descriptive finding ('first few words on the left of each line receive more fixations') and the front-loading prescription are stated by NN/g itself, corroborated by NN/g's 'First 2 Words' study. Correctly hedged as a tendency, not a deterministic law; LTR-specific. The descriptive half is eye-tracking-backed [STRONG-leaning]; the front-load prescription is established best practice grounded in that finding.\",\n    \"vote\": \"3-0 (claims 19,20)\"\n  },\n  {\n    \"claim\": \"[CONVENTION backed by eye-tracking] Treat the F-pattern as a SYMPTOM of weak visual hierarchy, not a layout to design for — users skip important content simply because it sits on the right. Strong cues (prominent headings/subheads, bolded key phrases, bullet lists, front-loaded keywords, trimmed copy, visual grouping) redirect the eye to more effective scan patterns. NN/g ranks four text-scanning patterns worst-to-best for effectiveness: F-pattern (worst), spotted, layer-cake, commitment (best).\",\n    \"confidence\": \"high\",\n    \"sources\": [\"https://www.nngroup.com/articles/f-shaped-pattern-reading-web-content/\", \"https://www.nngroup.com/articles/text-scanning-patterns-eyetracking/\"],\n    \"evidence\": \"Two verified claims (votes 3-0); the 'failure state' framing, the list of remedy cues, and the exact four-pattern worst-to-best ranking are all verbatim from NN/g and echoed by broad practitioner consensus (Smashing/Friedman: 'F-pattern is harmful'). Note: NN/g's wider corpus names six scanning BEHAVIORS (adding 'marking' and 'bypassing'), but those are physical eye-movement behaviors, not effectiveness-ranked strategies — so 'four' is correct for this effectiveness ranking, not NN/g's full taxonomy. 'Worst-to-best' is NN/g's qualitative judgment, not a quantified effect size.\",\n    \"vote\": \"3-0 (claims 21,22)\"\n  }\n]",
```

```json
      "url": "https://www.nngroup.com/articles/hamburger-menus/",
      "title": "Hamburger Menus and Hidden Navigation Hurt UX Metrics — NN/G",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "Quantitative study (179 users, 6 sites). Hidden navigation cut discoverability nearly in half: visible nav was found/used 1.5x as much as hamburgers on mobile, content discoverability dropped >20% with hidden nav, and hiding nav added ~2.5s to task time. The hardest empirical anchor for the mobile-nav and visible-vs-hidden parts of Topic 1. Pair with NN/g 'Beyond the Hamburger' (mobile + desktop) for what makes nav discoverable."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://www.nngroup.com/articles/breadcrumbs/",
      "title": "Breadcrumbs: 11 Design Guidelines for Desktop and Mobile — NN/G",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "Authoritative [CONVENTION] source for breadcrumb placement/format, backed by controlled evidence (cited studies found breadcrumb bars produced more efficient navigation and higher satisfaction). Key actionable rules: breadcrumbs supplement — never replace — global (top) and local (left-rail) nav; most valuable on deep, multi-layer sites; reduce reliance on the browser Back button. NN/g has recommended them since 1995 with 'many benefits, no downsides for secondary nav.'"
    },
    {
      "url": "https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.2466/pms.1996.82.3c.1187",
      "title": "Hierarchical Menu Design: Breadth, Depth, and Task Complexity — Jacko & Salvendy (1996)",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "Controlled [STRONG] study on the breadth-vs-depth question. Consistent finding across this literature (depth 1–6 levels, breadth 2–64 items): moderately broad, shallow structures beat narrow, deep ones for speed and search efficiency; perceived complexity rises significantly with depth; both extremes hurt learnability. Result replicates across populations (later confirmed even for blind screen-reader users), giving the IA breadth-vs-depth claim real empirical grounding."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://www.nngroup.com/articles/mega-menus-work-well/",
      "title": "Mega Menus Work Well for Site Navigation — NN/G",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "User-testing basis for the mega-menu recommendation: large/complex sites where standard dropdowns hide most options. Mega-menus show everything at a glance (recognition over recall) and surface secondary/tertiary pages, sharply improving discoverability of otherwise hard-to-find content. Direct support for the mega-menu and search-vs-browse sub-topics of Topic 1."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://www.nngroup.com/articles/navigation-you-are-here/",
      "title": "Navigation: You Are Here — NN/G (with Local Navigation as Wayfinding Aid)",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "Core wayfinding/active-state guidance ('where am I, where can I go, how do I get back'). Desktop local nav sits either horizontally below global nav or on the left side, forming an inverted-L; the selected item gains prominence via highlighted color and/or spatial offset (use both for accessibility). Local nav acts as a 'You are here' orientation cue. Best-practice [CONVENTION] backing for global-nav placement and active-state wayfinding."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://www.nngroup.com/articles/search-not-enough/",
      "title": "Search Is Not Enough: Synergy Between Navigation and Search — NN/G",
      "relevance": "medium",
      "snippet": "Search-vs-browse evidence: most users browse first (McGovern 2010 reported ~70% relied on navigation over search; only ~15% go straight to search), because clicking a visible link is easier and faster than composing a query, and users carry a corrupted Google-style search mental model into site search. Conclusion: search and navigation are complementary, not substitutes — navigation still 'reigns supreme' for setting expectations and immediate availability."
```
